LITERARY NOTES.
Writers publishing a book must needs be
far more daring than the aboriginal who
takes stand behind a duodecimo heelaman'
and dares the flight of stones sent whizzing
his lithe-armed tribesmen. Nobody
bothers to secure a sinless conscience before
casting critical pebbles. And since, once
in print, your defensive words are fixtures,
and bright eye and nimble wrist no longer
avail, there lie open to the assailant many
tender parts which the stiffest and most
rheumatic King Billy will defend with a
hardly perceptible flexure of the shield-arm.
Here, for example, is a bit of blue-metal
which “ The Banjo ” cannot dodge :
Dear Bulletin,— l am a man with a grievance
—not against the Government this time, nor the
editor, but against your “Banjo.” For the last
few years I have sworn by “ The Banjo ”; now I
Bwear at him. Ever since I read “Clancy of the
Overflow” I have been longing to visit the Over-
flow and get acquainted with Clancy; and if he
had gone to Queensland droving I intended to
hunt out where he are. At some inconvenience I
lately paid a visit to the Overflow ; but what did
I find ? There was not a soul on the station who
knew Clancy—they had never heard of him, in
fact. Much disgusted, I questioned one of the
station-hands : “ Don’t you read your Bulletin ? ”
“ Yes.” “ Then did you never read of Clancy of
the Overflow?” “Oh, yes,” he said, “ I fancy I
do remember a bit of potery about him.” Yah !
Then, as regards Dandaloo, which “ The Banjo ”
has immortalised, why, it is quite a common oc-
currence for Dandaloo drunks to get “bushed”
whilst travelling from one Dandaloo pub. to an-
other. I have not been to the Snowy River, nor
do I intend to go—l have lost too many fond
illusions as it is—but I may remark incident-
ally that every man I have met who hails from that
place has been an unmitigated blatherskite. Those
Monaro riders must always stay at home—pro-
bably they are snowed in. There is a rush at
Ironbark —a good one, too; half-an-ounce to the
dish—but wild horses will not drag me there, for
if the man from Ironbark should also prove to be
a myth my small stock of faith in mankind would
entirely disappear. And lastly, dear Bulletin,
were you ever in Menindie ?—dear little Menindie,
noted for its burning sandhills and whisky—also
burning. If so, you will be as curious as lam to
know where they got the green barley that they
stuffed into Old Pardon, the son of Reprieve.
There never was anything green about Menindie,
but I know plenty of men who have at times
painted it red. Finally—but never mind.—Yours
disgustedly, Feed Bkadshaw.
* * * *
Hardly so well aimed is a London missile
which reached the outskirts of its mark by
mail before last. The Pall Mall Gazette,
indeed, in its lengthy notice of “The Man
from Snowy River,” has thrown more rose-
leaves than turfs. E.g. :
Mr. Paterson is, without a doubt, a very good
writer indeed of a kind of verse which is as legiti-
mate a department of the art as epigrams or vers
de societe. And he need not, we venture to think,
fear the inevitable comparison with Adam Lind-
say Gordon. If the present volume contains
nothing quite as good as “The Sick Stockrider ”
and “ How we Beat the Favorite,.” it is at any
rate free from those pale reflections of Swinburne,
and that weak sentimentalism that fill two-thirds
of Gordon’s collected writings. Mr. Paterson, too,
has the finer gift of humor.
And again :
In a more serious vein, in conveying an impres-
sion, of the vast Australian solitudes, Mr. Pater-
son is at his best. He often shows real poetical
power, as distinguished from the cleverness and
fun which characterise most of his verses. “ The
Travelling Post Office” is one of these vivid
picture-pieces., more successful, we think, than the
actual narratives in verse, the genuine ballads,
though some of these are very good.
• • • •
Then by-and-by, at the tail of the article,
whiz ! comes the clod :
It is a pity that a writer who can make verse
of this quality, and who so often gives proof of
considerable technical skill, should occasionally
annoy his more critical readers by introducing
into his verses the unpardonable offence known
as the Cockney rhyme. Had it not been for this
we should have been even more enthusiastic about
Mr. Patterson ; but “loss ” does not, even in the
Antipodes, rhyme with “horse,” or “louder”
with “Judah” —no, not though every swagman
and stockrider on the Murrumbidgee, supported
by the entire editorial staff of the Sydney Bulle-
tin, should arise and testify to the contrary.
Alas, for British ignorance ! Did the writer
of this unenlightened commentary ever own
ahorse? Did he ever race one? “Loss”
doesn’t rhyme with “ horse,” quotha ! Why,
no such perfect rhyme was ever penned !
. The coincidence is absolute. As for com-
plaining of “louder” and “Judah”—good
Hebrews ! The Bulletin invites the Pall
Mall scribe to pass once—just once—through
the Valley of the Shadow of the Nose on any
Australian racecourse ; listen once—just
once—to Father Abraham, brother Isaac,
and son Jacob calling the odds ; and there-
after for ever squat on “The Banjo’s”
threshold in sackcloth and tote-tickets and
ashes and abasement.
HE * * *
A Cockney rhyme—what is it exactly ?
The dictionaries—even Dr. Murray’s big one
—don’t know, or won’t tell. But one guesses
it to be a rhyme favored by the hot young
writers whom the century-beginning let out
on the loose with a latch-key—Shelley, and
Keats, and Leigh Hunt, and Hazlitt, and all
that goodly company. At any rate, a bitter
Blackwood contributor dubbed their school
the Cockney school, and where else but in
the Cockney school should Cockney rhymes
be taught ?
* * * *
One knows, of course, by observation and
allusion, that a Cockney rhyme is an imper-
fect rhyme, with—should there not be ?
some straining of vowels involved, some mal-
treatment of consonants. The familiar
“dawn” and “morn,” “childhood” and
“wild wood,” are typical Cockney rhymes,
and they do grate on a critical ear. But
presumably assonance, with better-defined
consonants and vowels perfectly chiming
( mean and peer, robe and
“tome”), would not fall under the ban.
You cannot call the whole of Spanish
poetry Cockney. A Cockney rhyme is
not, then, a clean half-rhyme, or a bold
no-rhyme-at-all, but a hypocrite which
assumes the virtue it hath" not, a coin
which shines golden and rings base counter-
feit. Thus—if you put us on our oath—
“loss” and “horse,” “louder” and
lt T j i „ o ■. i j itmv
“ Judah are Cockney rhymes, and The
Banjo has sinned against Style.
* * * *
But suppose he had sinned against Thought
—were not his offence the graver ? Suppose,
» *■* “ <*
louder as a rhyme to the difficult “Judah,
he had sacrificed the good and characteristic
touch —
Says Jimmy : “ The children of Judah
Are out on the warpath to-day
and inserted something weaker, such as (on
the pen of the moment)
Says Jimmy : “ A concert at Chowder
Sings small beside Ikey Mo’s lay
would he have been the better verser ? It
seems not. This, of course, is neither ex-
cuse nor justification. It is the duty of the
perfect poet to put perfect thought into
perfect form, and a reasonable cavil may he
made at the imperfection of a poet who falls
short of the utmost possible on any line,
But, since humanity is imperfect, and poeti-
cal humanity plusquamimperfect, and a door
must be either open or shut, what is the
poet to do ? Is he to keep in the heat or
admit the draught ?—to prefer an imperfect
thought in perfect rhyme, or a perfect
thought in imperfect rhyme, or a mode-
rately-imperfect ditto in a moderately-
perfect ditto ?
* * * *
On the whole, it seems the thought carries
it. Yet there are amplifications and differen-
tiations and reservations. A rhyme which
would be execrable in “In Memoriam ”
might be permitted in a bush ballad—and
partly for this reason, that often bush ears
do not appreciate demi-semi-tones of prosody,
Many untrained or half-trained readers see
littleorno difference between a quarter-rhyme
and a whole one ; while they are keenly
conscious of the difference between a weak
or ill-expressed idea and a strong or well-
expressed idea. Why should a writer, who
can give them the ideas they value, cease to
compose, or compose only half as much,
because he cannot give them the form which
they value little or not at all ? In other
words, why cease supplying chops to people
who don’t want patd-de-foie-gras ?
When the reader has reached just here, he
will notice that j ust here the introduction of
some amoroua verses which “ S.-W.” has
written for B m, trait comes quite tnal-
,. .
apropos. They are introduced accordingly ;
This turns young Eros’s old theme dexter-
ously enough: face, eyes, mouth, breast, limbs
—she is all there. The male poet loves detail
in his amorous cuplets ; but why is the
female, at times quite as amorous, so much
more vague and general in her epithets ? It
isn’t in character. Woman, the natural
observer, who notes with microscopic eye the
minutest triviality of garb or wearer, must
see all the good and bad points of her lovers :
why doesn’t she dilate on them in print
as her lovers dilate on hers? Certainly
she does not: Sappho’s Phaon is only an
indistinct lump of loveliness, while Catullus
scrutinises Lesbia’s teeth with the anxiety of a
horse-dealer. “A mouth like a Gallic beagle,”
he says one lady had. Modem Sapphos are
content to describe their heroes as being
“beautiful as a young Greek god,” and
so on ; but they never specify anything
beautiful about him except his clothes, or his
nose, or some such irrelevancy. Even Mrs.
Raggles, of the U.S., whom a U.S. paper
praises for the taste implied in her remark
after visiting Europe : “ Well, I’ve seen the
Apollo Belvideer an’ I’ve seen Raggles, an’
gimme Raggles ! ” —even this exponent of
culture didn’t mention wherein lay Raggles’s
preeminence. Yet the most superficial study
of Swinburne, say, will show any average
woman what the average man prizes her for.
You bet that even Raggles, sitting spitting
at the stove in an Arizona saloon, and talking
heifer to the boys, would give them a re-
markably definite picture of the excellences
of his old girl. Now, since the literary and
poetic Male puts everything in writing, as it
were, why does the ditto Female play her
accepted instincts false in this one particular ?
Why doesn’t she retaliate, and compensate,
and elaborate ? The world is full up and
running over with the poetry of woman’s
eyes and limbs : let us hear the other side.
A WISH.
O, the face and the eyes and the mouth of her.
The breast where a man might laugh to die I
The fragrant limbs and the youth of her,
And the flush that oomes when it scarce knows
why !
Ah, float me far from the teen of life
Alone on a hill in her warm soft hold,
In the light of a glowing sunset, rife
With hues of purple and green and gold.
Lay my tired face on her shoulder clear,
My arm where the pride of her heart beat?
through ;
And open her eyes to my own eyes near—
Then the world and its glory be all to you !

BEFORE YOU
L . 33 A ?-2
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the'mohey in yoUr pocket, and take account
of. the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of-a-pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and -0,
who says that he is the man for your money;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-streek, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say. and to offer.
Please Pause i
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land T sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious*
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer —for you to plead ■ There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
HOW THE “FIRE-KING” CROSSED THE
SWAMP.
The flood was down in the Wilga Swamps, three
feet over the mud,
And the teamsters camped on the Wilga Range
and swore at the rising flood ; •
For one by one they had tried the trip, double and-
treble-teams,
And one after one each desert-ship had dropped to
her axle-beams ;
So they thonged their leaders and pulled them
round to the camp on the sandhill’s crown,
And swore by the bond of a.blood-red oath to
wait till the floods went down. ,
There were “side rail tubs” and “ table-tops,’
coaches and bullock-drays, .
Brown with the Barcoo Wonders, and Speed with
the dapple grays
Who pulled the front of his waggon out and left
the rest in the mud
At the Cuttaburra Crossing in'the grip of the
Ninety flood. ,
There was Burt with his sixteen bullpcks, and
never a bullock to shirk,
Who twice came over the Border Line with twelve-
ton-ten to Bourke;
There was Long Dick damning an agent’s eyes for
his ton of extra weight,
And Whistling Jim, for Cobb and Co., cursing that
mails were’ late ;
And one blasphemed at a broken chair and howled
for a blacksmith’s blood,
And most of them cursed their blasted luck, and
all of them cursed the flood.
The last of the baffled had struggled back and the
sun was low in the sky,
And the first of the stars was creeping out when
Dareaway Dan came by. i’ '
There’s never a teamster draws to Bourke but has
taken the help' of 'Dan ;'
There’s never a team on the Great North Road can
lift as the big roans can :
Broad - hipped beauties that nothing can stop,
■ leaders that swing to a cough ;
Eight blue-roans on the near-side yoked, and
eight red-roans on the off.
And Long Dick called from his pine-rail bunk :
“ Where are you bound so quick ? ”
And Dareaway Dan spoke low to the roans, and
aloud, “ To the Swagman’s, Dick.”
“There’s five good miles,” said the giant, “ lie to
the front of you, holding mud ;
If you never were stopped before, old man, you
are stopped by ,the Wilga flood ;. . n
The dark will be down in an hour or so, there isn't
the ghost of a moon,
So leave your nags in the station grass instead of
the long lagoon ! ”
But Dan stood, up to his leader’s head and fondled
the big brown nose :
. “ There’s many a mile in the roan team yet before
they are feed for the crows ; ,
Now listen, Dick-with-the-woman’s-heart, a word
to you and the rest :
I’ve sixteen horses collared and chained, the pick
of the whole wide West,
And I’ll cut their throats and leave them here to
rot if they haven’t the power
To carry me through to the gates of Hell —with
seventy bags of flour !
The light of the stars is light enough ; they have
nothing to do but plough ;
There’s never a swamp has held them yet, and a
swamp won’t stop them now.
They’re waiting for flour at the Swagman’s Bend ;
I’ll steer for the lifting light; .
There’s nothing to fear with a team like mine, and
—I camp in the bed to-night ! ”
So they stood aside and they watched them pass
to the glow of the sinking sun,
With straining muscles and tightened chains
sixteen pulling like one ;
With jingling harness aind. droning wheels and
- bare hoofs’rhythmic tramp, •' ' “
With creaking timbers and lurching load the
•“Fire King ” faced the f swamp ; •
She dipped her red' shafts low in the slush as a
. spoonbill .dips-lier beak, : r -I.’ . 1 .
The black mud clung to the wheels and fell m the
wash of the Wilga Creek ;
And the big roans fought for footing, and the
spreaders threshed like flails,
And the great wheels lifted the muddy spume to
the bend of the red float-rails ;
And they cheered him out to the westward with
the last of the failing light
And the splashing’hoofs'and the drivers voice
died softly away in the night;
But some of them prate of a shadowy form that
guided the leader’s reins,.
And some of them speak of a shod black horse
that pulled in the off-side chains—
How every time that he lifted his feet the waggon
would groan and swing,
And every time that he dropped his head you
could hear the tug-chains ring.
And Dan to the Swagman’s Bend came through ;
mud-spattered from foot to head,
And they couldn’t tell which of the roans were
blue and which of the roans were red.
Now this is the tale as I heard it told, and many
beiieve it true
When the teamsters say in their off-hand way
“ ’Twas the Devil that pulled him through.”
GLENROWAN.
- Some of the allegations made before a
Melb. Board which is investigating the telephone
muddle' are interesting. One of them is to the
effect that, when a deviation was made between
Richmond and S. Yarra, 12 posts on the old route
which were not wanted but were quite sound and
fit for future use, and worth £1 each, were sold for
4s. 6d. This was done apparently from sheer force
of habit. Then, according to the same witness,
they were cut down and stacked on a vacant allot-
ment, and some soft-footed person stole them in
the night, so that even the 4s. Gd. was never
recovered.

was told there were factory gins who slaved long

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MS. to distinctly unue, stand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, JULY 18, 1896.
Old Age Pensions.
The old-age pension scheme, which the
Seddon Ministry sprung, the other day, on
Maoriland House of Representatives, is the
very latest development of State Socialism
m these provinces. With this and the State
r ire Insurance scheme and, possibly, the
measure for the establishment of State pawn-
shops, on hand simultaneously, the M.L.
Cabinet certainly has material enough for a
long and riotous session. The fact that the
present is the last session of the existing
Parliament makes this lavishness all the
more remarkable, according to Australian
political standards. The policy of the old
Parkes school of politicians, at such a
time, would be to save up these measures as
a war-cry for the general election ; to stump
the country upon them, and blow the brass
trumpet of party fervour, and blare on the
big tiombone of advertisement, and make
them a means of working up a big majority
at the polls. The Parkes variety of states-
men believed in never doing anything if by
leaving it undone, it could be turned into a
valuable party war-whoop for future use. It
had few ideas—sometimes, in fact, it
had only one ; and it dared not use up its
inspiration too fast lest it should thereby
leave itself without a programme at the next
emergency. The Local Government Bill
for example, hung fire in New South Wales
for thirty years because every politician lived
more or less by professing that he was going
to carry it ; yet none of them really did so
because when once this stereotyped project
was used up no one knew of anything equally
good which he could profess to be going to
do next. The Seddon Ministry has aban-
doned all these old traditions, and is really
trying to do, inside of six years, as many
things as the average statesman lives upon
for thirty or more—not by doing them but
by generally intending to do them. Whether
this prodigality will leave the Government
stranded by-and-bye for want of any more
policy remains to be seen. At all events it
is a pleasant change from the old-time system
under which the country had to wait in-
definitely for reforms because its chronic
placemen had to put them away to live upon
in their old agq, The gradualness of a system
under which it was possible for everybody to
agree on the necessity of a measure) and then
doze over the details of it for thirty .years or
so, grew painfully oppressive.
* * * *
The Old Age Pension scheme is a measure
about the feasibility of which there can be no
serious room for argument. Past experi-
ence, in Germany and elsewhere) has shown,
despite sundry difficulties about matters of
detail, that the system itself is perfectly
workable. The Bill before the M.L. Legis-
lature provides that every citizen, aged 65
°r Upwards, who has been resident in the
province for the preceding 20 years or more,
shall be entitled to a life-pension of 10s. a
week upon application. The residence-quali-
fication is undoubtedly a hardship, but with-
out it Maoriland would become a splendid
place for the old men of all nations to swarm
into, and the scheme would break down by
its own weight. If the other six provinces
should by-and-bye adopt a similar idea, then
the qualification might safely be altered to 20
years’ residence in any part of Australasia,
and, with this limitation, each province could
take the old men and women as they
came, but until this consummation is arrived
at the restriction to old-standing local resi-
dents only is unavoidable. The necessary
funds are to be raised by some method of
taxation not yet definitely decided upon.
The nurden is not by any means so large
as it looks. According to the 1891 census,
the Maoriland inhabitants, male and female,
aged 65 and upwards numbered about.l4,ooo.
Assuming that 12,000 of these—a pretty hi'-di
estimate—were qualified by 20 years’ resi-
dence, the highest possible charge would be
£312,000 a year. But, as a very consider-
able portion of the old inhabitants are in
comfortable circumstances, it is probable
that the outlay, for a population of about
' 00,000 souls, would not exceed £200,000 or
£250,000 a year. On a like estimate, N.S. W.
could run a similar pension-system for, pro-
bably, £400,000 per annum ; Victoria, for
about £550,000 ; Queensland, for £100,000;
S.A., for £150,000 ; and Tasmania, where
the centenarians grow, for about £llO,OOO.
* * * *
This, however, would not be all, or nearly
ail, actual loss. With or without a pension
system the old and indigent inhabitant is a
fact that cannot be disguised. Sometimes
he has never had the means to save up money
to live upon in his last days. Sometimes he
r S means, but has failed to practise
the blessed virtue of economy. Very often
he has made provision with much hard thrift
and foresight, and some fraudulent bank or
exploded company or society, or some other
of the thousand possible commercial disasters,
has reduced him to indigence. There are so
many financial leakages through which the
savings of the masses drop through and
vanish that probably not much more than
half of those who laboriously economise to
provide for their old age ever gain
worth mentioning thereby. Then, if the
aged one has no property, and no relatives
who choose to support him or her as the case
may be, and is incapacitated for labor—and
the labor-market, even for the most ener-
getic person of 65 or upwards, is very flat
indeed—-he -or she must either live on the
community, or die upon it. The community
bears the expense, sometimes by grants
and personal subscriptions in support of
benevolent asylums ; sometimes per medium
of casual charity; sometimes in the cost of
having the ancient run in by a hired police-
man, and hiring a magistrate to send him to
an expensive gaol for vagrancy ; sometimes
by means of the hospital vote or otherwise.
au PP ort him it pays a policeman
to find his body, and a coroner and jury to
inquest him, and an undertaker to bury him.
In some way or other, however, the com-
munity pays for him, alive or dead.
* * * *
rn? 16 amoun t spent on charitable relief is
difficult to arrive at, but reckoning all
Government subsidies, and making a mode-
rate allowance for the great mass of un-
recorded private charity, it is probable that
this item costs Maoriland something like
£400,000 a year, and the other provinces in
a rather larger proportion. The amount ex-
pended on old age pensions would save at
least a part of this outlay, and the increased
burden on the community would probably
be imperceptible. The principal change
would be that the aged and indigent inhabi-
tant, who had contributed for many years to
the fund through the general taxation, could
apply for his pension as a matter of right
instead of asking assistance on all-fours as a
case of charity. The element of Bumble-ism
would be left out altogether. In place of
being a pauper, he would be a perfectly
independent citizen—as independent as the
one who goes to his insurance office to col-
lect Ins endowment policy when it falls due.
His children, if he had any, would not be the
children of a pauper. No officious charitable
person would have any right to inter-
rogate him about his soul, or invade his
home and insist on reading dismal tracts to
him, or to make rules and regulations for his
conduct, or to insist on him wearing any
livery. And if the system made
4UUU less paupers in a comparatively small
community it would'do a great deal towards
making that community vigorous and indepen-
dent, and one whose existence would be cal-
culated to make this world feel better.
* * * *
The project, as it stands, is undoubtedly
incomplete, though probably it is as complete
as can be expected for a beginning. It
makes no allowance for destitute persons
who Ai'e perffiafientiy incapacitated at a com-
paratively early age by accident or otherwise.
Old age, too, has no fixed point of commence-
ment ; some people are worn-out and senile
at 60, and to the decayed individual who is
trying to live long enough to qualify the
period from 60 to 65 is very protracted and
barren and hopeless. There is no possible
way, either, of providing for the patriarch
who has not put in the required 20 years
residence, save by the hap-hazard methods of
public and private charity ; until the pension
system becomes universal it is necessary to
draw a hard and fast line somewhere, else
the country with a pension system would
become burdened with the broken-down
grandparents and great-grandparents of all
nations. Still the scheme provides for the
most necessitous class of the community and
the one for which the country is most dis-
tinctly responsible. And if the provision is
not large it is at least large enough, in a
land of comparative cheapness, to keep
the recipient from absolute want, and
to save him from being a tragedy
while he remains alive and a newspaper
horror by the manner of his death.
Also, there is the possibility—assuming that
the measure becomes law—that the demands
on the pension-fund may prove less than is
expected, and that it may be found possible
to treat the patriarch rather more liberally
than is expected when he calls to collect his
annuity. In any case, the system will not
do evil by creating a useless class, for it deals
only with a class which is practically useless
for industrial purposes already. As the
average pensioner, too, is not likely to hoard
up much out of his 10s. a week, and as he
will not be an absentee, as the high official
pensioner often is, his dole will promptly go
back into circulation again, which will help
in some degree to reduce the burden—not
much of a burden at the worst. And Maori-
and will get some value for it, apart from all
financial considerations, in the load that will
be lifted oil its public conscience and the
extent to which it will feel better in its
dreams.
Some “Public Works.”
L'he best jokes of the public services of the
Australasian provinces are also the great scan-
dals. None but humorists of the brightest
description could achieve such a jest, for in-
stance, as getting Government to pay 4s. 6d.
and ss. 6d. for the excavation as solid rock
from a gigantic railway-cutting, when every
“ bushy ” within a radius of 40 miles from the
place knew that the country was a friable shale
which a child could extract with his toy shovel,
and the removal of which would be well paid
at Is. 3d. per cubic yard.
* * * *
Another joke of the same order occurred dur-
ing the construction of a great main line in
another province. A superior railway official,
of reputedly the highest engineering ability,
tunnelled a mass of the hardest rock, the
Government paying for the work at perhaps
the highest rate ever locally paid for tunnel
excavation. That was laugh the first in the
Joke. Laugh the second came in when the
same official pronounced the tunnel unsafe, and
induced the Ministry of the day to consent to
its being transformed into an open cutting,
which work, by the way, had it been undertaken
in the first instance, would have cost only two-
thirds as much as the tunnelling. Laugh number
three must have shaken the heavens, when the
whole of the open cutting was newly paid for
as though the mass of its cubic contents had
not already been removed, and paid for, at
tunnel rates ! That item of profit means a big
fortune in itself for the contractor.
* * * *
Fortunately for the country, jests upon that
scale cannot be indulged in nowadays, but still
the old class of jokist dies hard. In a small
way, he has his fling still. Towards the end of
the last grew,t building boom in a certain great
Australian city, but before symptoms of the col-
lapse were apparent, enormous importations of
marble mantelpieces were made" by several
down-South city firms. The boom fell in-
thousands of the mantelpieces remained ‘on
hand but there has scarcely been a railway-
porter’s hut, or any rookery of the humblest
kind, since erected by Government, that has
not been enriched by as many marble mantel-
pieces as could be in decency erected in the
structure. And nobody seemingly knows
whence all these marble mantelpieces sprang.
A school-inspector was once politely told to
mind his own business when he protested
against the fitting up of the quarters attached
to every tuppeny-ha’penny school-house with
elegant and valuable chimneypieces.
* * * *
Official Joe Millerisms of this sort are still
indulged in, even by theN.S.YV. Railway Com-
missioners. The green signal-lamp, for iu-
stance, 6° mdes away from the head-quarters
of the district, had tbe glass broken. It could
have been replaced by an intelligent official at
a local expense of Is. 6d. Power, however, to
carry out so common-sense a proceeding was
denied him by regulation, and what with the
visits of (1) an inspector to report on the
damage done, and the work required; (2) the
journey up of a workman to replace the glass ;
(3) his journey down the next day to bring his*
diamond, which he had forgotten the first time,
and finally another inspector’s trip to investi-
gate and report, the total cash expense to the
country amounted to between £3 and M. So
the joker who cultivates jocularity at the
national expense still survives.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Some Things that are Old.
VKBY fe'v things in this world are wholly new,
ihe Maori land Government, during recent
years, has been making history at a great rate
in the direction of State Socialism, and the
lory press has rained unutterable bosh for
many months about the recklessness of its
“experimental legislation.” And, as the
majority of the population knows practically
nothing itself, and takes its opinions ready-
made from the newspapers, it has been generally
accepted that the legislation is new, and that
no one knows where such reckless experiments
may lead to. Yet almost every one of these
experiments—to say nothing of many others
which are to come—is in full blast somewhere
or other, and was proved feasible and successful
many years ago—so long ago, in some cases,
that it began before the white man first in*
fested Australia. The State ownership of rail*
ways is a very old experiment which has been
tried over all the five continents with good re*
suits.' State life insurance is a British institu-
tion of a good many years standing, and is htil,
though on a diminutive scale, in connection With
the Imperial Post-office. The State Bank
idea is at least 200 years old, and ill the Old
World a dozen or more of such institutions eah
be found, The British Government tuns the
issue of paper-money in India, and the Canadian
Government does likewise in Canada, so that this
much-needed reform has sufficient British pre-
cedent—to say nothing of much other precedent
—to back it. The land-talc has existed in England
for centuries, and was once the principal source
of revenue, and it is by far the largest source
of revenue to-day in India. The purchase by
the Government of big estates to cut up in
small farms and sell on long terms, is a British
idea for the relief of Ireland, and the system
has been at work there for some years. It is
also an old German idea, and was largely
adopted in Prussia. The idea of a State Land
Bank is also old ; Norway has had such a bank
for 40 years past, and the Imperial Bank of
Russia does the same kind of business on an
immense scale. Also, some of the private banks
of Europe are compelled by law to invest a
certain part of their funds in this business at a
very low rate of interest. The State pawnshop
flourishes in Prance and Holland, and in the
latter country the three-ball business is a State
monopoly. The State Drink Monopoly, which is
obviously coming, has its counterpart in the State
Opium Monopoly of British India. The pro-
posed State Tobacco Monopoly is a public enter-
prise which has existed in France for 80 years ;
it also exists in other countries of Continental
Europe. The Old Age Pension system flourishes
in Germany and elsewhere. Except, perhaps,
the State Fire Insurance project, there is not
one of the “reckless Socialistic experiments”
which these provinces are now plunging into
that hasn’t been tried and proved feasible long
ago by slow-going, old-world nations, not on
Socialistic grounds but as matters of hard, com-
monplace finance. Most of these alleged “ex-
periments ” should command the respect of the
lory party merely by reason of their age, but
it is only the bad things that are old which
the genuine Tory loves. The good things that
are old, however old they be, seldom appeal to
him.

Parliamentary Salaries.
The proposal of solicitor Russell Hyphen
Jones, M.L.A., to reduce the salary of N.S. W.
Parliamentary representatives, in the name of
patriotism and disinterestedness, from £3OO to
£l5O a year, is one which should be squashed
hard by every democrat in the community.
The reason advanced for the reduction is mainly
the usual one—that payment was started to
enable the working-man to sit in Parliament
without loss to himself, and that £3 a week is
enough to cover all he loses through exchanging
his saw-mill or his bootmaking machine for
the Legislature. Also, another reason assigned
is the usual sneer that the working-men now in
Parliament couldn’t earn more than .£l5O a year
at boot-making or saw-milling, and if thev
don t care to accept that remuneration there are
plenty more bootmakers and saw'-millers who
will be glad to do so. This superficial
snort doesn’t even begin to express the
situation. The country, even when it sends
a bootmaker to Parliament, wants the
best bootmaker it can fiucl. It requires him to
go and live in the city for a great part of the
year, which, in many cases, means supporting
two establishments—not in the Social Gimlet
sense. It wants him to dress sufficiently well
to keep him from being an object of ridicule in
a top-hatted assembly; this isn’t absolutely
written in the contract, but it gets there all
the same. It recognises the fact that he is
expected to be a sort of charitable institution
for the relief of hungry and stone-broke consti-
tuents and that he has many calls on him which
he hadn’t in his bootmaking days. As a M. L.A.
he has -to be elected at least once every three
years, which is an expensive process; whereas a
mere bootmaker hasn’t to be elected, or to pay
for halls in which to address his constituents,
or to make progresses through his constituency.’
ruither, when lie has learned the business of
politics the country still reserves to itself the
right to take his occupation from him at any
moment; it asks him to give up a billet which
may be permanent for one which is wholly pre-
carious. Further, the argument that because a
man was a bootmaker before he became a poli-
tician he should only be paid bootmaker’s
wages is wholly illogical; on the same basis,
because Premier Reid was a civil servant
at a few pounds a week before he was a
Premier, therefore he should get only a few
pounds a week now. Also, if an M.L.A. should
only get £l5O a year because he couldn’t earn
more at shearing or some other occupation, it is
equally evident that solicitor Jones should con-
duct a big lawsuit for 255. because lie couldn’t
make more in the tinfe at shovelling mud
When solicitor Jones demands an all-round
reduction of Parliamentary salaries, he isn’t
necessarily so disinterested as he looks. He is
a resident of Sydney, so his own political posi-
tion involves none of the expenses incurred by
country members. To be M.L.A., regarded
as an advt. for his law-practice, should be worth
to him at least £6OO a year. By reducing Par-
liamentary salaries he reduces competition for
seats in the Assembly, and thereby makes the
billet, and the advt., and the £6OO a year or
whatever it is that the advt. is worth, so much
the more secure. Altogether it is just possible
that it would pay solicitor Jones and his kind
a great deal better to have no salaries at all
and so reduce competition to a minimum and
make the billet and the business it brings’ with


it practically permanent. Moreover, as Jones
undertook to give all his Parliamentary salary
to local charities —which probably helped his
election materially—he doesn’t lose a penny
even if the salary is reduced to nothing.
The Federal Capital.
Melbourne Table Talk remarks in an aston-
ished tone of voice as follows :
J. G. Drake, of Queensland, seriously takes up The
Bulletin idea of an inland Federal State, after the manner
of the district of Columbia in the United States, for the
Federal capital. He suggests 50,000 square miles at the
point where the borders of New South Wales, South Aus-
tralia, and Queensland now meet.
The Melbourne paper is seemingly surprised
that Drake didn’t take the matter up in a
more airy and frivolous spirit. Yet it certainly
is a question to be taken up seriously, if there
is any serious question in politics just now.
There are at least five good reasons why none
of the existing capitals should be chosen for the
Federal city. (1) Interprovincial jealousies
stand in the way ; it is impossible to select the
capital of one province without making the
others cool off greatly on the question of unity.
(2) None of the existing capitals is reasonably
central. (3) They are all within convenient
reach of invasion ; a sudden and well-planned
raid by an enemy might easily make the Federal
Government and . the military head-quarters
run for the bush, and rush the Federal Treasury,
and capture the Federal arsenals, and scare the
whole show into the mountains, just when it
was most needed. (4) They are all on the
coast, where the population is too much con-
centrated already. (5) There are difficulties
and anomalies about having the Federal and
local government in one city. Yet neither
New South Wales nor Victoria would be willing
to constitute its capital and the immediate sur-
roundings a Federal district, and thereby lose
half its population, and become a mere second
or third-rate province. And there are at least
eight good reasons for building the Federal
capital in the farthest interior—say in
the M'Donnell ranges of South Australia,
which is probably the most promising
site yet suggested. (1) It is almost the
exact centre of Australia, and when the rail-
way systems are completed this site would be
more accessible from all parts of the country
than any other. (2) It would place the Federal
Government, treasury, arsenals, ammunition
factories, military department, and probably
the head-quarters of the banks, which would,
most likely, move there in course of time,
beyond the reach of invasion and sudden loot.
(3) It would offer a chance for the biggest and
grandest experiment in Land Nationalisation
attempted in modern times. The Federal
Government could acquire that stretch of
wilderness at its prairie value, which is almost
nil, and be the ground landlord of the Federal
city and its surroundings for fiver, and realise
a handsome income thereby. (4) It would
draw population to the interior where it is
most wanted; not to the coast where it is
wanted least. No other scheme would “ open
up the interior,” as the threadbare phrase goes,
half so quickly or effectively. (5) The building
of the new city would afford a vast amount of
employment, while the land-values it would
create would do a great deal towards paying
the cost. (6) The gathering of population on
ihe new site would make it possible to finish
the S.A. transcontinental railway as a paying
spec, and it would also make it profitable and
feasible to extend the Central Queensland line
westward to the new capital, which would mean
more opening up of the interior and more em-
ployment. (The only reasons why Adelaide
and Port Darwin and Rockhampton don’t lift
up their voices unanimously in favor of the
project is that they haven’t fully realised
the advantages they would individually
derive from it). (7) It would definitely
secure the Northern Territory as part of
Australia (the only excuse for not handing it over
being that this country has so far failed to settle
it with population and “ open it up ”), whereas
the Territory is now British property, and
liable, if the British Government pleases, to be
jobbed away to black-labor syndicates. In
other words, this scheme would help to make a
United Australia, whereas now there is an ugly
weak spot in the scheme of unity. (8) The
proposed site is a plateau some 2000 ft. high,
with a beautiful climate, and is the best-watered
part of the far interior. And for these reasons
all single-taxers, latid-nationalisers, land and
other boomers, jerry-builders, patriots, pick-
and-shovel men, railway-contractors, and un-
employed, also all Hergott Springs, Oodnadatta
and Boulia, and their joint and several dogs,
should howl for this project; and all provin-
cialists who don’t want the rival capital to
become the seat of Government should do like-
wise ; and Adelaide, Port Darwin, and Rock-
hampton should howl loudest of all, for they
would be the nearest and most accessible ports,
and the trade of the Federal capital should
make them boom exceedingly. The Bulletin
has said all this before, but it is necessary to
keep saying the same thing over and over again
with dreadful iteration to make it soak into the
public comprehension.
Abusing tbe Law.
Once again the hopeless case of Widow Kil-
patrick v. Huddart Parker and Co. has come
into the Melb. Law Courts, and this time a
Judge was good enough to voice public opinion
on the matter. Widow K. —it is a time-worn
story—sued H.P. and Co. for the loss of her
husband in the steamship Alert. A jury gave
her <£6oo damages, whereupon defendants pro-
cured a new trial. This resulted in a verdict
of <£79l, and defendants appealed for a third
trial on certain grounds, which were referred,
us before, to the Full Court. Five Judges con-
stituted the Full Court when the second appeal
came along, and by a majority of 3 to 2 their
Honors upheld the second trial. Also, Justice
Williams made the following pertinent re-
marks :
“ There had already been two trials of this case, both of
which resulted in a verdict for plaintiff, and if the Full
Court on the first occasion had taken the same view of
the certificate's as the majority of the Court did now, no
new trial would have been granted. He might point his
observation by a short reference to the recent case of
Edwards v. the Metropolitan Board of Works. That case
was tried four times. On the first trial the jury dis-
agreed ; on the other three each jury gave plaintiff a ver-
diet with damages, which were necessarily small.
Defendants tried to get a fifth trial, hut the Court refused
the application, and plaintiff retained a verdict. He
(Justice Williams) might safely assume that after four
such trials the plaintiff who, like the present plaintiff,
was of small means, would not improbably find himself
ruined by his long struggle to secure what the Full Court
had at length held to be his due.”
Widow Kilpatrick, it goes without say-
ing, is now quite at the mercy of her
lawyers. Her costs, no doubt, far ex-
ceed the costs allowed to her, and her
damages, if she ever gets them, will not
suffice to pay the legal expenses charge-
able to her account. Meantime, Huddart
Parker and Co. have obtained leave to appeal
to the Privy Council, and the case resumes
business for an indefinite period. The
injustice to widow Kilpatrick lies in the
fact that the law has been purposely made
for lawyers and not for the public. There
is little or no finality about its rulings, this
side of the Privy Council, provided that
either litigant has the wealth and inclina-
tion to purchase appeals. Even a Full Court
is a ghastly fraud which may be set aside
by a Fuller Court. The great purpose of the
law is to create costs, and it misses no reason-
able chance of gaining its mercenary end. The
moral of the Kilpatrick case, and the Edwards
case aforesaid, was shamelessly admitted by a
barrister in a Melbourne suburban police-
court on the same day that Judge
Williams spoke his mind. The barrister in
question was summoned for payment of a
grocery-bill, and the grocer obtained an order
for the amount and costs, in weekly instal-
ments. Did the defeated one accept the law
served out to him ? No ;he glared defiantly at
the Bench, and said : “I shall appeal against
the order, and it will cost the defendants more
than the debt is worth.” There we have a
frank confession that the Law in the hands of
one of its professors is a bludgeon for smiting
the grocer who demands justice.
Doggerel - Drunken.
Doesn’t Mr. Jawbone-NEiLD’s revived trouble
with his critics arise from the not-sufficiently-
noted fact that the literary calling—dealing
fundamentally with the expression of ideas—
differs from every other in that it can be fol-
lowed, in some sort, by almost anybody who
can read and write ? The workers in all sorts
of other vocations who inundate editors with
hopeless rubbish are doing precisely the same
thing, in kind, as the most accomplished man
of letters whose work is eagerly accepted at a
fancy figure. The separating gulf, enormous
though it be, is nevertheless only a chasm of
degree, and it is just the fatal inability to
appreciate degree—the total lack, in fact,
of a chief essential to any effective literary
equipment—that produces the mighty swarm of
people who can’t write, and don’t know it !
and who stand, therefore, upon even a lower
literary level than those who can’t write and do
know it ! Mostly, also, it is the man who
can’t-write-and-doesn’t-know-it who is the
readiest to charge either inadequacy or dis-
honesty upon all criticism of his work that is
not at least commendatory. More especially is
this true of the doggerelist, w'ho is so cheer-
fully ready to label his futilities “ poetry ”
—a designation never arrogated by any
writer who, though capable of “ perfect
thoughts in perfect numbers,” is also cap-
able, ipso facto , of understanding that verse
—however excellent as verse—has no necessary
connection w'ith poetry. Dickens, for instance,
is, here and there, as poetic in his prose as he is
prosy in his “poetry.” The more, too, a man
has in him, the more likely he will be to suspect
that for a consensus of unfavorable criticism
there must be some justification in his work.
But, w'ith the literary tyro—and still more with
the absolute poetic incapable—there are only
two possible explanations of the fact that he
has been unanimously so classified by those
whose opinions in such matters are commer-
cially considered worth paying for and printing.
Either—in spite of this recognised professional
status —the critics simply don’t know what
they’re talking about; or—in spite of the
fact that the criticised’s literary fate is
probably a matter of the profoundest
indifference to everybody but himself—they
have malignantly conspired to stigmatise pub-
licly as balderdash those exercitations which
they privately agree with the perpetrator
thereof in falling down to adore. And the
Warrior-Bard of Woollahra —reverently pro-
strate before his own poetic shrine—presents a
gorgeous example of what may happen to the
cobbler who gets so foolishly far away from the
commonplace little last for which Nature
obviously intended him.
The Scope for Law Reform.
Editor Bulletin.—The Victorian Mines Act
affords three splendid illustrations of the way
in which young countries may set to work to
successfully sweep away old-world cobwebs.
The cruel provision of English law, engendered
nobody knows how, by which an employer is
not responsible for injuries to an if
said injuries have been caused by the negli-
gence of a fellow-employ 6, has been swept
away, as far as miners are concerned, by one
simple and effective section. The doctrine that
he who uses it is alone entitled to occupy land
is given effect to in the provisions relating to
miners’ rights and labor conditions. All the
feudal notions, attached still, to our disgrace, to
agricultural land, have been found unnecessary
in this kind of property, and the working of
the clauses certainly suggests their extension to
all kinds of real property as a feasible means of
solving many agrarian problems. Indeed, we
can hope sincerely to hear of a “farmers’
rights ” movement in the near future. And
lastly, the procedural provisions of the Court
of Mines have in one section amalgamated
law and equity quite as effectually as all
the elaborate judicature provisions have
in other branches of the law, and they did it
before the Judicature Act was heard of. These
facts should waken democrats to the conscious-
ness that it is foolish to await England’s lead
before initiating reforms. What the sturdy,
commonsense miners of the fifties, assisted bj
the luminous intellect of Mr. Justice Moles
worth, could do in a sphere of law w'mre prece
dents could give no help, surely the more care
fully-trained democrats of our own day can do
in other spheres with very little outside assist-
ance. Why do not some of our single-minded
and earnest young democrats band together for
the purpose of evolving a system of law, land
tenure, &c., suited to our circumstances, and
freed from feudal and aristocratic jargon ? For
instance, as a start, the question of inheritance,
the credit system, free justice, the limitation of
liability in contracts couched in highly techni-
cal terms not understood by one of the parties,
the law of evidence, and the marital relation,
would equip a probably highly - productive
symposium of democratic enthusiasts. Politi-
cal leaders cannot be trusted in these matters,
for private law, so often fraught with more far-
reaching consequences than public law, requires
hard study and does not pan out in much
momentary applause. We must evolve it our-
selves, and until we do so will not have proved
ourselves quite worthy of the freedom we
happen, at present, to enjoy.— Danton.
The success of the plea advanced by Minister
Gould for the retention of capital punish-
ment for rape drove several opponents of capi-
tal punishment to vote for its retention for
murder, when the Alerfd Stephen Criminal
Code first came before N.S.W. Parliament.
The deceased Pa rices, who was opposed to
capital punishment on principle and voted
against its employment for rape, voted for it
afterwards for the crime of murder, on the
ground that as the House had made the former
crime capital, it was bound to make murder
capital, or the perpetrator would add murder
to his other offence. And many followed
Parkes in that view, so that the
opponents of capital punishment for mur-
der looked fewer than at the time they
really were. Gould’s plea must, therefore, be
fully met, or the division-list, whenever the
question comes to a division, will show in favor
of capital punishment those who oppose it on
priiifciple, but who will vote for it for murder
because a majority will have previously carried
it for rape. The N.S.W. law on this subject,
as it now stands, is merely a law for the En-
couragement of Murder. The man who has
committed rape, knowing that he will forfeit
his life if convicted, will, on the principles that
one may as well be hanged for a sheep as a
lamb and that dead women tell no tales, kill
the victim whom he has outraged.
Murray Smith, instructing Melbourne Reform
Club in the Political Obligations of Citizenship,
made it quite clear that he doesn’t sin through
ignorance. “ W r hat are the obligations and
dangers of the citizen ?” asked the respectable
old Tory of his professional and commercial
friends in front. They, for the most part, hold
hideously incorrect ideas on the point, but
Murray Smith gave them the platitudes of
truth to learn, mark, and inwardly—despise :
His first obligation is energy, and his first danger
apathy, which, unfortunately, is too common. This is
specially true of those classes which are most interested
in sound government, and at whose expense the political
game is played. Mismanagement, wasteful extravagance,
public corruption, crude and ill-considered legislation,
though ultimately they fall with crushing weight upon
the poor, attack primarily the great body of industrious,
peaceful men, whose only desire is to obtain a fair reward
for their industry. . . To this class we should look for
enlightened criticism and constant watchfulness.
The Bulletin approves heartily the remarks
of Murray Smith. A great weight of public
corruption has descended upon the poor citizens
of Victoria with a'sickening thud, and a number
of industrious, peaceful persons were attacked
primarily by the same shameful tiling. And in the
face of this undoubted fact The Bulletin would
like to kneel, in a metaphorical sense, upon
Murray Smith’s chest and force him to admit
that he has never once publicly wagged his
tongue against the corrupt crowd of covetous
conspirators who are mainly responsible for
Smellburst’s present stink. The apathy of
himself and his loquacious likes—men who talk
themselves into Parliament and are afraid to
condemn gilded rascality when they get there —
is the origin of scandals, and the germ of the
blight that eats into the fruit of industry.
When did the personally-honest Murray Smith
fire off enlightened criticism at the pestilential
boomsters, and how much has his watchfulness
been worth to Victoria, presuming that he
keeps his eyes fixed on the corruption of public
life ? He doesn’t even hold a handkerchief to
his political nose. If his constituents have
brains enough to comprehend the meaning "of
his Reform Club address they will fulfil their
first obligation to the State by getting rid of
Murray Smith at next election. On his own
showing, he is a dangerous character who ought
to be left to wither at the bottom of the poll.
A Bill has been introduced into Vic. Parlia-
ment to secure insurance policies against rapa-
cious creditors. It has been urgently needed,
for, although a provision already exists protect-
ing them, it has been so cut down by legal
quibbling as to be rather a trap than an aid.
The courts, for instance, have been showing
serious signs of holding that the effect of the
said provision is nullified if a man allows the
usual clause referring to payment of all his
“ just debts ”to remain in his will. Everybody
almost, except when advised, puts this in, often
from a sort of wish to curry favor on the other
side of Styx, but certainly in very few cases
with the intention of pauperising his wife and
children. The new Act aims at obviating this
danger, and also provides that in the case of
insolvency the policy to the extent of £lOOO
shall escape the creditors. The definition of
“ insolvency ” includes liquidation and cotnpo
sition, the fat-man’s paths, but does not include
assignment for benefit of creditors, which is
the ordinary mode of insolvency in the case of
poor and honest tradespeople. This is a serious
omission, which existed before, and, as it has
been taken advantage of by remorseless credi-
tors, ought at once to be remedied.
The City of Melbourne Bank prosecution will
attract some more notice to the weakness of
counsel appearing for the Vic. Crown in cases
where the country’s credit is concerned.
Purves, Q.C., Dufey, Coldiiam, Maxwell,
and Mitchell will fight for one or other of the
lefendants, and against this formidable array
jf legal talent, bluff and trickery, the Crown
pits Finlayson and Johnstone. The Vic-
torian public must be growing weary of
Finlayson, whose particular forte is gentle-
manly forbearance ; and, anyway, the
public would like to know why Attorney-
General Isaacs doesn’t appear in court for his
own department, as a British Attor.-Cen.
would under similar circumstances. Isaacs of
yore protested against the nolle prosequi of his
superior officer, Bryan O’Logiilen, in the
Great Davies Scandal, and now is the young
man’s chance to prove himself a real champion
of fair play. Let him give the Crown a chance
to obtain a thorough trial by taking a hand in
it himself.
A characteristic sample of Premier Reid on
the blessings of Free-raid :
I am happy to say I can give this committee one of the
most Convincing tests in the world of the difference
between buying in a colonial Freetrade market and buy-
ing in a colonial Protectionist market. As this committee
knows, the Government of N.S. Wales take large con-
tracts every year for supplies for the public service. Our
superintendent of stores went down to Melbourne two or
three months ago, and he took the Government contracts
for this year in Victoria and our contracts, and the prices
in both.' He took out lines identical in quality, number-
ing 121 lines, of which I have a list. The prices were
taken out to Id. According to the prices which the Vic-
torian people pay on the Victorian articles, these would
cost us £21,069 for the year’s supply. This is according
to Melbourne prices. Yet the Sydney contractors’ prices
for the same articles for which Victoria pays £21,069 are
£10,400. That is a little bit of cold iron for my friends to
chew.
There are just eleven things wanted to make
this statement complete. It doesn’t say
whether the New South Wales contracts
referred to the first part of the financial year
under Protection or the second part under
partial Freetrade; or whether the goods in
question were protected in either province ; or
what goods they were ; or why figures were
not given for all the Government contracts,
amounting to some .£500,000 a year, instead of
for only one-fiftieth of them. It omits to men-
tion whether there were any other lines in
which the figures were the other way about;
or which province comes out cheaper on the
whole ; or what is the good of quoting 121
articles of such utter unimportance that the
province only uses £B9 worth of each article
per annum ; or where the list is to be seen ; or
who guarantees its accuracy ; or what evidence
there is, in the absence of publication, that there
is any such list at all. Otherwise it is a good,
useful-looking piece of evidence.

Professor C. A. Wood, 8.A., lecturing to the
Sydney “ Young Men Christians,” on “ The
War of American Independence ; its Place in
the History of the English People,” is reported
by Sydney D.T. as having “ failed to see” why
Americans keep up the 4th of July, and why
they “rejoice in their disunion with the
British.”
Instead of their rejoicing in the fact that they lived
under separate flags, they should rather lament it. . . .
He hoped that Americans would regard the anniversary
of the declaration of American Independence not as a day
of rejoicing, but as a day of humiliation.
Professor Wood hopes for the solid possession
of the moon’s reflection in a pail of water. Mr.
Wood fails to see why the descendants of men
whose fathers were first spewed out of England,
and then bullied at a distance and enslaved by
England, should rejoice about the day that gave
them the freedom of manhood and self-master-
doin ! Mr. Wood is of that sample of last-
century Englishman wffio thought he and his
kind alone had a right to own, to’ buy, to sell,
and to whop niggers ; and who could not under-
stand why a nigger should celebrate the day
that he became his own chattel.
A Tasmanian lawyer who pleaded guilty and
was sentenced to 18 months for a £l2OO forgery
at Launceston, was allowed to wear his ordinary
clothes on the rail-journey to Hobart gaol.
Other prisoners, convicted of much smaller
offences, get no such consideration in V.D.L.
It is thus made evident, for the thousandth
time, that there is one law for broadcloth and
another for moleskin. This paper holds that
being hawked through the public thorough-
fares in convict-garb is no part of a prisoner’s
sentence, but is an indignity to which (as tend-
ing to destroy the last rags of a man’s self-
respect) no criminal should be subjected ; yet
there is room for emphatic protest against the
disgraceful maladministration which sends the
uninfluential to gaol in leg-irons and broad-
arrows, while allowing the other sort to weai
on the way tailor-made suits and other appur
tenances of free and prosperous citizens.
Minister Cook’s Local Option Bill, now almost
ready for submission to N.S.W. Parliament,
may be termed a Bill to make every day Sunday
The present Sunday liquor-law is as drastic at
pure Local Option can hope to make its own
measure, and what is the consequence? Twenty -
three out of every twenty-five public-houses in
the land sell on Sundays, and break the law.
Can Local Option shut up pubs, more effectually
than tlffiy are now shut on Sundays ? If so, vt it li
what machinery ? And if it shut up certain
places called pubs., how will it reach the vendors
of ginger-wine who understand the meaning of
a wink ? And if Local Option go the length of
prohibition, will not private stills go to work in
lanes and backyards ? Where Sunday-selling
cannot be suppressed with all the law at com-
mand, what hope is there that Local Option can
do better? Reid and Want are credited with
knowing what a mistake it is to put too much
water in their whisky. To allow too much of
the watery part of their Ministry to go into the
business of Parliament is the same mistake in a
bigger affair.
Me lb. and Alfred Hospitals are querulous about
a new move of the Turner Govt, in distributing
the charitable vote—viz., the inclusion of a
clause that nurses shall not be worked more
than 168 hours in three weeks, eight hours a
day including Sundays. The hospitals work it
out that so many hundreds per annum will have
to be added to the salary-list, and so many
thousands spent for additional quarters—and
that is reckoned to be an excellent argument in
favor of sweating nurses. Rapiport was a
member of Melb. Hospital committee, and
his resignation was accepted with demur—
which shows what a nice chance nurses have.
Charity and sweating should not be combined,
and the Turner Govt, is to be commended for
trying to make Charity just before she is
generous.
