SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Lyceum . “ A Trip to Chinatown.”
Royal “A Woman’s Revenge.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s Potter-Bellew Farewells.
Royal “Trilby” Burlesque.
Bijou “Fun on the Bristol.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Exhibition Bu lding Food and Health Show.
Her Majesty’s has closed this week for re-
pairs, and this reduces Sydney to‘'three theatres
altogether. The last act, for the present at least,
in tne career of the big, luckless show-house was
Alf. Woods’ benefit, which took place with much
enthusiasm and a considerable amount of success
on Monday afternoon. It was of the usual in-
sanely scrappy order, and consisted of a little bit
of everything by everybody. A committee of 200
allegedly influential citizens tackled the social
aspect of the job, and 100 people in all took a
hand upon the stage. They included Her Majesty’s
and the Tivoli companies, the Tank, a lot of un-
attached dramatic talent, and most of the musical
ability of Sydney. The show was supported by
Premier Reid and his concealed deficit, the God-
fearing Ministry, a contingent from both Houses
of Parliament, the Mayor, and a lot of the kind of
people who have initials trailing behind them in
great quantities. The last act of the last bill put
up on the last day of the Woods-Marshall regime
was that respected farce, “ His Last Legs.” Pos-
sibly this was meant as an unkind allusion to
something or somebody; anyhow, Woods ought
certainly to have appeared in kilts just here and
returned thanks.
* * *
Hoyt’s frivolous musical trifle, at the
Lyceum, shows no change save that business is, if
possible, a little more tremendous than it was
last week, the management’s chief trouble being to
avoid prosecution for overcrowding and premedi-
tated suffocation. The “ Trip to Chinatown ”
so-called because nobody goes there—is now doing
its third week, and, on present appearances, it
should be able to run just about as long as the
Firm pleases. Up to date, this giddy nothing,
principally suggestive of a bunch of feathers in a
nurry, is said to be financially the most successful
W. and M. enterprise since the first Gaiety co.
* * * -*
It is definitely announced that the “ Derby
Winner ” will end its long career at Sydney Royal
to-morrow (Friday), and “ A Woman’s Revenge ”
follows on Saturday. Bland Holt is apparently
making a bigger pot of money this season than on
any previous visit to Sydney, and he proposes to
take the tide at the flood, in a manner of speaking,
and seize the cow’s husband by the horns, so to
speak, and put up another big spectacle before the
old one has worn out its welcome.
** * *
Now that Brough and Boucicault, whose
forte was the newest kind of giddy funnyism,
have left Sydney Criterion, Parson Haskett
Smith, who lectures on the oldest and most
mouldersome of ruins, and tells tales of dusty
catacombs, has seen fit to take over that house for
a series of entertainments. The rev. gentleman’s
show runs till to-morrow (Friday) night, unless he
decides to extend it and explain some more ruins ;
and The Bulletin cordially recommends every-
body to hear him at least once before he goes, and
thereby gain an idea of how very small a quantity
of dust he will make when he is dead. The Rev.
Smith is now delving into the shadowy wastes of
old Thebes and Memjihis and Babylon, and he
seems to know’every stone of fhese cities by
heart, and when he rebuilds them lovingly out of
his stores of remote knowledge they become so
real that the boomer almost resolves for a moment
to open a bank there immediately and issue inter-
minable inscribed stock at 4 per cent. No
one that The Bulletin has yet met can ex-
tract so much interest out of the dust and
ashes of old-time cities as Haskett Smith does.
The long immensity of the years when he unrolls
them makes Jawbone Neild, as he shrieks over his
poems, look like something |-inch high clamoring
at an atom, and the great aggregate footballer of
the period seem like an ant kicking a speck upon
a leaf, and makes his audience dream of the time
(a.d. 116,329) when people will look back and
think, in the shortening of the perspective of
things, that Victoria was right up against Noah’s
Deluge, and that Parkes and Lot were boys
together. The Bulletin loves Haskett Smith in
a dreamy, unworldly sort of way, and when it has
listened to him for an hour or so it begins to feel
that it really doesn’t matter whether the next
issue comes out on Thursday or not. The past is
such a long' eternity of dust, and the present is
fast becoming dust also, and the dust will look
just the same on the shovel whether anything
comes out or not.
** * *
William Crackles is now at the zenith of
his career at Sydney Tivoli, and Charles Godfrey
has still two weeks to run. With the Cri. and Her
Majesty’s both temporarily out of the business,
and the Gaiety, Standard, Opera House, and the
forgotten Alhambra all mouldering in permanent
dust and desolation, and the School of Arts hall
turned into a library, competition is at a very low
ebb in Sydney just now, so the three managers
who are still alive are having a period of glittering
prosperity. The Tivoli is too small for the busi-
ness that comes its way nowadays, and the pro-
spect of Harry Rickards slopping over into the
Town Hall seems imminent.
* * *■
That oldest of living musicians, the won-
drous Chevalier de Kontski, opens at Sydney
Centenary Hall on July 23. The well-known
Hugo Fischer is managing for the Chevalier.
The present double-bill at Melbourne
Princess’s seems a quick and vigorous entertain-
ment after the long French tragedies of previous
weeks. “ Cavalleria Rusticana,” in its original
dramatic form, is a crisp little story, practically
the same thing as the opera libretto, with a suit-
able orchestral accompaniment of Mascagni’s
music to help it along. The stage setting and
business management are even more effective on
the whole than in the opera, especially as regards
the mortal combat between the cuckold teamster
and the co-respondent, which is now fought out
with 18-inch blades fti the centre of a howling
mob. On the other hand, the curtain tableau was
stronger in the opera, where giddy Lola’s concern
for her husband’s safety (the other fellow
being killed) was reserved for a finishing
touch. The moral that a live husband is
better than a dead sweetheart is not enforced in
the drama. Bellew represents Turridor, the
soldier, as a less callous betrayer of his girl than
the operatic Turiddu, and Mrs. Potter is a more
crushed Santuzza than Signora Del Torre created
for us. She is charming enough in her lighter
phases of sorrow. But when a loud expression of
feeling is required Mrs. Potter becomes again the
artificial actress, struggling under a load of his-
trionic responsibility which she has not been born
or educated to carry. Scot Inglis is an interest-
ing, well-spoken teamster in a new velvet suit;
whilst poor, very poor, performances of Lola and
mamma Nunzia are given by Miss Raymond and
Mrs. Hill.
* * * *
“David Garrick” forms a cheerful con-
trast to “ Cavallieria,” also it has two chief parts
to fit the picturesque visitors. Mrs. Potter is a
pretty suggestion of a romantic girl dazzled by an
actor, and Bellew is the theatrical style of actor
who drives that sort of damsel crazy. To the
present writer’s thinking, there is very little
sincerity in the Garrick of Mr. Bellew. He doesn’t
assume drunkenness as though he meant it to be
taken seriously, or seem genuinely pained by
the thought that his hollow device is imposing on
Mrs. Potter. He appears to play at playing a
part, and at those times when his alleged love for
the lady is supposed to break through his promise
to her father, he is the stagiest of lady-killers.
One minute he pretends that he is trying to
cure his host’s daughter of her infatuation,
the next minute he employs all the tricks
of his trade to infatuate her some more. Garrick
in the drunken scene doesn’t carry out the real
meaning of the text, nevertheless he achieves an
attractive counterfeit, which is exactly what every
Bellew would do under the circumstances. His
elegant appearance is a banquet for the ravished
eye throughout. Probably no man off the stage
could become intoxicated to such advantage, and
reel with so much easy grace. Bellew is The
Bulletin’s idea of an archangel on the burst.
James Cathcart plays the old father in his best
old-fashioned method, and small character-parts
are made largely humorous by Miss Polly Emery
and Oily Peering.
** # *
The Cosgrove-Luella management started
at Melbourne Bijou with a crowded house, and a
furious rush of flowery tokens, on Saturday.
“Fun on the Bristol,” controlled by Cos-
grove instead of J. F. Sheridan, is neither as gay
as it used to be, nor as sad as many another
comedian might make it. Cosgrove follows his
model closely in some places, and in other places
his model leaves him yards behind. Likewise,
there are occasional points where he diverges from
Sheridan, but gets there all the same. Altogether,
Cosgrove’s performance of Widow O’Brien is a
genial piece of mumming, free from unpleasant
suggestion of a he-male in skirts, and full of
novelty for any person who hasn’t seen the char-
acter played by the original widow. The
rest of the show comprises John Forde,
W. J. Montgomery, and numerous musical items.
These latter are mostly sentimental and over-
anxious to accept an encore, but the voice of Miss
Marie Luella, clad in superb tights, and the
blackened warble of Miss Katie Towers, make
exceptions to the rule. Miss Luella in skirts, dis-
guised as Dora M‘Allister, seems to be at odds
with fortune and eager for the fray. “Fun on
the Bristol ” may do very well, or otherwise, at
the Bijou. Meanwhile, “ The New Housemaid ”
is an active preparation for an emergency.
** * *
Nothing has happened to damp the ardor
of the “Trilby Burlesque” boom at Melbourne
Royal. The tail end of the multitude still gets its
money refused at the doors, and venerable George
Coppin, who has an interest in the show, looks
more than ever like a public benefactor. “ Trilby ”
gathers humor as it rolls along. Svengali’s heart-
disease begins to seize him when least expected,
also that famous musician gets “tanked” with a
nightly increasing success, and his wild header
into the bowels of the piano has added a cubit to
its popularity. Gecko, too, improves on acquaint-
ance, whilst Maggie Moore remains exactly the
same invaluable asset that she was at the beginning
and evermore will be. “Trilby,” however, must
leave the Royal presently, with bright prospects
of striking Sydney at the psychological moment.
** * *
The dancing Durhams are the latest new
feature at the Opera House, where Tennyson and
O’Gorman clearly make themselves out smarter
than the previous individuals who had annexed
their style of conversation. The last nights of
the Frantz Family reveal fresh marvels in the per-
formance of this splendid acrobatic assortment,
and, as far as one can imagine, the seven Frantzs
have left nothing that is humanly possible to be
done by any similar artists present or future.
The Czar, it is cabled, has elected to
become boss publican of his wide dominions.
His last hope of coercing or cajoling the Nihilists,
who will hesitate to cut off their drink-supply at
the fountain head, as it were.
At Poverty Point .
A Brisbane critio to Tub Bulletin : —“ The performance
of the dramatised version of ‘ Trilby ’ is rather un-
natural in many points. In the first place, the strenuous
efforts which Miss Crane makes to prevent the audience
seeing more than three inches above her ankles are alto-
gether inconsistent with the character of the girl who is
in the habit of i osing for ‘ the altogether ’ to a roomful of
students. Again, in Svengali’s death-scene, although the
three knights of the brush and Zou-Zou, Dodo, and
Gecko can see that the oily Hebrew is in a pretty bad way
—sick unto death, in fact —not one of them thinks of pick-
ing him up when he is falling against the scenery, or send-
ing for a doctor. On the contrary, they all stand round
and hurl execrations on the Jew’s greasy head, and the
large-hearted Taffy takes advantage of his weakness to
seize him by the throat and violently assault him, thereby
hastening his end—a performance for which Taffy would
be legally held guilty of manslaughter at the very least.
And, after they have practically killed him off among
them, they let him lie in an uncomfortable position across
a table for about five minutes until the curtain falls.
Then the ‘ rag’ is again raised, with Svengali in the same
position, to show the audience, no doubt, that the neglect
of the ‘ Englanders’ was not an oversight on their part.”
Another Brisbane critic : “ Svengali told us he was
dying of heart disease or angina pectoris, or words to that
effect, and then acted to us that he was dying of tetanus
or strychnia poisoning. Also, Miss O’Ferrall taught us
that there is something new under the sun, to wit, a
patient recovering from brain trouble is seized with pul-
monary symptoms i! she walks about.”
Dear Bulletin, —“Camille’’and “LaTosca” are more
like living-skeleton shows than anything else except a
Rontgen X-ray exhibit. Bones are clearly visible “a-
wobblin’ like a basketful of snakes ” under the skin of
the fascinating Mrs. B.P. Bones are not pretty things
in the living subject, at all events. Mrs. Potter makes
painful and obvious efforts to avoid the commonplace.
But, unfortunately, all the great things in life are common-
place. I saw Duse, in London, open a door and walk into
a room ; she didn’t strut, or stagger, or lurch, or swagger,
or stride, or mince, or trip, or skip, she simply walked on
the stage. Quite an ordinary and everyday action. Yet
the whole audience gave a gasp, and, after the play, I
went home feeling like the last lines of Wordsworth’s
sonnet on Toussaint L’Ouverture. J.C.W. has been often
asked lately when he means to lure Duse here for a spell.
Is there a chance ? —F.
How shocked Australian ’assiety would be, writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, could it but see the
once much-gushed-over footlight darling in his latest
“ part,” the Pet of Piccadilly. The sad, suffering-a-
recovery look in those eyes now slaughters the Piccadilly
“pusslets” as formerly the dames and daughters of Potts
Point and Toorakia. The mistress or the maid, the
baroness or the ballet-girl, all are the same to this rakish
thespian.
Actor George Leitch is camped with a party of pros-
pectors on a lonesome island in Bass Straits, and, it is
feared, is perpetrating a novel. Some say it is a drama,
to be entitled “The Island of the Mutton Bird.”
The stage-stricken son of a one-time Melb. turf-celebrity,
writes a London correspondent to Tiie Bulletin, carries
his love for the “ purfesh ” to the extent of wearing his
facial make-up both day and night. Ugh !
Maggie Moore is much beloved of the Chow kiddies of
Lilly Boulke-stleet, who invariably accumulate round the
stage-door when Maggie holds the stage. When George
Rignold is lessee, the little Chowkins hide themselves
under the beds and up the spout at home and hibernate
till Maggie returns.
Wfat price “The Comedy of Errors” with Scot Inglis
and Harry Roberts as the Antipholi, and Edwin Kelly and
his son, Edwin Lester, as the two Dromios? They could
become as facially and physically alike as ever did Hos-
kins and Kennedy and the two Holloways.
Ada Crossley writes by last mail to a Sydney friend.
She had just returned from the great Cardiff festival,
where she achieved a great success (as per a huge bundle
of leading-paper notices), and for her “ O Rest in the
Lord” got, says the London Daily News, “a perfect
storm of applause.” The Magazine of Music, writing of
the Patti concert at Bournemouth, says that Miss Cross-
ley’s “ beautiful contralto voice and perfect style make
her a favorite wherever she goes.” She is well booked to
the middle of next year (including many oratorio engage-
ments), and expects to go to Switzerland in Sept, for rest.
Now comes a cable that she has sung before and been
greatly complimented by the Princess and Princesses of
Wales.
Poor Kate Yaughan, as the result of her recent benefit,
is coming to Australia accompanied by a nurse.
In America Kyrle BelT.w received many letters from
stage-struck society maidens asking him to play Romeo
to their Juliet. Kyrle invariably replied that his terms
were 500dOls. for each performance, and this generally
extinguished the maidenly enthusiasm. On one occasion,
however, a mature damelet agreed to the terms, and the
curly one was called on to woo the deadly Juliet. He
says now the agony of that performance was underpaid
at £lOO.
The unappreciated Arthur Jilwood, whom Boucicault
brought out last year, gets plenty of butter for his
wounded feelings from the London critics. Clement
Scott says: “Mr. Arthur Ehvood takes up Lord Jura
again and plays it far better than ever. His voyage to
Australia has been of immense advantage to him. The
same beautiful voice is there ; the same persuasive charm ;
but Jura has never before so enlisted our sympathies, for
he is a rarity in these days—a tender-hearted man. The
death-scene of Jura seemed to us beautiful.”
Elwood’s style was certainly suited to t,he delineation of a
calm passing hence.
“ Old Bigot” writes “ A treasured memory of Kyrle
Bellew. The Bigots’ Club in Dublin was—maybe is still—
a little gathering of journalists, artists, et hoc, who were
not bigoted about anything but liquor, and met every
Thursday night at nine o’clock until three o’clock on
Friday morning. Any distinguished visitor to the city
was made an honorary member during his stay, which
meant that every Thursday night he was expected to eat
stewed oysters, drink Guinness’s stout, and sing, recite,
or tell a new yarn. When Bellew brought along ‘ Romeo
and Juliet’to Michael Gunn’s Gaiety Theatre he called
in after the show on his first Thursday night, and later
got up to recite ‘ The Charge of the Light Brigade.’ It
was grandly done until the third stanza, when he ‘ forgot
his lines.’ One of the bhoys prompted him, and with a
profound bow of dignified gratitude, Bellew proceeded.
An hour or two later, when the crowd was dispersing in
the street, Bellew asked John Fergus O’Hea, ‘ the Arch-
bigot of Dublin,’ i.e., president of the Club, and then
cartoonist of United Ireland, which was the gentleman
who had been kind enough to prompt him. When he
was pointed out the eminent actor took him gently by
one shoulder, turned him round, and—kicked him. Then
he shook hands with him, and with an Irvingesque
‘ Good-night, gentlemen,’ vanished.”
Manager Williamson in Melbourne didn’t take kindly to
two of the Potter-Bellew dramas— “ Raquin ” and “ Fran-
cillon,” which partner Musgrove had recommended for
production. They are cerulean stories, both of them, and
J.C.W. accepts the comparative failure of “Camille” as
a proof that the local public doesn’t want more naughti-
ness at present.
Bellew, confiding in a Melb. interviewer, deduces from
the fact that the local playgoer does not display frantic
admiration for La Potter a conviction that Australian
audiences are far behind the times and cannot appreciate
subtlety of interpretation. Speaking of self and Brown-
Potter, the modest Kyrle remarks : “ It takes a public
that has been slowly developed in taste to appreciate so
big a change as this. [The change from mere melo-
dramatic ranting to ‘ subtlety of interpretation.] We have,
therefore, appeared here to some disadvantage.” Prac-
tically, the taste of the public is not educated up to a due
appreciation of the heavenly twins, Potter and Bellew.
The Divine Cora’s sorrow, it will be seen, is very like that
of Katisha : she has a moral collar-bone that is a miracle
of loveliness and a wisdom-tooth that almost stands alone,
but the dumb-driven public of Melbourne is oblivious to
these treasures ; for which, says Kyrle, the dunder-headed
critics are to blame. Which shows that P. and B. have
not struck overmuch oil, or butter, this trip.

Maggie Moore will really leave for America in Septem-
ber with a nice bag of “ Trilby ” profits to invest in new
dramatic goods, and a heart even fuller of gratitude than
when she came to Melbourne the other week. That advt.
of M.M.’s gratitude, by the way, was quite a genuine out-
burst. The perennial favorite recognises that the public
have allowed their liking for herself to color their opinion
of her show on some occasions. The common saying,
“ Maggie Moore is always good,” carries a second mean-
ing. They reckon her a real good sort as well as a real
good actress, and find a particular pleasure in being enter-
tained by her. The house is always with Maggie Moore.
“ The Voice of the Sea,” by riHolus, is a new song from
Allan’s, suitable for any mezzo-soprano or baritone who
may be feeling rather sad. It is a tuneful little ballad of
the dirge variety, and so brief that it comes to a full stop
ere the first tear of sympathy can begin to flow. In fact,
it is a very good thing to warble in the twilight, and
much less commonplace than several more pretentious
ballads just received from Ricordi, of London. Local
taste in song-construction Is improving.

“ The fool and his money are soon parted.” This par-
ticular fool is financing a biggish theatrical venture and a
professional lady of great velocity, who has already many
scalps at her girdle, the cowlicks of men of means who
tried to finance her alone and went under like plummets.
When the man of golden means gets in tow of the woman
of extremes it is practically all U.P. with the former, but
when a theatrical co. is thrown in to assist in the looting,
that man can go to bed and hear his money humming as
it runs.
The production of “La Tosca ” close upon the heels of
“ Charlotte Corday ” —said to be a little thing of Bellew’s
very own—is a decided give-away. The Corday drama is
simply “La Tosca ” writ small, with a dash of “ The
Village Priest” thrown in. In “Charlotte Corday” you
have in Marat the counterpart of Scarpia and Corday for
Tosca, and you get the fugitive from justice, followed in
both plays to the home of the artist, and in both plays the
heroine pig-sticks the villain to save her lover.

bournites. On Sunday nights he has been at the German
club drinking much lager beer and smoking many cigars,
yet at the end of the evening is as spry as a young man.
At Prof. Hall’s concert last Saturday the Chevalier was
noticed reading the flowery annotator’s small print with-
out specs.

The Wild-Cat Column.
N.S. Wales, according to Treasurer Reid, has
for the year ending- 30th June a handsome surplus of
£333,000. As, however, the usual statements of the
year’s receipts and ordinary and loan expenditure have
not been supplied at time of writing, it is absolutely im-
possible to form any opinion as to how much of the
surplus is genuine, or whether there is any real surplus
or whether there is a deficit, or what there is. On the old
basis the liabilities of the year, even if they were not paid
at the date when the accounts closed, were reckoned as
expenditure, but. on the new “ cash basis ” everything
which is not actually paid, even if it is far overdue,
is left to be provided for next year. Considering
that the year’s liabilities which are not actually
paid at the date when the accounts close often reach
£500,000, the advantage which Treas. Reid gains
by making no provision for these and leaving them bodily
out of his calculations should account for all the
surplus and a good deal more. The present sys-
tem is undoubtedly the best one, but the fact
that it provides a surplus for the first year
—the year which had no liabilities left over from
the one before, and which carries all its unpaid bills on to
the one after—is no special proof of brilliant financing.
No estimate is furnished as to the amount of the liabilities
thus left over, but they are known to include .£55,000
under the item of railway loans, £28,000 towards redemp-
tion of Treasury bills, another £75,000 under the same
heading, £37,500 under another Railway Loan item, and
£12,000 towards the Naval Tribute, and the ordinary
unpaid accounts—which may reach half-a-million—in
addition, so the total apparently saved in this way must
be very large. Further, in the absence of any statement
of expenditure it is impossible to tell how much of the
surplus has been arrived at by charging repairs to roads
and bridges and dredges and other works of daily occur-
rence to loan expenditure. In fact, in the absence of all
detail it is impossible to be sure of anything.
* * * *
It is a truly wondrous thing that N.S.W.’s
Freetrade Government last year raised £1,825,239 out of
Customs duties—almost the same amount as in Victoria.
Truly Freetrade is arriving very slowly.
The net public debt of N.S. Wales at June 30
was £60,656,873, and the increase for the year was
£2,437,340. The province passed the twenty-million limit
in 1882 ; it got over the thirty-million line in 'B5, and
passed the forty-million milestone in ’BB. In ’92 it got
past fifty millions, and now ’96 sees it well past sixty
millions. For 16 years there has not been one year
when the proportion of debt per head has not grown
bigger. The next borrow is to take place (locally, it is
alleged) in a few months, so the debt will now start to
sprint gaily for the seventy-million limit, and, with good
luck, it should not be far off at the close of the century.
• * * *
The balance-sheet of the Bank of New Zealand
for the year ended 31st March doesn’t contain nearly so
much information as it might do, but the results, on the
whole, are much better than they have been of late. The
paid-up capital is now £2,547,413. Of this, £2,000,000 is
4 per cent, stock sold in London under State guarantee ;
£500,000 is in 34 per cent, preference-shares taken up by
the State ; and" £47,423 is the small remains of the once
immense capital of the private shareholders. -The net
profits for the year are £52,576, so presumably this means
the profit after paying 4 per cent, interest on the £2,000,000
stock and 34 per cent, on the £500,000 of State-owned
preference-shares, also the £50,000 which the bank is sup-
posed to pay annually towards the expected deficit in the
Estates Co/ It isn’t specifically stated that these charges
are provided for, but the term “net profit” certainly
implies so. On this basis the £52,576 is a remarkably good
return for the year’s work. The deposits are nearly
£10,000,000, and the total liabilities to the public about
£11,600,000. Against this, the B.N.Z. has £1,750,000 coin
and bullion ; over £1,000,000 in Stace sc curities the
usual big, indiscriminate item for “advances, &c.
One quaint item in tbe balance-sheet is the good-
will of the Colonial Bank, £75,000. This was the sum
actually paid for the Colonial’s business, so, in a sense, it
is legitimate enough. At the same time, it will obviously
have to be written off, for no bank can keep reckoning the
goodwill of its own business as an asset for ever, and
writing this off means £75,000 of leeway to make up.
Another asset which looks as if it wanted a lot of explan-
ation is “ shares in the B.N.Z. Estates Co. £1,089,822.
In the absence of any balance-sheet of the Estates Co. it
is impossible to guess whether its shares are worth some-
thing or nothing or less than nothing, and if so how much
less, but considering all things the million or so (book-
value) of the shares held by the B.N.Z. is a somewhat
shadowy-looking thing. Altogether, the exact position of
the B.N.Z. is a hard matter to arrive at on the informa-
tion supplied. However, if the year’s net. profit of
£62,576 is the profit after paying not only the 4 per cent,
interest on the £2,000,000 guaranteed stock, and 34 per
cent, on the State’s preference shares, but also the stipu-
lated £50,000 towards the shortage in the Estates Co., the
bank is doing remarkably well. And if the last item has
still to be provided for out of the profit, the B.N.Z. is
more than paying its way, which, considering the extra
expense of the last reconstruction and of the Colonial
Bank amalgamation, is soi*sthing. And if there was any
information as to when the realisation of the Estates Co. s
assets might be finished, and how long the B.N.Z. might
have to contribute towards the deficiency therein (if there
still appears to be one), and if some estimate were given of
the real value of the Estates Co.’s shares instead of simply
mentioning that they represent “ book-values/’ it might
be possible to form a more definite opinion. The B.N.Z.
was such an incredible muddle before the State took hold
of it that the chaos resolves itself very slowly.
Compared with such institutions as the National
of Australasia or the Victoria, the B.N.Z. has a hard row
to hoe. The National has almost as large a capital, but it
is the shareholders’ own capital; and, times being had, the
bank simply pays no dividends on the biggest part of it
until thev improve. The B.N.Z.’s capital is almost all
money borrowed at a fixed rate, and it must pay its 4 per
cent, or per cent, dividend whether times improve or
not. „ *

The conversion proposals of the A.J.S. Bank
have been accepted by about £1,000,000 worth of London
depositors, so about £500,000 will be paid off immediately
and £600,000 more renewed at 3 per cent. This successful
operation means a saving of £30,000 a year interest, which
is a considerable change for the better; and, as the sa'mg
is effected off the English depositors, it also means that
£30,000 a year less goes from this distressful country to
help along the dear oM mother-land. #

The half-yearly report of the Trustees, Execu-
tors, and Agency 00. (Melb.), which appears in another
column, is a considerable amount better than the last one,
inasmuch as the net profits are £1702, against £256 last
half and £305 the one previous. Out of this profit the
directors spend £l2OO in paying a dividend at 4 per cent,
par annum, which leaves £502 to be added to profit-and-
loss, making the balance carried forward £4235. Con-
sidering the general state of the co., this dividend looks
rather uncalled-for. Six months ago £45,000 was taken
from reserve and allegedly put to a contingency-account;
but no contingency-account appeared in the balance-
sheet, and the amount was apparently all written off to
cover losses. This reduced the reserve to £40,000. The
present balance-sheet looks as if some more writing-off
was required before things got straight. The co.’s free-
hold property still stands at ) 65,000, and, as the net re-
turns for the half-year are £7l_9s. 2d. (equal to less than
five shillings per cent, per annum), it is just possible that
some writing-down would do this item good. Again, the
assets include £14,000 preference-shares and £lB,OOO de-
posits of the Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank.
These items don’t appear—so far as Tub Bulletin can
trace—to have been written down, except that £2OOO has
been taken off the deposit-item ; and if, with this
exception, they are taken at par, which seems to
be the case, the lamentable fact must be faced
that the deposits have a market-value of only
about £15,000, and that the £14,000 of preference-
shares have no very definite or certanuralue at all that
this paper is aware of. Anyhow, the fact th»££49,507 of
mortgages, preference shares, deposits, and smre deben-
tures appear to have yielded only £4(71 for the six months
(equal to a little less than 2 percent, per annum all
round), and that even leaving out' fhe•-pi:eference shares,
which produce no dividend at present, "the return is less
than 3 per cent, per annum, it would sAem as if some-
thing wanted writing down rather badly._ The busi-
ness is improving greatly ; the cash in bank is £3200 more
than it was six months ago ; and as a trustee the co. looks
absolutely sound, but there is a lot of leeway to make up
before it amounts to much from a shareholder’s point of
view.

The Boyal Bank of Queensland issues another
prosperous-looking report. The half-year’s net profits to
30t.h June are £6445, as against £6032 last half and 5946
at 30th June, ’95. A dividend at the rate of Wi per cent,
absorbs £4687 10s., and the fact that the Boyal keeps so
persistently to this small div. and doesn’t struggle after
an unattainable 5 per cent, is a good point in its manage-
ment. Another £l5OO is carried to reserve, making it
£31,500. The Royal’s reserve is all separately invested in
State securities, so that it actually is a reserve. By its
reconstruction-scheme, this, institution is pledged to
put one-fourth of its profits to this fund, and
if it has patience and brains enough to keep
on this course for a century or so, it should
became one of the safest and richest institutions in Aus-
tralia. The Royal’s total liabilities to the public, including
its reconstructed deposits, are £760.0 r.i, and against this
it has £215,000 in cash and State securities, equal to about
30 per cent. After paying dividend amt dividend-tax and
reserving the above mentioned £l5OO, there remains £7178
to credit of profit and loss, and £4OOO of this is applied to
start a contingency fund to cover possible losses and de-
preciation in the'future—a distinctly wise move, as it
may save the reserve from serious damage when the next
catastrophe comes along. Altogether, the position of the
Royal looks satisfactory in every respect.

The S.M. Herald objects to the State fire-
insurance idea in these terms:—“The M.L. Government
has not given better results from its life-insurance depart-
ment than have been obtained from, say, the management
of the Australian Mutual Provident Society, its most
vigorous competitor, and there is reason to doubt whether
a fire-insurance department will give a S', comparatively
good results as the life office.” This comparison with the
A.M.P. Society is the stereotyped argument. But the
A.M.P. had for many years practically a monopoly, and it
got together an immense connection before competition
began to run up the expenses. The Maoriland Govern-
ment Department, when it grows as large and as obi as
the A.M.P., may show as cheap a system of working.
Meanwhile its case is this : It is safer, by reason of its
State guarantee, than any other office. It is worked at a
lower cost than any private office of its size and age. Its
bonuses are lower than those of its principal private rival,
the A.M.P. Society, but this is in great part because its
premiums are much lower. Bonises are simply arrived
at by charging more premium t han is necessary and hand-
ing back the surplus ; at age 40, for £lOOO. of assurance,
the M.L. Government Department charges £2 15s. a year
less than the A.M.P., which is equal to a bonus of over £5
at the end of the year. Also, the percentage of expenses
is higher, but this is partly because the premiums are less;
£lO of expenses on £lOO of premiums is 10 per cent., but
if the premiums for the same business are only 91, then
the same expenses are 11 per cent. On a fair basis of com-
parison the State insurance office is an amazingly short
distance behind its far larger and older private com-
petitor. * m
The provision in the Vic. Companies Bill which
enacts that every co. must have its balance-sheet posted
up in the office where it will be visible to all comers
brought up that weariful person, ex-Treas. Carter, of
meat-man Patterson’s late Ministry. Carter said that
this move would only “pander” to somebody or other,
and would produce untold disaster of some kind or other.
And when it was mentioned to him that many banks post
up their balance-sheets now, and that most companies
with which the public is concerned advertise them in
the papers as well, Carter was almost too surprised to
speak.
♦ * * *
The Companies Amendment Act (Vic.) presents
a very one-sided appearance to ex-Premier Shiels, who
himself dabbles in finance. “The solvency of a c 0.,”
according to Shiels, “doesn’t depend upon stringent
legislation, but on that shrewd intuitive knowledge of
business which leads directors and managers to lend
moneys wisely.” By the same token the insolvency' of a
company depends upon that intuitive greed for gold
which leads directors to gamble with the cash. Stringent
legislation is required to make directors go straight.
That’s all. The policeman is the strongest of all incen-
tives to honesty.

we hope to congratulate Vic. Parliament upon a useful
measure,” says Melb. Argus. “ We hope !” Ominous.

The new Vic. Insurance Bill contains a very
proper provision making the age of the insured, as stated
in the policy, unchallengeable after a year from the issue
of the policy.
* * * *
The City of Melbourne Bank men are to be
prosecuted for “ concurring in publishing a false balance-
sheet,” and not for conspiring. “In view of the great
difficulty of proving conspiracy, it was considered advis-
able to substitute ‘concurring,’” says the officially-in-
formed Argus, but the explanation of this change doesn’t
harmonise with recent statements in Parliament. There
(and elsewhere in Melbourne) the lawyers have a cant say-
ing that “ nothing is so easy to prove as conspiracy.”
The old “ gag ” has served its turn. Now The Bulletin
waits patiently for the news that nothing is so easily dis-
proved as concurrence.

The Manly Co-operative Ferry Co., having no
alternative, has at last allowed itself to be swallowed by
its elder and more resourceful rival. Only part of the co.
died peacefully, the rest of it yelled loudly while being
carried to the grave.
* * * *
The Burglary Insurance Co., Melb., has had a
shortlife, the N.Z. Accident Co. having taken over the
concern. Insufficiency of biz proved too much for the
old co. The average Melbournite has nothing to risk, and
no common-sense burglar dreams of burgling anyone in
Smellbourfie nowadays—in fact, the genus burglar was
starved out long ago down South, ever since the day
when men with shaven upper-lips commenced to rule as
cashiers, head accountants and managers. They scooped
everything worth having. The N.Z. Accident Co. antici-
pates making the burglary insurance pay as a branch, as it
will not involve the cost of an extra office-boy.
* * ♦ *
The Reid Govt, fondly hopes that the fees here-
tofore paid to officials for swearing affidavits and exhibits
will now go into the public Treasury. But no Govt,
official is obliged to take affidavits. As the fees will not
now go into his pocket he will be too busy to “ swear ”
anything, but will often pocket a share of the fees all the
same. “Take ’em to Jones,” an official will say, “he’ 11
swear ’em for you,” and Jones and the official will whack
the fees.
* * * *
“ I have lately had disclosed to me the methods
of operation in the money markets in London, and I
never conceived before the depths of ruffianism in the
promotion of companies until I read that we have villains
in Melb., but they are mere children compared with the
deep, dark villains in London.” — Higgins, Vic. M.L.C.,
in the Companies Bill debate. * *
Let those who are being enticed to embark in
companies projected to work small sulphide-bearing
mines in the Broken Hill district reflect upon the position
at Broken Hill itself. There, big mines like the South,
developed on the proceeds of the carbonate ores, and
already equipped with winding and pumping plant, can
make only a small profit, if any at all, though the rail-
way-trucks run right to tlie shaft’s mouth, bringing
timber, coal, &c., and taking away ore and concen-
trates, on a specially-low freight-scale. These Broken
Hill mines have ore bodies which, for size and regu-
larity, are incomparably the largest and best in the
district, and yet the payment of divs. is a laborious
process, expedited now' and then by the discovery of
some bier “ chimney ” like that of the Junction, or by
the operation of the carbonates which still remain in
small quantities in the upper levels. If the investors
saw the economies (especially in the matter of hand-
ling the ore) that have to be exercised at the Broken
Hill mines to enable a profit to be made out of sulphides,
they would hesitate to put a penny into a sulphide mine
that hasn’t a railway running alongside its shaft. Some
day, perhaps, when a chemical or electrical process is
available for the extraction of the metals from the ore 'at
the mine, w’ithout the necessity to concentrate or roast
the ore, these small outside “ shows ” may be valuable ;
but, for the present, when water is necessary to concen-
trate, and when the weighty concentrates haye to be
carted to the seaboard, their prospects are inexpressibly
poor.
r * * * *
On a genuine enough improvement in the west
deep crosscut in the B.H. North shares have firmed. But
though they have an ore body it is poor, bearing no re-
semblance to the west lode in the Junction, of which it is
probably an extension.
“ Anticipation is greater than realisation,” and
so British shares “ slumped ” after the concentrating mill
was started. “There’ll be a rise when the mill gets
going,” everybody said ; and so everybody started to
“ pay out ” scrip at the same time. The result is quite
surprising to a lot of people who believed they had struck
such a brilliant idea. Yet the mill goes well, compara-
tively speaking, and underground few mines will go on
improving as the British does.

In connection with the Newcastle coal-miners’
strike and foreign competition, here is a striking com-
parison of the export-trades of N.S.W. and Japan since
1890 : -
Year. N.S.W.—Tons. Japan.—Tons.
1891 ... 2,244,729 ... 895,320
1892 ... 2,191,705 ... 900,398
1893 ... 1,835,090 ... 1,094,754
1894 ... 2,125,125 ... 1,265,504
1835 ... 2,166,230 ... 1,376,068
But a comparison of the values of the exports, stated in
the respective currency of the two countries, is still more
striking :
N.S.W.— £. Japan.—Yen.
1891 ... 1,160,965 ... 3,179,202
1892 ... 1,023,395 ... 2,854,299
1893 ... 814,929 ... 3,288,842
1894 ... 804,769 ... 4,674,304
1895 ... 773,954 '... 5,409,110
The figures are taken from the annual reports of the
N.S.W. Department of Mines and the last report of the
Deliartment of Finance of Japan respectively. Stated in
other terms, the selling-price of coal shipped by the white
man has fallen from 10s. 4d. per ton in LB9l to 7s. Id. per
ton in 1895; the selling-price of that shipped by the
yellow man has risen from 3J yen in 1891 to 4 yen in 1895.
* * * *
The lucky tributors (four men) of Great Cobar
(N.S.W.) copper-mine are understood to be now making
for themselves a steady £lOOO a week net profit. Between
1870 and 1889 the mine, according to Sydney D.T., pro-
duced 23,610 tons of copper from 213,182 tons ore, and
paid 1.151,000 in divs. But the shareholders didn’t know
that those 23,610 tons each contained 4oz. gold ! so that,
,say, £370,00il worth of gold was given away absolutely
without consideration. What a comforting reflection to
the innocents who turned over the mine to the tributors
as a troublesome and doubtful concern ! The tributors
are understood to he Messrs. A. J. Gould (N.S.W. Justice-
Minister), A. A. Dangar (two shares), Dr. Reid, of Single-
ton, and Longworth, manager and master-spirit, to whom
the enormous success of the enterprise is -almost solely
d le. The tributors, by the aid of water-jacket furnaces,
turn out, say, 3100 tons of copper annually (present value,
about £136,000, besides £50,090 worth of gold), as against
1460 tons, the highest output of the old company under
its antiquated system. They are said to have bought up
a very large number of the co.’s shares, and their tribute
has six-and-a-half years still to run.

* * * *
From Marble Bar (W.A.) : —Re N.W. wild-
cats and Geraldton Express, your leading article on the
Westralian mining-boom applies equally to North and
South. The gross ignorance shown by the British investor
is astounding. Dozens of reefs have been floated for large
amounts, which have not a ghost of a chance of ever pay-
ing even working expenses, and in many cases managers
are put in charge who have not the faintest idea of
mining, and squander the money of the foreign pigeon
w’ithout making even the slightest show of work. Small
leaders, buck reefs, conglomerate lodes—everything in
the shape of a matrix of gold has been palmed off on the
foreign mug.
* * * *
London Figaro wrote on May 21st: “After
some months of anxious waiting, and one or two false
starts, the boom in West Australian mining-shares seems
at last to be fairly under weigh.” The fifty or sixty W.A.
mines in liquidation, or about to liquidate, must have
been floated during one of the boom’s false starts.
* * * *
The General Gordon North Extended has been
re-baptised as the General Gordon Junction, and will be
floated (D.V.) under the briefer name. There are tales of
new developments in the G.G. Junction, which, on more
or less unreliable information, is to prove “ the dandy of
the line.” Meanwhile, the G.G. line seems to be chiefly
famous for its rich market resources. The gold has yet to
come.
* * * *
Mr. Zeb. Lane, at the Great Boulder meeting
in London “ The report I made in the prospectus
clearly set out that the value of the Boulder lode was
equal to soz. to the ton. I have returned to you, and, 1
think, proved that that statement is amply verified.
(Loud applause.) The mine started with great opposi-
tion, and after I left you I heard serious opposition was
brought to bear to see if they could not prove that the
mine was actually valueless.” Every sentence of Zeb s
speech was applauded, and when he was finished he was
“greeted with loud and continuous cheering.’ The
world judges all things by their results : the Great Boulder
is, in a way, the Bean case of mining. Not the faintest
allusion was made to the fact that the lode alluded to in
the original reports is not the lode from which the Boulder
gold is coming—-nobody then dreamt of the latter s
existence. The present rich lode was found long after-
wards, on cutting a very long and deep trench to try and
find the source of rich gold got by (try-blowers. This,
when struck, consisted of two very thin rich veins which,
when sunk on, opened out into a formation carrying good
gold from wall to wall. Marvellous as Lane’s luck has
been, it has not been big enough to go all round the W.A.
mines with which he has been mixed up, but the success
of one Great Boulder is enough to cover many failures.
* * * *
Somebody wants to know why it is that the
W.A. mining-market fell so sharply two days after the
heavy rain-fall over the Coolgardie fields. Was it the rush
to unload as soon as the fields, like the stocks, had been
copiously watered?

English shareholders at their half-yearly meeting in Lon-

* * * *
The alluvial mines at Pitfield and Rokewood, in
Victoria, are supposed to be a continuation of the main
leads which made the Sebastopol plateau (Ballarat)
famous in the early days of alluvial mining in Victor!'.
In another column is published the prospectus of th;
British Banner G.M. Co. (No Liability), being formed in
Melbourne to work a property of 200 acres at Pitfield.
This area is supposed to contain the same gutter from
which seven or eight mines—among them the New
Estate, the Beal Estate, the Eldorado, the New Moon, the
West Estate, the Pitfield Plateau, and the Union Co. —are
now getting gold. All these leads are supposed to trend
directly into the British Banner ground, where the exist-
ence of the gutter has been proved by boring. Capital is
required to put down a new shaft, so 12,000 shares are now
offered to the public at 2s. each. Mr. George D. Meudell
and Messrs. Ellison and Evered, brokers, Melbourne, are
open to receive applications. Some .well-known mining
men are on the directorate.*

Tasmania G.M. shareholders are wondering
where all the money goes. May crushings panned out :
Retorted gold valued at £12,769. This only paid a Is. 6d.
div. and income-tax, £3780, reserve-fund, £l5OO, general
account, £l2OO, leaving for working account, £6289.
June crushings : Retorted gold worth £15,476 ; another
18d. div. and income-tax, £3780, reserve-fund, £l5OO, and
£10,196 of which no particulars have been given. Adding
to this about £3560, estimated value of concentrates from
both crushings, we have a total of £20,045 for two months’
working expenses! According to these figures, every
pound’s worth of gold costs nearly 15s. to produce. It
looks like buying gold.

* * * *
The way in which balls are managed at
Guvmentouse, Melb., leads to curious complica-
tions. Ihe acceptable guests are divided alpha-
betically into two battalions, the first half of the
alphabet being invited to ball No. 1, and the other
half to ball No. 2. The result is that if Mr. B. is
engaged to Miss R. and has a hated rival in Mr.
W., B. never gets a chance of going to a vice-
regal ball with his girl, but has to stew at home
in the miserable consciousness that the detested
W. may be whirling round the fair R. in the in-
toxicating circles of tile waltz.

* * * *
The Vic. farmer who advertised fora wife
and got her is still airing his troubles in the court.
Xhe lady, it seems, has returned to the household
ot the solicitor with whom she had been in service
before her marriage, and refuses to join her
husband, while the solicitor refuses to attempt to
force her, or to afford facilities to her neglected
swam to even serve a summons on her. As the
.° r the restitution of conjugal rights has been
abolished in Victoria, and as the famous habeas
corpus case of Beg. v. Jackson, which denied the
husband s right to immure his wife, governs Vic.
law, it is hard to say what remedy our Meliboeus
has. He applied recently to a county court judge
to have the solicitor struck off the rolls, but was
gravely informed that the court had no jurisdic-
tion. A sympathetic barrister present advised
him to sue the solicitor for conversion. He is now,
it is said, issuing writs for tens of thousands
damages for illegal detention, dilapidation of heir-
looms, or something of that nature. The solicitor
concernpd is the principal member of one of the
chiel JVLelb. company and banking firms.

Absolute slavery is in vogue in Westralia
under the thin pretence of apprenticing aboriginal
blackboys to white settlers. These slaves are fre-
quentlv exchanged ” (in brutal candor, << sold ,, l
at so much per head. In any case, a so-called
apprentice (who knows nothing at all of the nature
ot his indenture) is the “chattel” of his master
in everything but law.
* ♦ * *
Sydney’s new Sheriff avows a determina-
tion to work the office on lines of the strictest
economy. It it hard to see how he can cut things
ner than has been the vogue for some years past,
lime was when jurors used to disagree, it seemed,
or the sole purpose of being locked up over night
such captivity meaning enjoyable companion-
ship with generous hospitality and a drive to (say)
Googeein the morning. Of late, however, it has
been cold tea, cold pie, and cold everything.
* * » *
The most hideous bicycle accident on
record happened in N.S.W. the other day. The
unfortunate rider, gliding swiftly down a steep
incline, did not notice until right on it than the
bridge below was barricaded. He collided with
he barricade, was pitched with great force over a
fit. fence, and upon arising threw away what he
took to be some foreign body that had stuck into
his forehead. It proved to be the bone of his
nose !

She has come at last. The trusting para-
dox —the widow whom 40 years of contact with
guile have not succeeded in sophisticating ! It is
a bizarre disguise; and, because human nature
jumps at oddity, even when repulsive, her adver-
tisement is almost bound to secure respectful and
maybe ardent answers. Here it is, nude from its
nest in the Sydney D.T. :
\/TATRIMONY. —Widow, under 50, no means, de-
sires correspond widower, with home, means.
Marie, P. 0., Summer Hill.
Oh, Solomon ! Who says now that the net is not
spread by the fowler in sight of the bird ?
* * * *
A Southern capital was lately in a turmoil
over rumors of several divorce-suits, in more than
one of which a “a gay Lothario” was credited
with being the disturber of conjugal felicity. He
certainly is to appear in the divorce-court : he has
surprised scandal-lovers by entering an action
against his own spouse.
* * * *
The New Woman has turned up in a prac-
tical form in Maoriland, judging from the plaint
of an infuriated Sabbatarian in a northern town.
According to this individual, two young and sinful
people in his district have become engaged.
With their joint savings they have bought a patch
of scrub-land, and every Sunday they arrive there
early and the girl clears and burns off the scrub all
day while the man pegs in vigorously at building a
wooden house. When the house is finished they
propose to get married and burn off the rest of the
scrub jointly, after which they will spend the rest
of their Sundays in putting up the fence. The
adjacent man writes to say that this line of conduct
won’t bring down a blessing on the new house,
but then nobody expects much of a blessing on a
four-roomed place built of weatherboards.
A “middle-aged” man, having been ar-
rested the other day on the N.S.W.-Yic. border
for having deserted his wife at Hay (N.S.W.), re-
marked : “I would sooner be hanged than return
to Hay.”
A Darling River boundary-rider, to make
*ure that, in his absence, travellers would not take
any of his bagful of flour, left a notice on top of
it —“Poisoned !” A swaggy passed, looked into
the hut, saw the flour, filled all his bags, and
wrote below the “poisoned”—“lf it wasn t
poisoned before, it’s poisoned now alright, you
dog.” That boundary-rider, on returning, threw
the flour into the river. He wasn’t going to chance
it.

* * * *
Wages are terribly low in Tasmania (writes
a Bulletin correspondent). One leading stud
sheep-breeder, formerly a politician, pays his
ploughman ss. per week : he has to plough an acre
per day, and cut chaff for two horses. An old
man on the same place, who acts as groom and
gardener, gets 3s. per week. I was lately in a
shearing-shed where a little boy of about 12 years
picked up and swept for 10 shearers. On my
remarking to the classer that the boy had too
much to do, he replied, “ Yes ; and the poor little
wretch gets just five bob a week for it all.” An
old maiden of means lately got a young lady as
companion, giving her £l2 a year, with a promise
of £3 more if she would milk. As no servant is

* * * *
Bank Place, off Collins-st., Melb., is the
local sharpers’ dumping-ground—and admirably
constructed it is for the purpose. There are ap-
parently only two entrances —viz., from Collins-
street and Chancery Lane but anyone who
“ knows ” is aware that there are at least a dozen
exits and entrances apart from the main thorough-
fares. The tenants on either side are either of the
Devil’s brigade, or are accountants, and to the
usual countryman the place has an eminently
respectable appearance. After the fleecing, the
victim is brought to the locality and asked to wait
outside whilst the hawk interviews a relative or
friend who occupies one of the offices. Enter the
hawk and if q lieklylostto view, never, if possible,
to again meet his victim, who at last quits his
post with the truth fully dawning upon him
—he has been shorn. One particular firm whose
principal was almost worried to death by enquir-
ing countrymen dailv wanting to know “where
their friend was—would he soon be coming out,”
&c., &c., instructed its clerks to ring up the Detec-
tive Department whenever they saw anyone wait-
ing on the pavement for more than five minutes —
the result being that hostilities have already begun
over numerous false alarms. Moreover, the legal
and financial sharks in the neighborhood now view
the game with envious eyes, and have come to the
conclusion that if the fleecer uses their premises as
avenues of escape he will either have to pay rent
or divide plunder.
* * * *
Suicide takes strange forms in the back-
country. The other day, a Queensland bushman
suicided by tying a stockwhip-lash round his
throat, fastening the other end to a tree-trunk, and
pulling violently backwards until he was stran-
gled. . . . Northern papers are also retailing
a story of a cranky Chow who took a pickaxe
down a well and hollowed out the sides until he
was crushed to death in the ultimate collapse.
His last words were, “Me allee same Blitish
subjeek.”
* * * *
A Sydney lady who has, as she puts it, the
advantage of being a divorcee, met a rebuff lately
on seeking to be re-married by a local Anglican
clergyman. He sent her angrily away with a
diocesan rule tingling in her ear. Under the
Primate a cleric may or may not, just as he likes,
marry a woman who has been divorced.
Demise : The old Age office, Elizabeth-
street, Melb., just pulled down. The London
Tavern, next door, never properly held its head
up after Syme flitted into his new Collins-street
place —more lucky than Mirams’s Premier Per-
manent palace, next door. The saying used to be
that the Age had a staff of Tories, and the Argus
one of Democrats. Most of the best Argus men,
such as Willoughby, worked on the Age. Indeed,
the Argus people used to regard the Age as their
training-university. How now? Only of com-
paratively recent years has there been the scandal
of Argus men going to the Age —but Windsor,
the only man who has edited both papers, was an
avant-courier, or stormbird. Look at “ The
Vagabond,” again—and Marcus Clarke.

A brace of burglars who “ opened ” the
suburban house of a Melbourne artist, took the
trouble to tear into a thousand bits a large picture
of Oeorge Higinbotham which hung on the wall.
Good Tories, probably, who had “ done time” on
the dead Judge’s order.
The pris’ner said, without demur,
In simple, touching' way :
“ A boon, I crave. I would prefer
If such a boon may be for me,
Though ‘ life is sweet,’ to hanged be
Upon the deadly gallows-tree,
Than going back to Hay,
Than going back to Hay—and Her !
Believe me, listen well
To what I sanely say,
For Hay, with Her, is Hell,
And Hell, without Her, Hay.”
THE CONQUEROR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Contentedly she smiles
To see him kneeling, pleading at her feet;
No tender feeling, naught of passion’s heat
The damsel knows
Till, fully conscious of his love’s defeat,
The suitor goes,
Enthralled no longer by her careless wiles.
’Tis then she smiles.
Contentedly he smiles
To hear her sobbing when, once more, they meet,
To feel the throbbing of her bosom sweet
Against his breast.
With richest spoils of victory complete
His lips are blest;
They walk on air together, many miles—
Tis then he smiles.
Reluctantly it smiles,
Whilst (calmly thinking of their youth unwise,
Each figure shrinking in the other’s eyes)
They own the truth
That, Love’s a fiction, born of lovers’ lies,
Which dies, forsooth,
When mundane fact its purity defiles.
Then Wisdom smiles.
E.F.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. bo
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

N.S.W. Public Instruction Department is different from
leave-of-absence in other departments. Teachers, you see,
get midsummer and midwinter vacations, &c Scriv. :
Bulletin never prints serials. It loathes them. Once it
printed several instalments of a continued-in-our-next
story, but after a few weeks it felt that it had a load on
its chest, and it dropped that story right there. And to the
best of this paper’s belief no one ever enquired what had
become of the rest of the tale. Bulletin doesn’t want
any story over two cols., and it has much respect for
people who, having written two cols., find that it can be
boiled down to one col.—and boil it accordingly H.C.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Time. 7.30 p.m. Place, the drawing-room of
Borwongonoo station, near Yarakagibee.
Hostess, Mrs. Sam Cameron, who was born
and bred and married and will die within
* cooee of Yarakagibee. She stands by the
piano. Her husband sits on the other side of
the fireplace. Some guests have arrived ;
others are arriving.
Mrs. Andrew Macintosh : “ Goodni-eght, Mrs.
Cameron ; how are you toni-eght ? ”
Mrs. Sam (in a low, nervous voice): “ Goodni-
eght, Mrs. Macintosh.”
Mrs. Andrew (afraid that anyone may hear) :
“ It’s a ni-ece ni-eght toni-eght.”
Mrs. Sam (she turns her head away, as though
the guests were listening): “Yes, it’s a lovely
ni-eght toni-eght.”
(They have shaken hands, and now stand
silently facing each other. Then Mrs. Andrew
moves uncertainly towards Mrs. Jem Cameron, a
guest near the door. She kisses her. They say,
“Well, Ethel!” and “Well, May!” ‘Mrs.
Andrew then kisses the next guest, Mrs. Henry
Macintosh, who kisses her, and says “How are
you?” as she says “How are you?” Then she
kisses and is kissed by Mrs. Jack Macintosh, and
Mary Macintosh, and Mrs. Dick Macintosh, and
Mrs. Peter Cameron, and shakes hands with
Peter and Jem and Sam Cameron, and Jack and
Dick and Henry Macintosh, and seats herself on
the edge of the green sofa, and sinks into the
intense silence of the arrived guests.)
Mrs. John Macintosh (mother of all the Macin-
toshes) : “Well, Sara ! (kiss) I see you’ve got on
your best dress, my dear. Is it true that the
Lancy Mortons are coming ? I’ve only got on my
old grey poplin. Mrs. Jack told me in the bed-
room, just now, that you were expecting them. Is
my cap on straight, Sara ? ”
Mrs. Sam (who was a Miss John Macintosh) :
“ I only asked them yesterday, mother, and I
hadn’t time to let you know. Sam made me ask
them, but I didn’t think they’d come.”
Mrs. John Macintosh : “ And they’re coming
after only getting asked yesterday ! ” She moves
away, and goes the round of kissing Mrs. Jack
and Dick, and Henry Macintosh, and Mrs. Jem
and Peter Cameron, with pre-ocoupied eyes and a
proud heart, because the grand Lancy Mortons
have come to her daughter’s evening at one day’s
invitation.
* * * *
Mrs. Lancy Morton and Mrs. Sam Cameron.
Mrs. Lancy Morton (a tall, peevish-looking,
fair woman in green, with a fringe net, and a cloak
with a high collar): “ How do you do, Mrs.
Cameron. Will you excuse me if I keep my cloak
on ? I have a cold. Lancy is changing his shoes
for the dancing, and will follow me in a minute.”
Mrs. Sam : “ Dancing! ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “I understood there was
to be dancing.”
Mrs. Sam : “ But we haven’t got a floor ! ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton (who is reported by herself
to have been a Sydney belle): “I certainly under-
stood that it was dancing. Lancy told me so
himself, and asked me if I would play my waltz.”
Mrs. Sam : “I am very sorry. If we had a
floor —but there isn’t any floor. We haven’t got
one. ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “If you have dancing,
your guests are always entertained. I find it the
simplest form of entertainment. ‘ Evenings ’ are
so slow without dancing, I think, unless you have
the very best music. And, of course, you can’t
have that in Yarakagibee.”
Mrs. Sam : “Down at mother’s they have a
splendid floor.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “In the season I used to
dance nine hours a night at the Sydney dances —I
used to get twenty invitations in one week.”
Mrs. Sam : “ Fancy ! ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “People in Sydney never
think of asking you without giving a dance for
you. I know they always did for me.”
Mrs. Sam : “ I wish we had a floor. ” (She begins
to wish, too, that she had never asked Mrs. Lancy
Morton to this slow evening of hers.)
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Are the Coolloobooloo
Joneses coming to-night?”
Mrs. Sam (nervously) : “ No, they’re not.
They’re not coming.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton (very much surprised) :
“ Aren't they? Oh! I thought they would be
sure to be here. Lancy said they would. Are the
Wurragurra Smiths coming ? ”
Mrs. Sam (reddening painfully) : “No, they’re
not coming to-night.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton (intensely surprised) :
“Not? Oh, fancy I I thought they’d be sure to
be. I wanted to see Ernestine Smith, too. That
was really what I—l thought she’d be sure to be
here.”
Mrs. Sam : “ Here comes Mr. Murphy from
Jimmibong.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton: “Is that the Mr. Murphy
who sells wine ? ”
Mrs. Sam: “He owns the Jimmibong vine-
yards. ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Fancy ! He’s always
trying to get Lancy to buy wine from him. Fancy
him being here. Do you know him well? ”
Mrs. Sam (miserably): “ He doesn’t make the
wine himself. Everybody likes him, I think.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “I have never met him
at anyone’s house before.” (She moves away,
and is seized by Mrs. John Macintosh, the mother
of all the Macintoshes.)
* * * *
Mrs. Dick Macintosh and Mrs. Sam Cameron.
Mrs. Dick Macintosh (whispering): “ I see
you’ve got Mrs. Lancy Morton here, Sara. Is
she nice ? ”
Mrs. Sam : “ Oh, she’s so nice, Lily. You
wouldn’t believe how nice she is. I have just
been talking to her for a long time. ”
Mrs. Dick : “ She knows how to dress, doesn’t
she, Sara?”
Mrs. Sam : “Well, her husband is dull enough.
Sam says his father bought the whole of Coollalic
Station outright, and made it over to him. It’s
worth seven hundred thousand, Sam says. Mrs.
Morton used to get twenty invitations a week in
Sydney.”
Mrs. Dick : ‘ ‘ Did she really ! Who told you
so ? ”
Mrs. Sam : “ She told me so herself.”
Mrs. Dick : “ Did she really ? Was she talking
to you for long ? ”
Mrs. Sam: “Yes; we’ve been having quite a
long chat. Oh, I like her so much. I think she’s
such a nice person, Lily. Doesn’t she do her hair
beautifully ? I must get her to show me how she
does it. Here comes Mr. Benedict, the new
curate.”
* * * *
The Curate and Mrs. Cameron.
Curate : “ I am glad the weather favors you,
Mrs. Cameron.”
Mrs. Sam (who has heard how clever the Curate
is): “ The moon is looking lovely toni-eght. ”
Curate: “Wonderful what an effect moonlight
has on scenery. I walked here to-night through
the bush, and my only regret was that I could not
read Tennyson by moonlight.”
Mrs. Sam : “ Have you read Tennyson’s * In a
Garden ’ ? I like it so much.”
Curate: “In a Garden? In a Garden? Do
you mean Maud ? ”
Mrs. Sam : “I mean ‘ln a Garden,’ by Tenny-
son. It’s very sad.”
Curate : “ In a Garden. In a Garden ! But I
don’t know anything of that name by Tennyson.
I know him from cover to cover, too. How does
it begin ? ”
Mrs. Sam : “ Oh, it’s about a man and his wife.”
Curate : “In a Garden ; In a Garden. By
Tennyson. A man and his wife. Don’t know it
at all.
Mrs. Sam : “ They’re married, and he goes away
to sea.”
Curate : “No, I can’t have read it. It must be
something published since his death. Is it long
since you read it ? ”
Mrs. Sam: “I was talking about it to Mr.
Arthurs, the clergyman at Goodonoo. He said he
liked it so much ” (She would turn the con-
versation now if she could).
Curate : “ Can you tell me anything about it ? ”
Mrs. Sam (desperately): “Hegoes away to sea,
and when he comes back she is married to another
man.”
Curate (light breaking on him suddenly) :
“Why you must mean” —(he stops suddenly,
then he mumbles to himself—“ In a Garden ,
Enoch Arden. In a Garden, Enoch Arden," and
gets out on to the verandah. Years hence, when
e gives his famous lecture, “Never Judge by
Hearsay,” this will be the funniest story in the
lecture).
* * * *
Mrs. John Macintosh and Mrs. Lancy Morton.
Mrs. John Macintosh : “ Well, Mrs. Morton,
and how do you think you’re going to like Yara-
kagibee ? ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ I have never in my life
known such a slow place.”
Mrs. J.M. : “ Slow ! Now, do you think it’s
slow ? Oh, not in the season, when the show, and
the races, and the balls come on. It’s very lively
then, Mrs. Morton.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Do they have balls ? ”
Mrs. J.M. : “ Yes, they have a race-ball in my
son Richard’s woolshed, and the show-ball is
generally in my son Henry’s big barn.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Heavens ! a race-ball
in a woolshed ! Do they have the races in Mrs.
Cameron’s back yard ? ”
Mrs. J. M.: “Which Mrs. Cameron, my dear ? ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton: “Which? Goodness
knows ! How many are there ? ”
Mrs. J. M.: “ Well, there’s Sara —Mrs. Sam—
and Ethel-Mrs. Jem—and Clara —Mrs. Peter
—and then Mrs. James Cameron— Sam’s and
Jem’s and Peter’s mother. That’s four Mrs.
Camerons.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton (ignorant of the relation-
ships) : “Well, if they’re all as slow as this one
—I never went to such an evening. She doesn’t
introduce. Doesn’t get up any music, or singing.
And there all the men sit along one side of the
wall, and all the women along another, and no-
body attempts to say a word. I don’t know why
Lancy made me come, unless he thought it was
good for business.”
Mrs. John Macintosh : “Dear me! I think
they ought to be having some game soon. Sara
is very good at games.”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Who is Sara? ”
Mrs. John Macintosh : “ She is my daughter,
Mrs. Sam Cameron. I’ll go and ask her about the
games.”
* * * *
Mrs. Lancy Morton and her Husband.
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Come home, quick.
They’re going to play games. Come home, quick!"
Lancy Morton: “Well, they won’t hurt us.
Will they play kiss-in-the-ring ? ”
Mrs. Lancy Morton : “ Kiss-in-the-ring ! My
dear boy ! Do you know that everyone of these
women are related, and all the men, too. And
the men are all married to the women, and their
mothers are here, and the women are all sisters or
sisters-in-law, and all the men are brothers. Ive
had toothache all the evening. You never told
me that they were all one family. Ive ruined
your prospects here for ever. And now you want
to play kiss-in-the-ring. Why, there’d be battle
and murder in five minutes. You’d be torn to
pieces by Jem and Sam and Dick and Peter
and Henry, for you’d have to kiss Mrs. Jem, or
Mrs. Sam, or Mrs. Dick, or a wife of one of
them.”
Lanoy Morton : “At that rate, we had better
go home.”
* * * *
Later.— Mrs. Andrew Macintosh and Mrs.
Peter Cameron.
Mrs. Andrew : “ Sara is such a good hand at
giving an evening. She can talk to anyone at all.
Mrs. Peter : “ Oh, she’s very good ; she always
was. She knows how to do the thing properly.
The Lancy Mortons said ‘ Thank you for a very
pleasant evening’ when they were going away.”
LOUISE MACK.

There is to be a fearsome rO-union of Syd-
ney Scotchmen to commemorate the fact that
Bobbie Burns died 100 years ago—2lst July, 1796.
At first blush it seems strange that the poet’s ad-
mirers should rejoice over barley-bree and sheep’s-
entrails on such an anniversary of gloom; but
anyone who has seen haggis at a vast distance will
realise the aptness of the tribute. There is
nothing in the wide world so funereal as haggis.
To this paper, one of the humorous fea-
tures of the fist-fight between Crick and Meagher,
in a Sydney hotel-bar the other day, is the fact
that Meagher attributes some of his bad blood to
a Bulletin article in which, it is suggested, Crick
had a finger. The Bulletin doesn’t want to spoil
sport, but it assures Dean’s solicitor that the
article presumably referred to was an unsuggested
expression of the honest opinions of a working-
journalist who doesn’t know Crick from a crow.
At length Melbourne City Council has
adopted the minimum wage, or, at least, three
minimums—one for boys under 18, one for men
under 55, and another for men over the latter age.
This arrangement provides for the “ pore old
man ” for whom councillors have always expressed
so much concern, and leaves the rascally con-
tractor all the loop-holes he could wish for where-
by to get his men at boys’ wages or pay them as
victims of old age and general debility. It will
be found that the corporation laborer of the future
will never be older than 18 till he suddenly
becomes 55.
Set a cableliar to catch a cableliar. The
Age lately bowled out the Argus manufacturing
fiscal facts in the fablegrams to make comment
for the leading articles. The Age forgets its own
doctoring of the cricket-news, so as to tone down
Australian defeats in London, etc., etc. The fact
is that too much prominence altogether is given
to the imaginative expansion of cabled hints from
London, and the member of the cable syndicate
that takes special pains to separate the inferences
from the facts, will do a great deal to abolish the
what-will-they-say-in-England feeling that dis-
courages some alleged Australians from the cradle
to the grave.
At Sydney School of Arts, the other day,
Gov. Hampden in a speech mainly about Austra-
lian literature :
We lived in these days in such hurry and excitement
that our every-day life, and especially the every-day life
of those actively engaged in the settlement of a land, was
not such as to provide the conditions for a high standard
of literature. The press was admirably conducted, but
he did not suppose that the most enthusiastic pressman
would say that the daily press itself was likely to raise the
standard of literature. The man who was engaged on the
press had to think more of producing facts than of the
style in which he produced them. Yet, even in that
matter, he was struck very much since coming here by
the admirable literary work in one of the weekly news-
papers. It was produced in such a way as not to strike
most readers, because it was not in the body of the paper,
but on the fly-sheet. However, he was not going to
mention names. (Laughter).
H.E.’s allusion to The Bulletin was gall and
wormwood to a lot of people, and Daily Tele-
graph was the only newspaper which had the
nerve and the grace to report this portion of the
Governor’s remarks.
Be the Colonial Bank and Ward Farmers’
Association muddle, the Seddon Government has
been moved to appoint a Parliamentary committee
to inquire into the amalgamation of the. Colonial
and B.N.Z.—with a view to proving that it was
not done to help Treas. Ward out of his difficulties
or for any other corrupt motive. The first com-
mittee proposed consisted of Premier Seddon,
Minister M'Kenzie (to inquire into their own con-
duct), three Government supporters and three
Tories—altogether, five Ministerialists to three
Oppositionists. The Opposition refused to take a
hand unless the numbers were equal. Then the
matter was referred to the House, and as the
Opposition finally refused to take a hand at all,
the committee is to be all Ministerialists. Con-
sidering that the archangel Gabriel couldn’t make
the report of such a committee look absolutely
unbiased, it would have been a great deal better
to leave the politicians out of the business alto-
gether and let three judges tackle the matter.
Another way would have been to simply say that,
up to date, there was nothing to disprove, and the
Government didn’t require whitewashing, and
have no committee at all. As matters are the
Government is in a cul-de-sac. The Opposition
refused to be represented, and yet it will point out
for ever afterwards that because it wasn’t repre-
sented the whole business was a farce. Just here
news comes along that the M.L. Council has
appointed a committee of its own, which is prac-
tically all Opposition, so the fact that the matter
should have been severely left alone sticks out
more and more.

In reply to The Bulletin’s queries re
Sydney civil ambulance and transport brigade,
Herbert J. Page writes that the Govt, grants
premises and some ambulance appliances.
The brigade is supported by voluntary contribu-
tions. Nearly 200 cases have been dealt with
since commencing operations, and the brigade is
ready night or day to render first aid to the in-
jured and transport to the hospital if necessary ;
no charge being madeffor services. The premises
and records are open to inspection.
Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C. (S.A.), has pitched
a cunningly-fashioned stumbling-block into the
pathway of Chief-Justice Way’s possibility of
Privy Councillorship ( versus Windeyer). Said
Ebenezer in effect: “If Way is appointed to the
judicial bench of the P.C., Kingston will take the
S.A. Chief Justiceship, and just fancy Charley
ex-footballer, ex-full-private, ex-would-be-
duellist, &c., &c., Chief-Justice ! ” It is quite
possible that Kingston, having been a man of
warm blood and having graduated feelingly
through a few very human follies, would make a
first-class, common-sense Chief Justice; but, all
the same, Ebenezer’s presentment is artfully
painted and baited.
A know-nothing male having introduced
into M.L. Parliament a Bill to give servant girls
a weekly half-holiday, Lady Stout (the lady who
publicly believes in Chinese immigration) brought
down this resolution to a more or less Tory
meeting :
That the members of the Southern Cross Society wish to
draw the attention of Mr. Kelly to the urgent necessity of
providing a half-holiday for mothers of families who have
no servants, and who never get a rest night or day, and
would suggest that a Bill which provided" for this urgent
need, by compelling the husbands to remain at home and
mind the house and children while their wives take an
airing, would be hailed as a boon by many hard-working
and deserving women, (Laughter.)
The new Hotel Cecil, London, which has
by now opened its doors, is only exceeded in size
by one other in the world— viz. , the Palace at San
Francisco. There are 700 bedrooms, and 3CO
sitting-rooms. The decorations of all the apart-
ments are sumptuous. The place is being run in
the first instance by the liquidator in Hobbs’
estate, but no doubt it will presently pass into the
hands of one or other of the big hotel go’s. The
manager is Bertini,so well-known at the Criterion,
who has had world-wide experience) and the chej
is the famous Antoine Coaste.
One of the first results of Chief Justice
Madden’s decision that the police are not to con-
duct or assist in private cases in the courts of
petty sessions appeared, the other day, at South
Melbourne. The complainant was a boy, aged 10,
with a badly-damaged skull, who charged two
persons with assault. He had no means to hire a
barrister, while defendants had. Therefore the
case resolved itself into a legal conflict between a
barrister, on one side, and a 10-year-old boy, with
a broken head, conducting his own case, on the
other, and the rush of legal technicalities swept
the small plaintiff off his feet before the case was
fairly commenced. He had no brief in his hand,
no wig : he hadn’t learnt the art of glaring at the
other party, and asking questions in a deep, fierce
voice that came right from his boots ; and he was
promptly badgered out of court.
At Colling wood, the other day, a swag-
bearing, begging vagrant was found to have in his
“ drum ” seven boxes of matches, 3 yds. black
pudding, 7 loaves, several dozen paper “screws
of tea and sugar, a lump of butter without a
wrapper, an elastic-side boot, a piece of mouldy
cheese, a stale cabbage, a cauliflower, a skinned
rabbit, some beef-steak, a pair of trousers, and
several shirts, socks and handkerchiefs. Evi-
dently he was going to make Irish stew, of the
ordinary boarding-house sort, when the police
interrupted him. There were 20 previous con-
victions. Six months’hard.
The United States, after a trial of a,
slightly reduced tariff, are evidently going back to
the most aggravated kind of protection. It is
fully evident that the Republican party will win
the Presidential election, and what it means to do
with its victory is sufficiently shown by the nomi-
nation, by a stupendous majority, of M‘Kinley,
the most advanced Protectionist in American
politics, as the party’s candidate for president.

wasn’t coming in U.S., now drops the painful
subject, and waded in the other day to show that
the overthrow of the old Macdonald party in
Canada means that Freetrade is Actively coming
along there, and must triumph all over the world
sooner or later. Hope deferred, &c.

A Bill to authorise women to sit in Parlia-
ment, or to hold any public office or position what-
soever, is before Maoriland Legislature. It may
not pass just yet, but it is bound to come sooner
or later. The woman politician is the natural
