Hancock’s protest against the sale of metal
broken in Melb. Gaol is not much called for. The
prison output for the past six months only
amounted to £I3G, and “honest labor” would be
none the better off if the metal were supplied to
municipalities gratis. The question is, shall pri-
soners put in a little useful work? If so, some-
thing must be done with the result.
S.M. Herald considers Parliament should
invent a means of suppressing or moderating the
present method of questioning Ministers in Parlia-
ment. As the revelations re the professional
charges in the Dean Commission were squeezed
from Ministers by the pertinacious questioning
the present practice allows, the Herald’s sud-
denly-displayed desire for a change admits of
malicious interpretation.
Premier Reid (proudly) : ‘ ‘ The whole ex-
penditure out of the Treasurer’s advance account
for 11 months of the year was only £10,000.” The
figures show that the expenditure for 12 months
was £26,000, so Mr. Reid speaks only of 11
months. This quaint way of never telling the
whole truth is a political characteristic of G.
Reid. The reduction to £26,000 is something to
be proud of, but even when he is in the right
G.R. can’t tell the whole truth about it.
The Vic. Government scheme for State
relief to the poor—in other words, for establishing
the British Poor Rate in Australia —knocks one
great Australian legend on the head. Up to date
Australia has tried to believe that there is no per-
manent poverty in the country—only temporary
want of employment ; and the State has been
giving temporary relief for 40 years, and charging
it mostly to loan-funds, and professing to believe
that everything will be right next year. _ There
have been relief works, and private subscriptions,
and a “ charitable vote ” almost as long as any-
body can remember, but any formal recognition
of permanent poverty has been carefully avoided.
Now it is coming, and it is just as well that it
should, so that the Australian may understand
where his country has got to and not keep his
head buried in the sand any longer.

Gov. Hampden, when in Melbourne last
week, accepted a seat on the floor of the Leg.
Assembly one business night. The last distin-
guished N.S.W. man who accepted that honor
was Ninian Melville in the days of his Chairman-
ship of Committees. The late Parkes was invited
to the floor on his last flying trip, but he said
“ No ” with such emphasis that members thought
of repudiating all future overtures towards Feder-
ation.
The situation of the unhappy Mrs. Kil-
patrick’s case against Huddart Parker and Co. up
to date is something like this: (1) There were the
usual preliminary proceedings before the first
trial, and the necessary costs; (2) there was the
first trial and the necessary costs ; (3) application
by defendants for second trial, and third batch of
costs; (4) second trial, and fourth batch of costs ;
(5) application by defendants to Full Court for
third trial (fifth batch of costs); (6) application by
defendants to Full Bench of Judges for third trial
(sixth batch of costs); (7) application by defend-
ants for leave (granted) to appeal to Privy Council
(seventh batch of costs) ; (8) appeal, which will
presently ensue, to Privy Council, and eighth
batch of costs. What a lot of law there is m
Australia for the rich litigant!
N S.W. Postmaster-General Cook wants
to shunt the unemployed and the victims of the
sweater upon “the Churches.” Before the
saintlv Joseph was a “God-fearing Minister
and a haltered Reidite, he was a Labor-leader
and a lay-preacher among the Lithgow miners,
on their unionism and his reputation for Godliness.
Then oh ' then, he wanted to dump the unem-
ployed and the “ sweated ” on to the Legislature.
That gained him a passport into the Legislature,
and into the high places of profit and power ; and
now oh ! now, he wants to dump his marketing
stock elsewhere—on to the Churches, on to any-
thing anywhere, so long as it isn’t the Legislature
that shelters Joseph.

That is to argue that the Almighty is a Free-
trader ! Which can hardly be thrust down the
throat of a people that calls itself a Christian com-
munity, and brags that it borrowed its Divinity
from the Hebrews ; for wasn’t the God of Abra-
ham, Isaac and Jacob distinguished for His par-
tiality to a “peculiar people,” and didn’t He
variously “protect” that race, besides specially
legislating for the shipbuilding industry in the
time of Noah? It is late in the day, and a reversal
of orthodoxy, to claim God Almighty as a
Foreign-trader.

Ex-Treas. Ward’s speech in his own defence
in M.L. Parliament was to have been circulated
in pamphlet form, but fear of libel-law came
in. A speech published in Hansard is privileged,
but if reprinted as an extract the privilege dis-
appears. Ward got over the difficulty by giving
a special order for several thousand extra copies of
Hansard, which were duly supplied at cost-price
by an obliging Government. And of such is the
kingdom of politics.
Premier Reid in his financial statement :
From 1871 to 1891 the total trade of Victoria increased
by £10,800,000, or 40 per cent. During the same period
the total trade of New South Wales increased _ by
£30,500,000, or 146 per cent., much more than three times
the Victorian increase.
The inference is obvious and the figures are abso-
lutely true. But what a different impression
could be produced by selecting some other equally
true figures from the same table ! For instance :
“From 1891 to 1893 the total trade of N.S.W.
decreased £10,300,000, while that of Victoria only
decreased £9,100,000. In 1894 the trade of N.S.W.
decreased £5,000,000, while that of Victoria re-
mained stationary.” The beauty of figures depends
mainly on careful selection. G. Reid's shipping
figures are cooked much the same way. “In Vic-
toria,” he says, “the mail steamers are counted
four times, while in N.S.W. they are only counted
once.” The fact that a steamer is reckoned twice
at every port —inwards and outwards—doesn’t
enter into the Premier’s commercial knowledge.
The mail steamers are reckoned four times at
Melbourne and twice at Sydney, just as interpro-
vincial boats, which go no further than Melbourne,
are reckoned twice there and four times at
Sydney.

Dr. Talmag-e’s Latest.
A recent quotation from this eloquent, earnest
is worthy perusal and reflection. He
says : “ Almost everybody has some defective
spot in his physical constitution; a dull ear or a
dim eye, or a rheumatic foot, or a neuralgia brow,
or a twisted muscle, or a weak side, or an inflamed
tonsil, or some point at which the east wind or a
season of overwork assaults him.” “ I was suffer-
ing,” writes Mr. H. Glenister, a prominent
George-street, Sydney business man, “ from acute
disease of the kidneys, followed by dropsy, and
was under no less than seven doctors for two years,
being in bed throughout this time. They each
pronounced my case hopeless, and I was then ad-
vised to try Warner’s Safe Cure as a last resource,
which I did, together with Warner’s Safe Pills,
and after a few bottles I received such wonderful
benefit as to be able to resume business. My case
is a well-known one in Sydney, and all my friends
consider the result simply miraculous. It is with
feelings of sincere gratitude that I write saying
what wonderful relief Warner’s Safe Cure has
given me.”*

An Acting-Judge, whose sentences are
characterised by marked severity, has, an obser-
vant barrister lately pointed out, nine chances to
one for a permanent appointment on any Bench in
Australia over the Acting-Judge who tempers
justice with mercy.
A Melbourne bibliophile has a copy of
Browning’s “Bordello,” first edition, inscribed,
“to his dear friend, R. H. Horne, from R. 8.”
He gave fourpence for this treasure, which he
found in a local bookseller’s cheap box some years
Bince. It would fetch pounds now.
Ex-attorney Meagher, it is announced, is
going to lecture on crime and criminals, including
Archer, Lee and Cummins, Williams and Mont-
gomery, and others. The humor of the thing is
that the voluble attorney more or less defended
them all, and they were all carefully hanged.
Like most old sea-dogs of high position,
Vice-Admiral Tryon, a conspicuous figure in
Sydney a few years ago and later on tragically
drowned, has left a pious memory behind, and the
other day the church of his native Northampton
unveiled a monument to commemorate his virtues.
Attorney John Madden, Chief Justice
Madden’s father, estimates that, in half a cen-
tury’s hunting, he has only been down two or
three hundred times. George Watson more. Yet
within about a twelvemonth young Mrs. Highett,
young Bygate, young Manifold, and young
M'Murtrie have been slain.
King O’Malley, the Yankee insurance
drummer and M.P., is one of the new oratori-
cal terrors of S.A. Parliament. O’Malley, an
alleged democrat, says he “ will never take his
foot off the necks of the gilded spurred roosters
while they fatten on the poor man’s corn crib.”
He was once editor and proprietor of the Grey
Gulch (Cal.) Fighter.

committed for trial, was raked up by an ex-oon-
stable, discharged from the service at the instiga-
tion of Kirby a few years ago. Mr. Justice John-
ston reckoned Kirby the smartest man in the
Force it was he who so adroitly investigated the
ease of Hall, the Timaru poisoner, in ’B6.
A very, very gilded Australian lady, who
is pestered out of her life by all manner of charity-
seekers and fortune-hunters (same thing), knows
how to give a straight hint on occasion—as a curly-
headed society youth lately discovered. At a
recent big ball, his attentions had grown painful
both to onlookers and recipient. “It is most kind
of you to attend on me like this, Mr. ,” said
Miss at last, “ but I’m sure nothing will
come of it.”
General Hutton, when in command of the
N.S.W. forces, was never “stuck” for an oath,
but as he publicly cursed only the officers (he used
to apologise to them in private) he was not un-
popular with the rank-and-file. Colonel Tom
Price, in command of the Vic. Mounted Rifles, is
alleged to curse both officers and pri vates on
parade, but he struck a snag at Hamilton, Vic.,
the other day. The local Spectator alludes to his
language in blistering terms.

Sydney, had to preach at Brisbane the other Sun-

A MARRIED MASH.
"That tall gentleman was so attentive a few minutes ago, but he won't look at me now”
"Yes! that's my husband —he must have seen me come in!”
Novelist B. L. Farjeon, in his novel “ The
House of White Shadows,” published 11 years
ago, in a measure anticipated the Dean-Meagher
embroglio. The plot deals with an advocate who
takes up the defence of an alleged murderer and
secures his acquittal. Immediately afterwards
the accused confesses his guilt to the advocate
who decides to keep the secret, with the result
that he and the murderer “go under,” but in a
more dramatic fashion than “Possum ” Dean and
Meagher.
Ex-Governor Read, of Darlinghurst Gaol,
once had sent to him on approval a warder with
such a lazy style that Read sene a warder proper
to bring a chair. The chair having arrived he
calmly told the man to be seated, as chairs were
always provided for the weary. The tired warder
innocently accepted the seat, much to the
amusement of gaolers and gaoled, but next day
he left the service for ever.
Though it was denied, on publication in
London Daily Gourier, that Prince Persimmon
had benefited to the amount of a million by
Baron Hirsch’s death, the statement was never-
theless true (writes a London correspondent to
The Bulletin). Two-fifths were hard cash, and
the remainder cancelled obligations. The Duke
°f York also reaped a share of the harvest repre-
senting at least half a million.
J. T. Hogan—who, as “ our London cor-
respondent, is allowed to tell the tale of his own
deeds in -Mel b. Herald —relates what he terms a
funny story in which he figures. When the Colo-
nial Marriage Bill was before the Commons, it
was opposed by Lord Hugh Cecil, and several
members, not having read it, asked what it was
all about. “ Oh,” said a member, “it is a Bill to
enable Hogan to have a wife in every colony.”
A much-mentioned and recently-retrenched
N.b.W. civil-servant some years ago was addicted
to looking upon the rum when it was red, and at
one hotel made himself such a nuisance that at
last the landlord told him he would be served
there no more. He disappeared for half an hour
and re-appeared in the bar dripping wet : he had
gone upstairs and taken a bath with all his cloth-
ing on, boots and hat included. He called for a
dr. llk, and, holding the glass up, said : “This is
the last drink I’m going to have,” and, up to date,
he has kept his word.
31elb. : “ Nick Fitzgerald, M.L.A., had an
oratoriego-i n the other night at the Humane Society,
sandwiched between Govs. Hampden and Brassey
and blazing them both out into darkness visible
with his bit of fireworkery. Nicholas always
reminds one of the kind of horse-races known as
the Shorts, Hurry-Scurry, and Helter-Skelter.
He bolts off at a terrific bat, with a glorious
sludge-flow not unlike eloquence, but cracks up
all at once, as a rocket bursting in blue fires or
a golden shower. All is portico, no edifice, and
he ends just when you expect him to begin.”
Poor old Jawbone Neild appeared on the
platform again the other night in Sydney and
raged furiously for over an hour—mostly about
the people who didn’t appreciate his ' alleged
poems. The general burden of his lament was
that he treated these people with silent contempt
After one terrific storm and bang-crash of denun-'
ciation, however, he mentioned that his critics
were vipers who wrote falsehoods for bread ”
which is a beautiful figure of speech, anyhow.
But it goes to show that Neild doesn’t understand
the press. That kind of viper writes falsehoods
for whisky, and finds the bread on the free-lunch
counter.

Policeman Rogers, of Daylesford, has the
thankless, nerve-splitting job of fetching home
absconder lawyer Geake. He must take him from
South America to North America, London and
India on the road.
N.S.W. Ex-Sheriff Cowper’s ebullient as-
sault on the majesty of his political surroundings
before his chair of office had actually grown cold
shows the thinness of the Civil Service crust under
which many public-spirited men pass seemingly
contented lives. But, ebullient as Cowper now is
he manifestly possessed more horse-sense than
Sydneys first Sheriff, John M'Kaness, who in
less than two years after his appointment, under-
took to play the part of independent public man,
and was incontinently cashiered. Cowper has
drawn official salary and successfully held his
tongue for 27 consecutive years, during which the
country must have been going to the Devil quite
as fast as it is going now.
A REVIVAL .
This gentleman informs us that his business
is LOOKING UP.
“Find the woman in the case.’' Cecil
Rhodes s latest freaks, especially the Jameson
raid, are now attributed to the desperation of a
disappointed man. Less than a year ago Rhodes
offered his hand and wealth to the still-
beautiful Dowager Countess of Dudley (sister
of the Prince of Wales’s friend, the historical
Lady Mordaunt, and mother of. young Lord
Dudley, who visited Australia in the Car-
mgton days). He was rejected, and since
his rebuff the “uncrowned king ” has become ten-
fold more reserved and arrogant than before, and
has disclosed a capacity for blundering equalled by
few. His erstwhile friends predict, as a result of
his present “ tight place,” either a tragic termina-
tion to his life, or the initial steps towards the
United States of South Africa, with himself as
President.
Brighton (IMelb.) proposes to make atone-
ment for its old-time rejection of Higinbotham in
favor of dear Tom Bent, by starting a monument
to the memory of the late Chief Justice—
When Higinbotham shone on earth
The Brighton voters scorned his worth
(May Heaven be their pardoner !)
And “chucked ’im” for a candidate
Who served their suburb, not the State-
Tom Bent, a market gardener.
But now that Higinbotham’s dead
They say to him they chose instead—
“ Our blessing isn’t on you, Bent.”
Disdainful of that bluffer bold,
They deem the man he beat of old
Deserving of a monument.
Set up a marble statue white,
Upon its base this record write—
“ From Senate we ejected him,
’Midst brutal shouts of rude applause,
And bade him stop away because
We honored and rejected him.”
Higinbotham’s monument is builded. Brighton
had better attend to the wants of Tom Bent:
A monument the chieftain owns;
None fairer mankind knows—
*Tis builded strongly of the stones
Thrown at him by his foes.

EXPERTS IN FOOT WEAR.
July Cosmos appears with Alex. Mont-
gomery as editor. Cosmos has undergone such
remarkable vicissitudes lately that ’tis wonder it
survived at all, and the new number is handi-
capped way behind scratch. Allowing for the
handicap, COSMOS begins to show something of the
potentialities which lurk in an Australian Maga-
zine. D. J. Quinn crystallises some good impres-
sions of the N.S.W. bar ; the editor chats agree-
ably with Dion Boueicault; P. J. Holdsworth is
represented by some sonorous verse, and Ethel
Turner by a sad little child-story; and Louise
Mack continues her serial. There are sundry ex-
cellent portraits and illustrations.
A Sydney writer speculates whether
the sheriff, 'if he actually hanged felons
with his own hands, would be shunned by his
friends and neighbors. “ I think not,” is the con-
clusion. The Bulletin maintains that the
sheriff, if he personally acted as hangman, would
have the social status of a hangman. _ The
Bulletin has seen the sheriff waltzing with a
lady at a public ball. Where is the woman who
would have her waist encircled publicly by a man
who had been cold-bloodedly choking a woman for
a fee ! Let the hangman be set down in his own
loathsome place. Even now the office of sheriff
finds scant respect in many quarters. There is a
legend about a sheriff who, while on backblock
circuit, found that a room in the court-house was
being used by a roads-and-bridges engineer as a
temporary office. ‘ Tell that ah ! roads man
that he’ll have to find an office elsewhere,
said the sheriff to the tipstaff. Whereupon tho
engineer, overhearing, called out:' “You tell that
ah ! damned hangman to go to blazes. ”

The mine consists of six blocks of 12 acres each (72
Acres in all), upon which a considerable amount of de-
velopment work has been done with the most satis-
factory results. Full particulars of which see abridged
report by Mr. R. T. Evans, F.G.S.A., &c., who was sent
to Western Australia by the directors to specially inspect
and report on the property.
The capital of the company consists of 30,000 shares of
£1 each ; of these 18,750 are fully paid up, 6250 paid up
to 10s., and the 5000 reserved shares are now offered to
the public at 2s. on application, 2s. on allotment, and
thereafter Is. per share payable on Ist every month, com-
mencing Ist September, 1896, and monthly thereafter
until paid up to 10s., when they will rank alike with the
6250 shares already paid up to 10s.
The whole of the proceeds from the sale of these shares
will be placed to credit of the company, less brokerage
and expenses only.
ABRIDGED REPORT.
Melbourne, 12th June, 1896.
The Directors Bonnie Doon Gold-mining Company.
Gentlemen, —
Acting in accordance with your instructions, I duly pro'
ceeded to your property, known as the Bonnie Doon
Gold-mining Company, 'and after a lengthy visit in
thoroughly examining and testing all the known reefs
and veins opened up to present, have now the honor to
lay before you the following detailed report
ACREAGE and LOCALITY.
The property is situated at Siberia, about 65 miles
north-east of Coolgardie, and consists of six 12-acre
blocks—72 acres in all —numbered respectively 615, 2407,
2468 (formerly 418), 2595, 2593; and 2594. Block 615, upon
which the main workings are located, immediately adjoins
on the south-east the once famous Christie’s Reward
claim, from which a vast amount of alluvial gold and
dollying stone was obtained in the early history of the
field.
MEANS of COMMUNICATION.
The main Siberia road runs directly through the eastern
portion of the property. It is in a good state of repair,
and affords excellent means of communication with and
transport of material from Coolgardie. A large number of
teamsters are at present availing themselves "of this route
to Menzies in preference to proceeding via the 90-Mile. On
the completion of the Coolgardie-Menzies line you will be
within 17 miles of the 45 siding, so no difficulty as far as
transport of machinery and material is concerned will
ever be experienced.
LODES AND THEIR FEATURES.
So far shafts and costeens of various depths have been
opened up on 10 makes of stone, but as all of these are
not of a continuous character, some being irregular lenti-
cular veins with no proof positive of downward continuity
and others only “ break heaves ” from other v ins, I shail
therefore deal first with those I consider the main fissure
veins, viz. Nos. 7 (allied with 6), No. 8, and the water
shaft formation.
No. 6. —No. 6 is the reef or vein from which some fabu-
lously rich stone was taken some time ago. It has a west-
north-west by east-south-east strike, or within a few
points of east and west. It has an underlie north with a
dip to the east. A vertical shaft has been sunk to a dis-
tance 65ft., passing through the golden stone on its dip
east at 15ft. Here a small cut was put in, and some
excellent ore obtained, namely, that exhibited in Mel-
bourne a few months ago. The stone at this point is
about 15in. thick, and still carries fair gold, but the shoot
is fast dipping away east. The quartz appears to have
laminated bands of vesicular ferruginous stone running
through it at intervals, and whenever this occurs good
coarse specimen gold is obtained. The stone will vary a
great deal in its gold-bearing proclivities, as well as in its
conformation and regularity. In the various pan tests I
made I was particularly careful in eschewing any material
that had a tendency to specimen-stone. The results ob-
tained gave an average return of 2oz. to the ton, with loz.
sdwt. per ton from the rubble schist alongside the hang-
ing wall. There is thus about 2ft. 6in. of payable crushing
material. The footwall is composed of grey felspathic
material intermixed with soft diorite, the hanging wall of
grey and brown diorite. After testing the formation at
the 15ft. level the shaft was continued down to its present
depth, 65ft., connection being made at 40ft. with No. 7
underlie shaft a few feet north-east therefrom. I shall
treat further on this reef after describing No. 7, and ‘the
views that I hold with regard to its probable action at
point of dip contact with the other body (7).
No. 7. —This reef is one of the three permanent fissure
bodies on your property, and was first opened up in a
trench about 40ft. north-east of No. 6. It is about 3ft.
wide, and is composed chiefly of dark ferruginous quartz.
It is commonly known as a north and south reef, but its
actual bearing or strike is north 10 degrees west. It has
an easterly underlie, with a dip to the south-east. An
underlie shaft has been carried down on the back of the
reef to a depth of about 65ft., when it crossed the No. 6
shaft at about 40ft. vertical from the surface. At about
55ft. down the underlie shaft an underlie winze was sunk
on the stone to a depth of 25ft. The reef here still main-
tains its uniform width, and is enclosed between nice-
looking soft micaceous and kaolin schists. The width of
the formation including veinstone is about 18ft. The
over and sublying rock is hard blue diorite. The average
result of stone taken from the reef representing a length
of nearly 85ft. gave the magnificent average return of
4540 z. per ton of a splendid sample of both coarse and
fine gold. The schist also carries an appreciable amount
of gold, and I would advise that your manager send a
couple cwt. for bulk treatment in Melbourne.
No. 6 (Continued).—Close to breaking through from
No. 7 to No. 6, the track of the latter reef is seen under-
lying directly on to the back of No. 7. This traek is
about 2ft. wide, the walls being filled in with kaolin
schist, and diorite with a thin vein of gold-bearing stone
on the footwall. This is only the tail end of the stone
west, the main shoot having dipped east as previously ex-
plained, but the question arises what will be the ultimate
fate of No. 6 on its point of dip contact with No. 7
further south-east ? That No. 7is a permanent fissure I
am assured of, therefore I can only conclude that No. 6
must eventually give way to the more masterly body, but
what its terminal point of existence may be it is im-
Prospectus.
possible to say. Itmay not be for some considerable depth,
for with each successive south-east shoots of stone coming
into point of dip contact you will have longer lengths to
work on. No. 6, therefore, in my opinion, will continue
for some considerable time to come to yield quantities of
good payable ore of given quantities at given distances
apart. If the vertical shaft was continued down to, say,
75ft. or 100 ft., and a crosscut driven north-east, it would
test both Nos. 6 and 7 at one of the points of dip contact.
I would strongly recommend this work be undertaken.
No. 8 (a) is another masterly body, and one of the main
fissures of your mine. It is distant about 200 ft. north-
east from No. 6. Its angle of bearing is about north
25deg. west, with a south-west underlie. It was first
opened up in a trench about 90ft. long, the stone being
from 14in. to 15in. thick. Towards the north end of the
trench a small blind shaft has been sunk on the stone to a
depth of 10ft., and proved good payable ore to exist. To
the south a vertical shaft has been sunk to a depth of
50ft., the reef carrying down in like manner to about 42ft.,
when the stone began to assume an underlie, and subse-
quently bifurcated or split into two branches, one branch
going off very fast to the west, the other continuing at a
more vertical angle. Sinking was continued on the hang-
ing-wall or western branch for a further Bft., and then
drives were put in approximately north-west and south-
east on the course of the vein for distances of 16ft. and
18ft. respectively. In the south-east end over the back
the stone is about 2ft. wide, and in the face 3ft. The
stone has a most pleasing appearance, is granular in tex-
ture, slightly ferruginous in composition, and appears to
lie in laminated floors. The footwall vein is coming in on
the eastern side, and in the course of another few feet
should coalesce and form a large body of stone. In the
north-west end the stone has risen over the back, indicat-
ing the dip in the other or south-east direction. The two
branches here are about sft. apart, and the diversion will
increase in this direction, so it would not be advisable to
continue the north-west end on this vein any further at
present unless to rise up for stoping purposes. The en-
closing walls throughout consist of decomposed diorite
and schist. Samples taken along each drive and face
gave the average handsome return of 3|oz. coarse and fine
gold per ton.
No. 8B is an underlie shaft, 40ft. south-east of the
former one, and is apparently sunk on an offshoot vein
from that body. It will, however, judging from appear-
ances, eventually join the other reef at a greater depth.
The shaft was sunk on the underlie to about 60ft., the
reef being 12in. thick throughout, and enclosed between
nice schistose country. The vein then took a vertical
turn and was followed down on that angle for a further
10ft., making total depth 70ft. The stone is similar in
character to BA, and samples 2oz. per ton. Samples
taken from the heaps at grass at both these shafts, repre-
senting about 20 tons, gave the highly gratifying result of
3oz. per ton.
WATER SHAFT DEVELOPMENT.
1 now come to a matter that has every probability of
developing into something exceedingly good, viz., the'for-
mation, as it is locally termed, lately passed through
in the water shaft. This shaft, which at time of
my visit was down a depth of 100 ft., was positioned
out for the purpose of hot only securing water,
but also at the same time for operating therefrom
on the majority of the reefs on their east dip and
strike. The site has been well selected for this double
purpose, and the shaft is being carefully sunk and well
timbered, and will answer all requirements for some time
to come. The country down to 37ft. consisted of very
hard diorite ; there the formation-change occurred. This
consists of 18ft. of micaceous and kaolin schists inter-
spersed with numerous veins and spurs of calcitic and
ferruginous quartz, all more or less of an auriferous
nature. The strike of this formation is north-west and
south-east, with a southerly underlie and an easterly dip.
Towards the south end of the shaft, where the formation
was passed through, a number of these veins coalesced
and formed a body of stone 3ft. wide. Underneath this
again was about 12in. of kaolin schist, and then the diorite
appeared and continued to the bottom. Numerous spurs
continued to be met with after the formation was passed
through, some underlying at a north-east angle, others of
a vertical disposition. Until the last few feet the
country rock has been broken and disjointed, but now
it has a distinct stratified plane, the strata striking
north-east and south-west with an apparent underlie
north, or, in other words, a transverse bearing and dip to
the formation. Three feet below the cap of formation
I took a small quantity of ferruginous quartz interspersed
with schist and obtained prospects equal to Bdwt. per
ton. From one of the vein spurs forming the 3ft. body I
obtained 16dwt. per ton, whilst from the intervening
micaceous and kaolin schists the splendid result of 2oz.
(of a coarse and fine sample of gold) per ton was given. I
am of the opinion that this formation has been formed by
the coalescence of No. 7 or 8 with some of the other east
and west veins. You will observe that with No. 7 a
similar class of schistose material occurs, and about the
same width. To prove whether No. 8 has any connection
you could further extend the south-east drive, and this
would be by no means an unproductive work, for you
would not only be raising high grade stone, but would also
be opening up the ore body for future stoping, as well as
proving the relation of 8B vein to it. I would push on
the water shaft with all possible speed, putting in open-
ing sets at 100 ft., and thence every 50ft. until you
had reached the fluid element, then would crosscut
south-east to cut the formation at the various levels,
and thus prove its continuity and value. I would
even advise that a crosscut be driven now at present
depth, but I should not discontinue sinking in order to do
so, as the striking of water is a very important matter to
you for future battery and stock purposes. lam much
impressed with the formation, and am of the belief that it
will eventuate into a highly payable body.
LEASE 2468.
This is the only other section comprised in your pro-
perty on which any work or discovery has been made.
Close to the south-east boundary, in a trench about 15ft.
long and 7ft. deep, a make of stone varying from a foot
to 15in., striking east and west underlying north, has
been opened up. The country is of a soft, earthy, dioritic
nature. The stone wears a favorable appearance and
carries a little gold, but it is impossible to say more about
it till further developed.
TIMBER.
Plenty of timber for firing purposes exists on the pro-
perty, and good sound mining timber in unlimited
quantities is procurable a mile away.
WATER CONSERVATION.
Irrespective of shaft supplies, a good catchment area
exists on your north and eastern boundaries, and a dam
could be constructed at a nominal cost.
BATTERY SITE.
An excellent milling site is obtainable close to the water
shaft, with a good get-away for the tailings.
CONCLUSION.
In concluding, it affords me great pleasure in stating
that you possess a most valuable property, which time,
perseverance and capital cannot fail to transform into a
profitable concern. You are on the eve, I believe, of a
valuable discovery in the water-shaft, and this, combined
with the excellent and permanent prospects of Nos. 7 and
8, and to a large extent 6 and 4, renders it a property
that you can conscientiously and confidently recommend
to the English investing public. All my estimates are
based on pan tests, and, therefore, necessarily under
rather than over estimated. I desire to record my thanks
to your mine manager (Mr. F. J. Friedman)‘for the
courtesy and assistance he rendered, and can testify to
the able and practical manner he is carrying on your
works.
SYNOPSIS of TESTS.
No. 6 shaft, 2oz. per ton ex specimens
No. 6 shaft, rubble schist from wall, loz. per ton
No. 7 shaft, 4j4oz. per ton
No. 8 shaft, 3*4oz. per ton
No. 8B shaft, 2oz. per ton
No. 8 heap (sample), 3oz. per ton
Yours obediently,
R. T. EVANS, F.G.S.A., &c.,
Mining Engineer and Metallurgist.
Melbourne, 12th June, 1896.

TO MINING COMPANIES, BATTERY OWNERS
AND OTHERS.
Increase your yields by Saving the Fine Gold
and Pyrites by the use of
Wynne and Treyartha’s
Patent Battery Plates.
This Invention, lately patented, revolutionises the ordi-
nary battery treatment of ores, for by its use copper-
plates, quicksilver, and chemicals are entirely dispensed
with. The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs'. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the
ordinary copper-plates and blanket tables. The tailings
on leaving the stamper boxes pass over the plates which,
from their construction, catch the fine gold and pyrites,
allowing the sand and water to flow away. When the
plates are fully charged with the concentrates they are
then tilted backwards by means of a lever, throwing the
contents into a trough underneath. In practical use
much better results have been obtained by these plates
than by the ordinary copper ones, the yield of gold being
increased by fully 10 per cent. Excellent results have
been achieved in the treatment of refractory ores, where
the gold is associated with some foreign mineral, which
has proved deleterious to the mercury and causing the
loss of the gold. The plates are strong, simple in con-
struction, and easily handled, and can be obtained from

Messrs. Nicholson and Co., whose name is
ever-popular with the musical section, have, in
their portion of the splendid Equitable Buildings,
the handsomest music-shop in Australia. Spacious
as are the ground-floor, upper-floor, and basement,
they are none too ample for the firm’s require-
ments for displaying the Ronisch, the Bechstein,
the Wilmer, the Mignon, the Schiedmayer and
Soehne, the Nanon, the Challen, and other pianos •
or for comfortably accommodating the unequalled
stock of music ; or for giving the workmen in the
basement every facility for repairing. The shop-
front is a study in this form of decorative art, and
is being copied by firms of less importance. Com-
pared with other business-establishments, Messrs.
.Nicholson and Co.’s new premises are one of the
sights of Sydney. Note should be made of the
six teaching-rooms, which are not the least attrac-
tive of many praiseworthy features.*

The Goold Bicycle Co., Ltd., has estab-
lished a branch at 64 King-street, Sydney, where
The Brantford, the flying red bird, is displayed to
its own and public advantage. The machine is
famous for its light and practically unbreakable
nickel steel tubing, and for its cycloidal sprocket,
which minimises the friction and makes the
driving mechanism perfect. Mr. C. J. Knowles
is managing the Australian business, and is
making a name for himself in Australian cycling
history.*
The firm of Washington H. Soul and Co.
has been established in Sydney just on a quarter
century, and its ever-increasing popularity has
been recently evidenced by the establishing of a
well-appointed branch at Church-street, Parra-
matta. The Sydney house has always borne the
reputation of being not only the largest and most
complete house of its kind in N.S. W., but also of
being the most profitable establishment for pur-
chasers of every grade. The Parramatta branch
will thoroughly maintain the firm’s character.
Among a multitude of requisites attention is par-
ticularly drawn to Soul’s Australian Relish and
Soyer’s Baking Powder, household commodities
which have won constant favor by their quality
and moderate price.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Dungan having won the G.N. Steeplechase,
after all, a good many people would like to know why
that- game little bit of stuff performed so feebty in the
Hurdle Race a week earlier. Since there is no possible
doubt whatever as to the honest dealing of everybody
connected with Dungan, the animal’s inconsistent run-
ning may be accounted for by a change of training tactics
between the two struggles. Perhaps he left the Hurdle
Race “ on the track,” and picked up the Steeple through
being eased in his work when he was shown to have been
somewhat overstrung. This is very likely. Anyway, the
touts were for once justified in sticking to their fancy.
Dungan, said the watchers, ought to have shaken the first
event on his “ form ” in private, and when he got beaten
they protested, with much superfluous bad language,
that he was the victim of circumstances unknown. The
public, taught by experience to believe what they see
rather than the things they hear, were right off Dungan
last Saturday, and the cheering over his victory came
mainly from early backers who had mourned their short-
priced investments as lost. Those who defer their punting
until the eleventh hour went around grumbling, under
umbrellas, at the common sense which had persuaded
them to let a good horse, owned by straight sportsmen,
go out unbacked at the tempting odds of 10 to 1.
Dondi and Timoni, as suggested by The Bul-
letin, went near to winning the Steeplechase, but old
Mikado 11. never looked dangerous. Either the veteran
has lost his pace entirely—for he fenced admirably—or
else the mud interfered with him more than it troubled
some of the others. Mikado’s friends were very confident
at the finish, and the old ’un carried a larger number of
individual sovereigns than the first favorite, Joi. Folks
in general regarded Joi as a sham idol, rushed into posi-
tion by a few big bets and a number of newspaper-writers.
Leap Year, the outsider likely to win if he
managed the first tw r o fences and got his “cronk” hind
leg into proper working order, came to grief at the second
jump. Probably Leap Year was the lamest horse that
ev r went out for a G.N. Steeple. Before they had gone
2UO yards the lightweight was running awkwardly in the
rear, but as this was merely a repetition of his display in
the Hurdle Race, where he eventually worked his waj 7 to
the front at 2.) miles, there was no telling how he might
finish—until he came an awful cropper. The Millers’ pair
—Larnook and Whernside—were the only other ones to
topple, and their bad luck, coming after The Artist’s
downfall pn the previous Saturday, gave the stable an
awful shock. Three out of four falls in the double event
happened to Mr. Sep. Miller’s colors, usually so safe.
The Sydney horse, All Right, seemed to be
winning the Open Hurdle Race from Ouyer last Saturday
up to the point where Freestep made his run. Then All
Right dropped back at a surprising rate, although Ouyen
was able to struggle on into third place, Freestep being
“ pipped ” by Billy Boy.
In a sporting sense, the damp, unpleasant
Steeplechase Day at Flemington was a big success. Very
few accidents, and three exceptionally close finishes.
Despite his third-rate Flemington effort over
small sticks, Dungan will probably be first choice for
Caulfield G.N. hurdles with only 11.10. He is not in the
four-mile steeple, which distance would suit Mikado 11.
had he not met with an accident. Songster, fourth at
Flemington, may sing a different ditty this time, and
some interest in the proceedings may be taken by Banjo
from S.A.
* * * *
Natty Dungan ? s narrow National victory recalls
the neck win of Sussex from the Panic mare Twilight the
year the steeple was inaugurated. Sussex was the first of
the Grandmasters to land a big race. Panic’s famed stock
won every item on the programme at that meeting bar
the National.
* * * *
Dungan’s heavy ballast was the third highest
borne to victory in 15 years, and Timoni was the fourth
light-weight runner-up. The grey ran a wretched third
in the two-mile Steeple at the V.R.C, June meet, when
Buzzi scored second in the Hurdles. An additional mile
suited both in the Nationals, and the light-weight double
nearly came off.
* * * *
The Miller stable contested every race on
National day. Its two reps, fell in the big event; Miss
Gladstone, winner of a double at Williamstown a fort night
previously, ran second twice ; favorite Ouyen was third in
the Hurdles, and weight anchored Dart in the Open
Steeple.
• * * *
There were some fine opportunities offered for
guessing at Warwick Farm on Saturday, when favorites
succumbed with monotonous regularity. The Captain,
Cook up, having been shunted by the public after recent
failures, duly won on a 6 to 1 chance. The first three
couldn’t get a place at Rosehill the week previous. Royal
Rose, not considered good enough for any of the spring-
handicaps, gave weight and a hot battle to the winner.
The public followed Chipping-Norton, but Patrona isn’t a
patch on her Cup-winning brother.
♦ * * *
Vic. owners are ill-represented in A.J.C.
Epsom and Metropolitan, but Wallace will be a fine horse
to head the list with in the mile-and-a-half. Newhaven’s
name shines in the Epsom, as well as that of many other
good animals, who are either in the stable or the paddock.
Coil is an absentee.
• * * *
The win of a horse under 14st. at Coreena,
Central Q., has occasioned an argument as to whether it
isn’t downright cruelty to race the breath out of grass-
fed animals under such crushing welter-weights.
• * * *
The good old custom of backing one horse for
both Cups still prevails, and big bets are chronicled
weekly. Though a few have gone very near it no animal
has ever won the double, and the amounts netted by the
ring during the last 16 years over this particular form of
betting would run into vast figures.
* * ' » *
Bookmaking in Melb. has actually come down
to penny bets.
* * He • He
The cockleshell meeting between the lion and
the mouse summarily cut short the winning career of the
Chelsea pigmy. The favor in which Harding was held
may have been due to English patriotism, but The Bulle-
tin shrewdly suspects that his backers were deceived by
the corpulent aspect of giant Stanbury, and that big Jim
paddled out for the occasion to keep himself warm. The
time to Hammersmith, it is' cabled, was the fastest on
record ; after that the affair was a procession.
* * » *
The astounding statement that three of the
M.L. representative crew in the Parramatta River inter-
provincial fours contest were drugged is printed by a
Marlborough (M.L.)paper. The three in question belong to
that district. A nice yarn for a beaten crew to go home
-with ! Of course, the accusation is absurd. A “doctored ”
.crew could never have rowed a long, severe race and
.exhibited such finishing dash as did the Maorilanders.
Straight steering and the avoidance of a foul might have
seen Vic. in second place instead of first. This serious
drugging allegation, on top of the row at the time, will
Lkely knock the interprovincial fours on the head. What
have the men themselves to say ?
* * * *
Melb. : “ The refusal of C. B. Fisher’s certifi-
cate is a surprise, not because he particularly deserved to
get through, but because so many other lame ducks have
their loose legs thus legally bandaged. However, this
fine old gentleman can morally afford co be out of the
category of whitewashed boomsters.
' * * * *
The inability of the law to assume anything
that is perfectly obvious was again exemplified last week
when a “ this and the next” merchant was charged at
a Sydney Court with laying “The Captain and Cooee.”
When things looked glum, attorney Crick hotly contended
there was no proof that the names mentioned repre-
sented racehorses, and got his surprised client discharged,
the bench not being sure about the matter. There seem
to be about 111 ways of evading the Gambling Act, any
one of which will serve if advocated by a clever lawyer
who owns spider-legged swift-’uns himself, and doesn’t
know whether they are “ racers ” or not when discussing
them in their legal aspect.
* * * »
A Melbourne sporting tipster—of the kind
whose predictions are quoted by the tipping fraternity on
the mutual admiration principle—took Dart to be' the
best of Mr. S. Miller’s lot for the G.N. Steeplechase. The
owner’s friends had known, for weeks before the race,
that Larnook and Whernside would represent the stable,
all going well. Dart and Redleap were scratched.
* * * *
According to the unreliable racing historian,
fully 50,000 hats were flung into the air when Persimmon
won the Derby, and the Prince of Wales walked down the
course to lead the hero in. And the grovel waxed more
intense a few' minutes later—“ The cheers w'ere almost
hysterical now, men’s excitement having turned their
heads. A spectacle which has known no parallel on any
racecourse in the world continued. The Prince of Wale's
shook Marsh’s hand a dozen times, and w hen Watts dis-
mounted he shook the jockey’s. Porter, his old trainer,
was similarly dealt with, and so were many hundreds of
others. The cheering had known no break from first to
Last, but when the 'all right’ was called, there was
scarcely a man on the course with a hat on his head.”
Albert Edward, shining in the reflected glory of his ’orse,
was undoubtedly a popular event.
* ‘ * * *
The story goes (The Bulletin prints it in
the public interest, in order that it may be contra-
dicted if untrue) that Brickey Colley, the famous
Australian jockey, w'ho died the other day, really
succumbed to exposure. Returning to the hotel
where he was staying, he was refused admission
to his bedroom on the ground of being drunk, and
was bundled off into the stable, where he died of cold
frozen to death on the n’ :hf of the record frost. Letters
sent to the paper- si: •: »sl ng enquiry into the circum-
stances of the poor jockey’s death did not appear.
* * * *
The V.R.C, judge, an excellent authority on
latest “ form,” fancied Leap Year for the Steeple.
Surcingle : “ GoycTer, now of Katoomba, turned
up at Flemington for this Grand National meet, after an
absence of 15 years—since 1881, when his mighty Sussex won
the big prize. No man was ever more respected on the
Australian turf, though he combined the owner, victualler,
and bookmaker.”
* * * *
The opening exhibitions of the N.S.W. country
football teams in the metropolis left much to be desired.
Bathurst showed the best form, beating Newcastle by 9 to
3. They made a speciality of dribbling rushes, and the
Northerners were quite bewildered by so much feetball.
A dead-heat was registered in the Ar nidale-Orange match,
each scoring 8. Both played remissly, and the kicking
wasn’t even up to junior form.
* * » *
In a football-match at Bendigo (Vic.), one player
was sent to dreamland with a whang under the ear. There
was soon an all-round row, and the police were about to
read the Riot Act when they S’lotted the original delin-
quent and marched him off. When the crowd was swept
hack, players discovered that someone had sneaked the
ball. It was eventually found under the buttoned over-
coat of a juvenile who looked rather stout. .
* * * *
Saturday’s Sydney Rugby sensation was the cut-
down of the premiers in Agri. trophies semi-final. Went-
worths rubbed out an old score, and the gall of defeat
rankled in the Randwick breast when the crowd showed
symptoms of insanity over the result. Warbrick scored
the only try in the first minute ; after that, much energy
wasted. The winners’ eagerness to inflict a bigger drub-
bing caused the award of numerous frees against them.
* * * *
Wallaroo had a holiday game with the ’Varsity,
excelling by 30 to 10. The marsupials swept all before
them with a forceful sweep, and, had the match been
against Randwick, they would still have won. Roberts,
playing with much vim, scored 4 of the 8 tries, and Col-
quhoun, for the losers, played his best game this season.
Geelong’s unexpected defeat of Essendon re-
stores a mild amount of Melh. senior-football interest.
Collingwood and Essendon now tie for first place, with S.
Melb. 12 points behind, and other clubs nowhere. Geelong,
almost last on the list, deserved its win, though neither
team played very good football.
» * * *
English fair play, writes a travelling Australian
to The Bulletin by last mail, is dead and decomposed.
The first successes of the Australian cricketers were
sneered at and discounted at the expense of the opposing
teams. Now a pale-blue funk has set in, accompanied bv
its inseparable companion, blind partialism. At Oxford
especially was the latter most noticeable, dragging the
following comment from a London daily “ All the
Oxford partisans shout as if they had never before seen a
wicket fall, and it may be said at once that never in an
artisan district have I met with so one-sided, unsports-
manlike, and prejudiced a crowd as this assemblage of
ladies and gentlemen. Never a cheer for the best play of
their opponents, loud and repeated plaudits when a score
comes from a snick or miss-hit by an Oxonian. When
Hartley beats and bowls Trott the ladies are particularly
jubilant.”
P. S. Waddy, an Australian, played for the
’Varsity against his countrymen, totalling the third
highest on his side. In the preceding match, against
Surrey, he also batted well, and secured most of the
wickets, contributing materially to the first defeat of the
premier county.
The regulation “ Wives of players shall not ac-
company the team when playing in the provinces,” is
already a dead letter, judging by the appearance of the
various missuses on the Oxford ground. One spouse was
not much inspirited thereby, and may be linked with
M’Kibbin—l for 122—as a comparative failure, up to date.
The prince of Australian captains, W. L. Mur-
doch, is batting in good form for Sussex this season,
scoring over the century against Gloucester. Mrs. W. L.
has a hobby for collecting bats off which big scores h ive
been made. Sir Timothy O’Brien, who had contributed
to the collection, tried hard for a new bit of willow by
telling W. L. he was starting a museum of unused bats,
and he would accept one from him !
Bexhill, where the Australians meet a team of
Earl De La Warr’s, is a new seaside resort, now being
pushed and boomed by that young man for all-and
more—that it is worth, says The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent. He sold the remainder of his estates to
spend the money in developing this place, which is within
view of Eastbourne. A smart, affable, good-looking chap
chairman of the Dunlop Tyre Co., married to Gov!
Brassey’s second daughter.
* » * *
Lilley is the first All-England wicket-keeper to
rank as a useful batsman since the days of Alfred Lvttle-
ton. M‘Gregor and a number of predecessors with the
gloves were little or no good in front of the wicket
Among the bowlers Pougher and Hearne can be counted
on for runs, likewise the waning Peel, whereas Richard-
son and Mold, especially the latter, are much inferior to
Australia’s batting “ tail.” England doesn’t seem to pro-
duce Giffens and Trotts, capable of first-class work both
with ball and bat. Certainly Ulyett was a double-bar-
relled gun, but he didn’t keep up his form as Gif. has
done, and his fielding, after he grew fat, was a mere exhi-
bition of laziness.
Kent, which has been one of the luckiest of
English counties against Australian elevens, looks far too
weak to stand a chance of beating them this season.
Surrey seems likely to win one match—out of two —with
the visitors, Sussex may make a tight game of it, and
perhaps Derbyshire, which is strong in bowling.
• * * *
The Australians have plenty of work to do
between July 16th and 29th. They meet in succession All
England, Derbyshire, the M.C.C. and Surrey.
* * * *
The particular luck of the Australians up to
date has been not so much in winning the toss four times
out of five, as in never losing it against a strong team,
save only the M.C.C. By the law of averages —which,
however, doesn’t apply to such a short series--the Austra-
lians ought to have the worst of the luck through the
latter half of their tour.
* * » *
Re the complaint that the Australians make
their matches go slow for the spectators —Yorkshire
scored an innings of 450 against Notts, and Notts replied
with a total of something over 600 for two innings—an
average of 350 runs per day.
**‘ * *
After personal inspection, the English papers
seem to have dropped describing Giffen as “ the grey-
haired Giffen.” Fact is, George has apparently aged
backwards since his last tour ’Ome. Also, English
papers make no reference to the peculiar appearance of
Lightning Jones mentioned in Australian papers.
* * * *
Joe Darling had a good offer to stay in England
and play for Middlesex, according to an Adelaide friend.
Joe runs a good cricketware business in Adelaide, and
preferred not to accept any offers at present. Jones, on
the other hand, has accepted an English offer of £365 a
year and quarters.
* * * •
Says a London weekly : “Centuries have been
scored against every team save Surrey and the Australians.
This clearly indicates where the real trundling talent
lies.”
» * * *
The team of English Players pitted against the
Australians would probably have been beaten by a second
selection. Say Richardson, Lohmann, Abel, Havward,
Gunn, Shrewsbury, Hulme, Peel, Wainwright, Tunhicliffe,
and little Brown. These would have been a pretty fair
match for the visitors, although it is clear that nothing
short of an all-England eleven can reckon on making a for-
midable score against Australian bowling this trip.
* » * *
At the end of the Australian tour it will be
found that Giffen, as of yore, was the one indispensable
man. The “ old man ” still goes on making many runs,
and taking many wickets.
* * * *
Iredale’s latest scores—94 not out, 22, 114, 9,
40, 106, and 171.
* * * *
In 1862, when Caffyn’s All-England Eleven
visited Australia, a single-wicket- match was played
between Englishman Griffiths (with three men to field for
him) and 11 of the Ovens and Murray district. The
eleven scored only one among them —a no ball ! —and
nine of them were clean bowled. Griffiths then went in
and made 6, being caught by Reid. There were two wides,
the total being 8. Of the 11 who made this mammoth
score of one, only two are now alive, Curtis Reid and
Baldock. The former is squatting in Queensland, and the
latter is the well-known Indian horse-buyer.
* ♦ * *
Charley Bennett, the N.S.W. cyclist, who left
for England last week, is a marvel in one department of
wheel work. Rut Bennett on top of a hill with any men
in Australia and, pedals barred, he will reach the foot
before them. Neither Zimmerman, Martin, Megson, or
Parsons could come near Bennett in coasting, though to
the outsider it would seem impossible that, riding with the
feet off the pedals, the rider could affect the speed of his
mount in any way. But Bennett proves the reverse and
proves it every time.
* * * *
The proposal to prohibit the galloway business
within 40 miles of Sydney would receive just as much
support if the boundary was confined to seaward.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union is inviting English
authorities to send a riding team out in October next to
give an impetus to Australian amateurism. As a special
inducement they are asked to pav their own expenses A
long journey for silver pots. But the Union, in return,
promises to send a team ’Ome some other time. The pro-
ject is laudable ; unattractive amateurism certainly wants
livening up in Australia, but whether foreign wheelmen
will come to the rescue on the terms proposed is uncer-
tain.
* * * *
The horse will soon only be kept as a Zoo
curiosity. A Pennington petroleum motor, 201 b. weight,
fitted to an ordinary carriage, can do the work of two
horses cind its only food is a dose of cheap oil every
morning.
* * * *
A well-known Melb. musician thus defined a
“scorcher.” “Ze scorchaire, he pedal presto; but ze
senseeble man, he pedal adagio.”
* * * •
The Yankee team of racing-cyclists, taken across
the Atlantic with the avowed intention of “sweeping
Europe clean,” have hit nasty snags in Paris. The two
stars of the team have gone down before local men, and
the last big meeting ended in a riot owing to the’Murkans
drawing the color-line by “dealing it out” to a nigger
cyclist in the dressing-rooms. The show ended in a riot,
and the crowd tried to set fire to the wooden track.
French cycling seems to be like Australian pony-racing.
* * * *
An American millionaire-cyclist has caused to
be constructed a silver-plated bicycle, with handle-bars of
gold and ivory studded with jewels, and a saddle of the
finest morocco-leather. It bears the owner’s monogram
in diamonds. Even the pneumatic tyre of this gorgeous
machine is, it is to be presumed, inflated with millionaire.

Who is to succeed Speaker Berry in the
chair? It is well-known that when Wnxon and

room. One present aspirant is also strong on the
genial bottle. He keeps a constant stock at Parlia-

This trait should help his chances. ) There is much
philosophy in “ ’ave a glass along o’ me.

A recent issue of the Planters’ Monthly con-
tains a description of this remarkable nut and its
WS Kola has the property of staying hunger whilst
supporting the struggle for existence. If a supply
of nuts had been included in Mr. Stanley s outfit,
his men would not have suffered so terribly from
want of animal food. „ .
As might be expected, the value of Kola in-
creases as it makes its way into the interior ol
Africa, and some of the tribes farthest removed
from the sea pay for the dry powder an equal
W ln h a of dlsp d at d ch St 'from Mr. George Alexander
qfpvens Her Majesty’s Consul at Bahia, he calls
to the GrlaT POWERS of ENI )U t-
ANCE in lifting heavy loads and transport it g
them to long distances in the tropical clima e
attributed to the free use of Kola Bean. An o d
African, after biting a piece of the magic Bean
lifted 179 English pounds weight on to his head
and carried the same for a distance of four (4) miles.
Still another merit is named, however, which
will interest the Temperance Party. The negroes
who chew Kola Nut have no desire to Jink mm.
and hence save money and retain their strength.

Vic. stipendiary justice. Mrs. Campbell,
the starving wife of a sick, long-out-of-work
laborer, and mother of eight children, one of them
a permanent invalid, stole two bags of wheat for
tucker purposes, and was hauled up at Numurkah
before a paul and an unpaid beak. She was un-
defended, pleaded guilty, and was spoken for
sympathetically by the sub-inspector and local
constable. Then the humorous stipendiary said
he wouid deal with her as lightly as possible”
and fined her £5 and Bs. 6d. costs, or in default a
term in gaol. To fine a poverty-stricken, starving
woman £o and call it a light sentence is only
possible to a Vic. beak.
An inquiry into the working of Melbourne
Telephone Department is throwing light upon the
joys of a Govt, billet. At the outset numerous
witnesses gave evidence that their immediate boss
confined himself, in a great measure, to the in-
spection of wine and women during business-hours.
1 he books kept in the boss’s office were originated
'A a telegraph-messenger, aged 23, who stated
that he could run the clerical work without any
assistance from his superior officer. The messen-
ger. it seemed, had been introduced to sundry bar-
maids m the office by the boss ; also he had visited
pubs at the early hour of 10.30 a.m. in the boss’s
company, and on one occasion a loose-limbed
Hebe had removed his hat with a high kick.
J Ins, however, happened on a Saturday afternoon.
® messenger couldn’t remember any cases of
high-kicking during business-hours. Two line-
repairers deposed that they frequently saw the
boss unsober or “muddled ” in his office, and one
of them had formed part of a friendly deputation
to remonstrate with the magnate on his giddi-
ness. The boss’s chief conversation with the
men was about women : not about the virtues of
women, but rather the sort of conversation which
the Enquiry Board would be likely to hear if they
went where prostitutes were to be found.”
Altogether, the evidence made it clear that that
boss s billet was a little paradise, where he had
lived the happy, innocent life of a “ model man ”
until the serpent came, in the guise of bad associ-
ates, and taught him to go astray. As far as the
departmental work was concerned, the boss’s
'T-ir behaviour doesn’t appear to have made much
difference. He was as useful after the Fall as
before it, although his duty of doing nothing was
performed m a less perfect manner when he took
to liquor and ladies. The mistake he made was
m coming to the office at all. He should have
stayed at home in receipt of his comfortable
salary, leaving the messenger to see that the office
didn’t run away.

Prospectus.
pROSPECTUS OF THE
British Banner Gold Mining
Company, N. L.,
Pitfield District, near Ballarat.
CAPITAL:
£12,000 in 24,000 Shares of 10s. Each.
12,000 Contributing Shares, paid up to 2s. x;o be
handed to the Vendors and the Promoters,
and £4OO Cash, in full payment for the pro-
perty.
£2OO of the above cash to be utilised in payment
of the debts of the old Company.
12,000 Contributing Shares, paid up to 2s. to be
offered to the public at Is. per Share applica-
tion, and Is. allotment; and of the proceeds,
£BOO will be placed to the credit of the Com-
pany free of all flotation expenses.
Provisional Directors:
CHAS. L. CHRISTENSEN, Melbourne.
RUDOLPH HAGER, Ballarat.
MAXWELL BRUCE, Ballarat.
T. W. MASON, Melbourne.
W. S. PHIPPS, Melbourne.
R. A. STANLEY, Melbourne.
Brokers:
G. D. MEUDELL, 54 Queen-street, Melbourne.
ELLISON & EVERED, Collins-street, Mel-
bourne.
Legal Manager (pro tem):
J. P. CAMERON (Meudell and Cameron, Ac-
countants), Broken Hill Chambers, 31 Queen-
street, Melbourne.
THIS COMPANY is being formed for the
purpose of working and developing at a
greater depth the well-known lease of the old
British Banner Company in the Pitfield district,
held from the Crown. The lease consists of 200
acres, and is free from payment of any royalty.
The following is a brief rdsurnd of the past
history of the mine, supplied to Mr. John P.
Roberts, Ballarat, by a thoroughly experienced
and practical man, who worked in the neighbor-
hood for many years :
G. D. Meudell, Esq., 54 Queen-st., Melbourne.
“I worked in the old British Banner, and
in the Stanley, and in nearly all the prin-
cipal mines of the Pittfield District. The
ground north of the British Banner was
extraordinarily rich. The Maiden City and
Boyd’s Freehold paid handsomely; those two
small parties having, I believe, divided over
£20,000 in dividends. Going south, the sinking
became too deep and expensive for small parties,
and the old British Banner was formed to work
the deep ground. This Company sunk their shaft
too far to the east, and away from the gutter, and
bottomed shallow. Then they drove west 500 ft.
and struck the hard basaltic rock in the face of
their drive ; having then sunk a small shaft and
driven further west, they struck the main gutter,
which yielded about to three or four sets of
timber. Being too shallow by about 30ft. to work
the ground profitably, and their capital being ex-
hausted, they were compelled to suspend opera-
tions, and the district became neglected until re-
cently. There are now, however, a number of
Co-operative Companies doing remarkably well on
the same belt of country to the south, and a con-
siderable quantity of gold is being obtained
quietly by them.
“ As regards the present British Banner Com-
pany, this Company bored the ground at consider-
able cost, the last bore was in extremely hard rock
and not bottomed at 120 ft., and the Directors,
being satisfied that it was on deep ground, discon-
tinued boring, and seleoted a site for a shaft about
250 ft. to the north, with a view of avoiding this
very hard rock in the sinking, and of being near
the junction of the Maiden City gutter. This
shaft, which is 9ft. by 3ft. 6in. in the clear, went
through the basalt into the bed-rock at 90ft., and
dipping west, which would indicate that the shaft
is, as expected, near the junction of the two
gutters. As it was then necessary before any
further progress oould be made to erect machinery,
and our party being a small one, we have had to
let matters stand for a time. The area of the
claim is 200 acres of Crown Lands, held under
lease recently granted, and no royalty to pay.
“I have marked on the plan which I enclose,
what is generally believed will be the course of the
gutter, also, approximately, the position of the old
shaft and the new.
“ Yours very truly,
“JOHN P. ROBERTS.”
23rd June, 1896.
Report of Mr. X. M. Smith upon the Property.
Mr. G. D. Meudell, Stock Exchange,
Melbourne.
“Dear Sir, —I visited the lease known as the
British Banner on the 13th inst., and made a care-
ful inspection of the various mines situated near
that property. The British Banner Lease consists
of 200 acres leased from the Crown, consequently
there will be no royalty to pay, as is the case of
most of the mines in the same district.
“ In the early days of the field the ground north
and south of this lease yielded immensely rich
returns, many thousands of ounces of gold being
got on the northern and north-eastern boundary
of the lease, and some £15,000 was paid in
dividends by the Maiden C ty mine, a mine on
the north of the British Banner; while from the
Stanley, on the southern boundary, handsome
returns were also got, and some large nuggets
were found on that company’s ground—one
weighing 1270 z.
“Further south is the New Moon mine. This
mine only recently bottomed on highly-payable
wash, and they are now doing good progressive
work preparatory to blocking out; while further
south are the Pitfield Plateau, Victoria Mint, and
Woody Yalloak mines, besides a number of mines
held by co-operative parties, and at present yield-
ing highly profitable returns to the holders.
“ On the British Banner a shaft was sunk in the
early days, and the bottom reached at a depth of
about 200 ft., and a drive carried on the bottom to
the west a distance of over 600 ft. Here wash was
met with, but, as it was dipping into deeper
ground, a monkey shaft was sunk from the drive,
which bottomed on very rich wash-dirt. From
about three sets lloz. of gold were taken, but,
owing to the heavy expense of working from this
shaft, the Company decided to sink another shaft.
A suitable site was chosen, and the shaft was sunk
and timbered to a depth of about 90 feet., when
the Company’s funds became exhausted and work
was abandoned. This shaft is well-timbered and
divided into three compartments, and would re-
quire to be sunk an additional 130 ft. to strike the
wash. In sinking this shaft some very hard rock
(basaltic) was passed through, but the bottom is
now in nice soft slate country, which I think will
continue down to the wash.
“ In the early days of the field the water was a
source of great trouble, but, owing to the number
Prospectus.
of plants now erected on the south of the pro-
perty, the water-trouble will be easily overcome,
and, owing to the comparatively shallow depth of
the wash, it will not require very expensive ma-
chinery to work the mine, and the shaft could be
sunk and timbered at a cost of about 355. per foot.
_ “ In conclusion, I may state that this property,
situated as it is on one of the best leads of gold
discovered in Victoria, is certain, when properly
opened up, to become an extremely valuable pro-
perty, and I have every confidence in recommend-
ing it as a really good mining-investment.
“I am, Faithfully yours,
(Signed) “T. H. SMITH.”
COPY OF TELEGRAM TO MR. G. D.
MEUDELL FROM MR. T. H. SMITH.
“ Cape Clear, 15th June, 1896.
“ Can strongly recommend Banner. Must sink
additional 120 feet. Country good sinking.”

The secret of fast contract-work. Don t
pay men by the piece; pay them by the day, but
also set rival gangs to work at similar jobs, ancl
offer a tempting prize to the gang that does its job
first. By this means you “ sweat ” all the gangs,
and the prize, that you give the winner is saved
five times over in the difference of cost between
this sort of day-work and ordinary piece-woik.
See it ?
At Childers (Q.), a few Sundays ago, a
missionary baptised a number of kanakas by im-
mersion in a waterhole. The usual hymns were
sung and the stereotyped exhortations given, but
immediately the boys got on dry land again a.
tribal row occurred, and they set at each other s
throats in earnest. They had become militant
Christians.
Judge Hodges (Vic.) has decided that a
barrister is only a common threepenny mortal like
anybody else, and if he doesn’t appear m cour
to'conduct a case in which he is retained he must
hand back the money paid him. I his puts the
gorgeous horse-hair magnate on the same plain
business-footing as the man who is hired to cait
mud and who isn’t paid unless the mud is carted.
There are four things in particular that need to be
done to put the. legal brotherhood on a sane basis.
1) When a barrister is paid to do certain work,
and fails to do it, he should be liable to return his
fee, like any other man. (2) If he undertakes to
do certain work, and his client is injured by his
failure to do it, he should be liable to be sued tor
damages—like any other man. (3) If he does the
work, and his client or his client’s solicitor doesn t
pay, he should be allowed to sue like any other
person. And (4) when the client pays the barris-
ter’s fee to the solicitor, and the solicitor sticks to
it, as he often does, the barrister should have the
same, power as any other man to get the solicitor
gaoled and struck off the rolls for larceny as a
bailee. These four arrangements would do tue
profession a whole lot of good.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE. #
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will hnd it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.
Lecturing at Sydney the other day on
leprosy, Dr. Hodgson advised Australian lepers, if
they could, tu skip to England, where they would
be better treated by the local authorities. lo
which The Bulletin enthusiastically shouts
“ Hear, hear.”

A deputation of Melbourne W.C.T.U.
waited on Minister Peacock the other day with a
new idea rc the frowsy old “ sex-problem,” which
it thinks about all day and dreams about all night.
It wanted a moral convalescent home for girls who
had “made a mistake,” and a law authorising
their detention there until they had grown suffi-
ciently self-reliant to be let out again into the
wicked outer world. Then the W.C.T.U. would
have them all herded together so that it could
come every day and admonish them severely, and
catechise them as to how they “feel,” and preach
to them, and show'them to all its old-woman
friends with much unction, and tell its old-
woman friends their history, and have a real,
good, dirty time without any male relative
around to throw the gloating saints out on their
spectacles as they deserved. It is a truly great
conception. As things are now the W.C.T.U.
harridan has to chase the “ unfortunate ” at her
own home, and can’t talk to her so severely as she
Would like for three hours at a stretch without
some of her relatives interfering, so the proposal
to lock her up for an indefinite period where she
can be talked at with impunity all day is abso-
lutely brilliant. There would doubtless be
a sort of committee-room attached to the
establishment, where the W.C.T.U. and
their friends could sit in comfortable chairs
round the room. Then they would send
for the show “unfortunate” and stand her up in
a humble attitude in the centre of the floor, to be
interrogated and the particular points of her sin
explained to the shocked old lady who hadn’t
heard of it before. Also, there would be a lot of
pleasant excitement in finding out about the girl
“ who had fallen,” and capturing her, so that the
whole street would hear about the case, and bring-
ing her away in the van belonging to the estab-
lishment ; and bringing her back again escorted
by a W.C.T.U. deputation when she was suffi-
ciently reformed, and inspecting her afterwards to
see that she kept reformed. But Minister Peacock
looked at the deputation with a cold expression in
his other eye, and offered no encouragement, and
so far the inspiration has got no “ forrarder.”

difficulty just now is how to get' his money in. l ln
the recovery of debts, especially in circumstances
of difficulty or delicacy, Holds worth & Co., 101
Swanston-street, Melbourne, meet with singular
success.*
Insomnia due to street noises, howling
dogs, crowing cocks, and the like may be averted
by inserting in the ears, when turning in, small
plugs of cotton-wool steeped in vaseline. Wadding
alone will not do, as, when dry, it conducts vibra-
tions, but, vaseline!, it does not.
