Club Humor .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the Moku Club there was a clique of
practical jokers. Carleton himself was not a man
of that sort by choice. But he loved a joke, even
a practical joke, if it was not too asinine, though
he felt it when he thought that it was he, and he
alone, who was responsible for Moggeridge’s pre-
sentation.
Moggeridge was 60, decidedly over-fed in ap-
pearance, and gone at the knees. He had not the
look of a hero, and he had been known to get on a
table out of the way of an inquisitive rat.
One night, however, on his way home, close on
the hour when one day meets the other, he saw the
color of fire in the lower windows of a two-storeyed
house. He scrambled on to the verandah, aroused
the inmates sleeping on the second floor, and had
them out of danger before the fire-brigade reached
the scene.
A few days afterwards there was a ceremony at
the club. When the place was pretty busy
Carleton came along bearing a handsome Russian-
leather case with silver clasps, and mounted with
an elaborately-engraved silver plate. He disposed
his burden on a table in a manner calculated to
attract attention, and half-a-dozen humorists in
league with him pressed up, as if interested, to
see what was going on. When Carleton had
aroused sufficient interest in his proceedings he
rose to make a speech. After a few apologies
about his own shortcomings, and the honor he felt
it to have been selected to take the lead m an
affair of such moment, he proceeded, to the great
astonishment of the man himself, to refer to Mog-
geridge.
Moggeridge in Moku was a man without an
affinity, as one might say. He was a lone man,
and, being a bachelor, he was still more lone than
other men of his type, who have wives and
families at home who have learned better to
understand them. His loneness had grown up
with him, and though in his younger days he had
often wondered why he did not mix more with
men, the problem had ceased to interest him years
and years before. He was neither unsociable nor
unfriendly, but people thought he was both, and
left him alone. They did not know him—he did
not know himself. They, however, made a mis-
take which he did not make, which he could not
make. They thought he had no heart, whereas
he knew he had a great, soulful something within
him that gave him more trouble than people
thought possible to a personage of his unattractive
and droll exterior.
Moggeridge, in a word, was just the sort of man
that the ordinary fool permits himself to think
Nature has sent into the world for his amusement.
Carleton was not that sort of a fool, though he
wandered so far from his lights as to act like one
on this occasion.
He had undertake the thing, however, and he
laid himself out to do it well. When he began to
touch on Moggeridge the club waited anxiously
as men do who scent a deep and subtle joke.
Carleton did not jump into the middle of things
without paving his way. He was too clever to
allow his joke to escape him. So when he touched
upon Moggeridge he did it very carefully at first,
and with reticence, as though he was anxious not
to offend against good taste. Gradually, however,
he seemed to warm to his r subject, and, increasing
step by step in fervor, he ended by pouring out
upon the astonished hero such a wild rush or
eloquence as drove the club half mad with enthu-
siasm. Before he had done, Moggeridge, who was
not used to hearing his virtues sung, was as red as
a turkey-cock, and was shuffling uneasily m his
boots. For “he who publicly praises you, patron-
ises you. ”
When Carleton had finished a second speaker
arose, and after him another, and yet another.
For over an hour they laid themselves out to apply
■to Moggeridge every'flattering term their mother
tongue could offer. As they proceeded the “hear-
hears ” and “bravos” grew louder and louder, and
the undercurrent of laughter became harder and
harder to suppress.
Moggeridge alone saw only on the surface, and
to him the thing was real. Nevertheless he> did
not enjoy it, and every moment he grew redder
and more uneasy. It was with difficulty he got
on his feet when the silver-mounted case was
handed to him, and when “ For he is a jolly good
fellow ” had been sung he made an effort to speak.
Public speaking was not Moggeridge’s forte,
and he did not pretend that it was. Therefore
nobody was surprised when his first sentence had
half-a-dozen beginnings, and no ending. He was
very, very much agitated, and those nearest to
him began to fear trouble. He was still trying to
collect his senses, when a sudden change over-
spread his face and before anybody could stop him
he fell heavily to the ground. Immediately every
face grew solemn. There were a few who left the
club as soon as they saw trouble ahead, but the
men who were mainly responsible stuck to their
posts. ..ix
Carleton had taken the leading part in the tun,
and he did not shirk the position now. His coat
was soon off, and with shirt-sleeves rolled up he
worked as some men will for their dearest friend.
He bathed the fallen man’s hands and feet m hot
mustard and water for fully two hours before there
was any sign of returning consciousness. Then
Moggeridge rallied for a few moments. The first
thing he did was to thank Carleton for his good-
ness and kindness. He said he did not know he
had so many friends until he heard the kind things
they had all said about him. He was sorry to put
them all to such trouble, but he knew he was
suffering his last attack. He had been taken that
way before, but he felt that was the end.
Carleton was not an emotional man, and he
would have liked to say a few words of hope had
there not been a sensation in his throat that choked
the words back. And before that had gone Mog-
geridge had lost consciousness again.
Carleton and a few others stayed with him until
midnight, when the doctor came and told them at
once that Moggeridge was already past his aid.
A.poplexy, brought on by over-excitement, had
killed him. , , . ,
Half-an-hour after, when Carleton left the club,
he picked up the silver-mounted Russian-leather
case and took it with him. And not a soul asked
what he was going to do with it. Every man
knew that Carleton could be depended on to do
the right thing. . .
What he did in this case was to place the thing
in the fire without opening it, and watch until it
was past identification.
* * * *
In tlie morning the maid-of-all-work found
a few pieces of bent and twisted metal in the grate.
She did not know what it was, and she put it
away with the ashes as not being worth inquiring
about. Those bits of thin metal were all that re-
mained of a common tan belt which had reposed
in the handsome leather case that had been pre-
sented with such ceremony to Moggeridge the
night before.
Maoriland.
J. G. HADDOW.
Worth noting by dwellers in malarious
districts that those who take plenty of salt with
their meals escape malaria, and that in intermit-
tent fever sucking rock-salt or table-Balt assists
recovery greatly.
Mysterious disappearance seems to be the
inevitable fate of nearly every hangman, the
latest to fade silently away being the official
woman-strangler of Vic. The man s history is a
remarkable one. He is the son of a wealthy Eng-
lish manufacturer, and received an excellent edu-
cation. In ’BO he reached Melb. under engage-
ment to a big wine-and-spirit firm as head traveller
at a salary of £5OO per year, but after a couple of
years of the work he decided to strike out on his
own,” and bought a suburban hotel. This he kept
with fair success until ’B5, when he sold out and
returned to England. There he received a sum of
.£5OOO from his father for the purpose of starting
in business in Melb., but an 18-months jaunt over
Europe and the States was more to the pro-
spective slaughterer’s liking, and when he arrived
in Melb. he had scarcely a penny to bless himself
with. Half-a dozen temporary warders being re-
quired at Pentridge Prison, he put in an applica-
tion and was appointed, and it was while in that
capacity that he became acquainted with Jones,
the hangman, who suicided rather than hang Mrs.
Knorr There seemed to be some magnetic sym-
pathy between the men from the first, for they
r ‘ pal’d up ” immediately, and off duty were seldom
seen apart. The budding hangman soon got
transferred to the police force and was
generally placed on duty at the corner
of Elizabeth and Collins-streets, possibly the
most aristocratic beat in the whole of Vic.
Many a sassiety damsel who was assisted a
cab-block by the handsome constable on the
point ” would probably be disgusted to learn that
the beauteous bobby is the man who holds
the Australian record for woman-choking. In 88
our policeman left the force “ for more lucrative
employment,” say the records; but this employ-
ment, whatever it was, doesn't seem to have
lasted long, for in a few months he is found
taking his first lesson in hangmanship at Ballarat
Gaol, when he acted as Jones’s assistant at
the turning-off of sharebroker Johnson. He then
migrated to Sydney, where he earned an honest
living doing a ‘‘bit of flogging ” at the various
gaols. But the work was laborious; he was
ambitious and had ideas above perspiring behind
a gory lash. So, when Jones cut his throat two
days before the date of Mrs. Knorr s hanging, his
chum deferred weeping over his old friend until he
had penned and posted an application for the
vacant billet. This letter was successful and,
starting by the next train south, he arrived in
Melb. in good time, and the job which even Jones,
the slayer of 20 men and the criminal assaulter of
little girls, had shrunk from committing, was
taken on by his delicately-nurtured and highly-
educated pal. A little romance forms a pretty
sequel to the story. Within a month after the
demise of the esteemed Jones, the new hangman
led to the altar a blushing bride —the widow of his
predecessor.
“Scotty the Wrinkler” Talks by the
Card on Whisky.
Dear Bulletin,— lt is your duty to let th® pu
know what trash is talked about old spirits•
say the old malt peat-reek was better for you than
the modern. So it was—better than imperfectly-
distilled spirit. The least-bad spirit for anyone is
pure alcohol diluted with pure water, and tins is the
white spirit against which such an outcry is made.
I do not wish to be prolix, but pray for a little
space. When patent stills came into us ®
’55 to ’6O, they abstracted the alcohol and separated
the fusel-oil (called feints in Scotland). Tne spirit
was called foreshot, and was about j 5 oP;
Water and six months m a sherry-cask made
whisky, better whisky than any new malt whisky
made "by the old copper and worm stills. Ine
smoky flavor can be given by a drop of creosote
in a barrel. But rum, brandy, and gin are the
same spirit flavored with, say pineapple, es fmce
of brandv and juniper, the two former being
colored with, perhaps, burnt sugar. I write as _ a
born distiller and professional taster. AU the
spirit from our still went to France to be made
into brandy: we found the local trade too slow.
And there is hardly such a thing as malt whisky.
Indian corn is the matrix. A proportion of malt
must be used so as to convert the starch into grape-
sugar. I think one-eighth is sufficient. Vitriol
is used in mashiwt the maize, and yet there
is no vitriol in the spirit. It is neutralised
by chalk or lime. I can get better brandy made
within ten miles of Naranderathan many draught
or case alleged French brew. But fashions change.
Whisky is now the tack, so that the spirit does
not go to France. Five large distilleries have
combined 'including our one) to make saleable and
good brands of Scotch whisky. I won t tell you
the names because y/norant people will not recog-
nise the truth of what I write. As a collateral
proof of my statements, allow me to quote the
Scotsman, April 11th of this year, re the trade of
British India.
Brandv has again given way in favor of whisky, tne lm
ports being respectively 240,010 gallons and 466,464 gallons.
A considerable proportion of the brands is made in Ger
many, and our country does not appear to bave *
poly of the manufacture of whisky, since in 1-894-5 755 b
gallons were imported from Germany. The character of
such importations may be inferred from the fact that it
was pertinaciously argued last year on behalf
tillers in Austria that it was legal, under the Merchandise
Marks Act, to import liquor labelled Glasgow wh sky
made in Austria.”
Cutlery made in Germany, English saddles made
in Germany, Irish linen madem Germany, English
language printed in Germany, British princes mid
princesses manufactured in Germany, but—-new
me miserum ! Ichabod, Ichabod ! Sic transit et id
genus omne , last and most bitter cup to drinK
Glasgow whisky, made in Austria! Ihe im-
provement of grog by age is due to the vola,-
tilisation of the higher essential oils (fusel) and
a chemical change, which neutralises their m-
jurious effects. The taste can be got m three
seconds as shown above. Grog cannot, like wme,
become too old, unless weakness is a fault. But
new, thoroughly distilled spirit is not more in-
jurious than very old vatted, if such a thing
exists, teste the ancients who hang about the still-
head, the cellar, the watch-house and the cooper s
shop of any large distillery. If the Australian
brandy-makers, who started to sell the rmula-
mulla brand, could obtain the same price as Beu-
nessy or Martel, they could afford to produce the
identical poison.—Yours, exhausted by the vast
importance of my theme, Scotty the Wkinkler.

Half-Hanged!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One—two—three rifle-shots in quick suc-
cession broke sharply on the silence of the sultry
night, and then, as every beach loiterer turned in
the direction of the reports, over the still harbor
waters came the hoarse alarm, “ There’s murder
on board the Marion Rennie.”
The dwellers in Levuka, in the early seventies,
were accustomed to this sort of thing. Cakobau,
by the grace of George Austen Woods, Sydney
Charles Burt, and James Stewart Butters, was
king of Fiji and its dependencies. Cotton was
the name of the other king, and “ blackbirding ”
was one of the native industries which flourished
under the joint sway. The harbor was seldom
without two or three labor vessels, and ructions
among the “ recruits ” were of frequent occur-
rence. The Marion Rennie, a topsail schooner of
about 200 tons, was perhaps the unluekiest craft
engaged in that most humane and civilising
traffic. There was scarcely a plank in her deck but
had been baptised in blood ; hardly a trennail in
the planks which might not have stood for a man’s
life.
This time the alarm was fully justified. Tom
Muir, the skipper of a coasting cutter, was the
victim ; Antonio Franks, a yellow-skinned half-
bred Cuban, the ship's cook, was the murderer.
The ship had only returned from her cruise that
morning, and her recruits were still on board.
Muir, coming off to see some of his old mates in
the fo’c’s'le, had appropriated the dusky damsel
promoted to the brevet rank of cook’s mate.
Franks protested ; Muir knocked him down ;
then Franks went to his galley, got a knife, and,
returning, drove it home to Muir’s heart. The
murderer was at once seized by the sailors and
made fast; then, as the boats were away, the
captain had fired three shots from his rifle to bring
off the police.
* * * *
In due course, Franks was tried and sen-
tenced to be hanged. The date of the execution was
fixed, and a warrant issued directing the sheriff to
carry it into effect. The preparations for the event
aroused considerable public interest; for it was
the first time the Government had exercised the
self-delegated right of swinging a man into eter-
nity. The gallo.vs, that typical emblem of ad-
vanced civilisation, was duly erected, but the
greatest difficulty was found in securing a hang-
man. At last a degenerate beach-comber was
discovered willing to earn a fiver easily; and,
having been filled up to the brim with chain-light-
ning the night before, he was “jugged” as a
drunk-and-incapable so that there might be no
doubt of his being on hand in the morning.
When the morning came the criminal’s irons
were struck off, and he, with the gaoler, parson,
hangman, and other officials, awaited the coming
of the sheriff to demand the body. Time passed,
and brought no sheriff. His two deputies, duly in
attendance, stared blankly at each other; and
even the condemned man appeared to resent this
discourteous want of punctuality in attending the
demise at which he was to figure as the corpse.
The hour appointed struck. The next half-hour
passed in suppressed agitation, and at last it was
announced that, in consequence of the unavoid-
able absence of an indispensable character, there
would be no performance that morning; where-
upon the irons were replaced and the wondering
crowd dispersed.
Dire was the dismay in the official halls at
this fiasco. Premier Woods was white with wrath
unspeakable. Chief-Secretary Thurston breathed
threatenings and slaughter against the negligent
official who had thus heaped ridicule upon the
Administration. Attorney-General Burt would
have choked with choler but for the necessity of
bunting U p precedents to afford guidance in the
emergency ; and Chief-J ustice For wood made the
lights burn blue with sulphurous exhalations
from the curses he heaped on the head of that
unlucky sheriff. Moreover, he swore in 17 dif-
ferent languages simultaneously that the criminal
should still hang as high as Hainan, an to t la
intent he issued another warrant directed to the
tw P deputy-sheriffs, commanding them to carry
ou * the execution the following morning,
_ Xt ' vas on F f nks ’> but had to As-
double, however, at once cropped up with the
amateur hangman, who was by this time sober
and sorry for himself, and who not only demanded
lus fiver on the plea that he had been on hand
r f d >' t( J perform ns part of the contract, but
absolutely and emphatically declined to renew the
engagement Prayers, tears, threats, entreaties
P rc l v^ d equally unavailing to alter his resolution,
f nd 1 would have been serious if, at the
last moment, a mate of Muir s had not offered to
discharge the duty out of dear love to his dead
Ap lld ’ and t 0 square on Franks,
u,n S? w e re thus adjusted for the second essay,
and again the gruesome time came round when
lro . n .® were being filed off the prisoner in the
ce ’ while outside the obliging friend black-robed
and mas ked like a familiar of the Holy lnquisi-
tlol>> soa P ed and r ° ve a rope which he then went
and made fast to the gallows-tree.
* * * *
It was a dull, dreary morning, and the
skies were drizzling as though weeping over the
work in hand. The smith was either clumsy or
slow in removing the leg-irons, and the rope was
soaked and swollen before the criminal was ready,
Then he walked firmly on to the scaffold, said a
few words to the crowd, stood carefully on the
trap while the rope and cap were adjusted, and, as
the black domino pulled the lever, fell through,
hanging limp and motionless, a good ten feet,
As soon as it was all over the crowd below scat-
tered, and the deputy-sheriffs, having returned to
the adjacent quarters, were in the very act of
surrounding a good four-fingers of rum, when in
burst the sergeant of police with the horrified ex-
clamation : “My God, gentlemen, that man’s
alive ! ”
“ What ! ”
“ He's alive and singing out-- 1 For God’s sake,
cut me down or shoot me ! ’ What shall I do ? ’’
A quick rush brought the amateur officials back
under the drop. It was a true bill. The swollen
rope had not slipped through the knot. The man
was gasping horribly, and begging in a gurgle to
be released or put out of his misery,
To shoot him according to his wish was out of
the question. The hangman, who, according to
professional precedent, should have humanely
slithered down the rope and danced upon his
shoulders, was absent; so there was nothing for it
but to cut him down. Cut down he accordingly
was ; removed to the cell, fully recovered, and
then left to enjoy his own reflections while the
officials reported the disheartening failure of this
second attempt to give him his quietus as by law
directed.
* * . , * , ™ . ,
If the first failure had caused. an official
commotion, the second created a positive volcanic
eruption of ministerial and judicial ire. Every
one connected with the affair was roundly rated,
bullied, and denounced in good set terms; and the
indignation of the offended magistrates was in-
creased by the difficulty of finding an answer to
that awkward question What was to be done
with Franks? To hang him a third time was im-
possible, Public sympathy was aroused ; and it
would have caused a revolution. He could neither
be legally kept in gaol nor legally discharged, for
in the eye of the law he was dead, though in
proper person most persistently and most incon-
veniently alive. There was no warrant for his
detention, and no authority for his release ; and
official wisdom stood nonplussed at its own impo-
tence to deal with the dilemma.
In this difficulty it did the best thing possible :
it did nothing. Eor three years Eranks enjoyed an
unauthorised existence as a prisoner of the Crown
illegally in custody, and then one dark night he
was quietly shipped off to Maoriland. Strange to
say, during those three years he presented no sign
of the terrible experience he had undergone ; but,
two years after, a man made his way to New
Caledonia whose neck-muscles were contracted so
strangely as to distort the whole of his face and
turn his head over his shoulders. That man was
the half-hanged Antonio Franks.
* * * *
But what kept the sheriff from the execu-
tion as first arranged ? This can best be explained
in his own words, which he —now an Aus-
tralian stipendiary—can confirm. Some short
time after the event he was relieved of his office,
and on meeting Chief-Secretary Tlmrston--now
Sir John Bankes Thurston, Governor of Fiji and
High Commissioner for the South Pacific—he
demanded the reason.
After some little fencing the ministerial mag-
nate replied : “ It should scarcely be necessary for
you to ask. You should be able to understand
that, after your neglect of duty in the Franks case,
the Cabinet cannot continue to repose confidence
in you.”
“What!” was the indignant response; “you
dare tell me this, when you know from the certifi-
cate of her medical attendant, one of your Cabinet
colleagues, my wife was then so near to death’s
door that I would not have quitted her bedside
even to have enjoyed the pleasure of hanging you,
sir ! ”
T. H. PRIOHARD.
Some Chinese gardeners residing near
Laura, a far-north country village, pathetically
appeal to the Brisbane authorities :
Sir, —We, the undersigned Chinamen gardeners living
on the Laura River, beg respectfully to bring the following
grievance under your notice with the hope you will give
us redress.
There is a large Native Camp close to our gardens, and
our potatoes, corn, fruit, vegetables, &c., are constantly
being stolen from us by the blacks in this camp, who
com 3 in such numbers we qannot protect ourselves unless
by firing on them, and this we are not allowed to do.
There is only one Constable at the Laura township, a mile
and a half from us, and he is powerless to assist us.
We request you will order this camp to be broken up
and the occupants compelled to remove to some place
sufficiently distant to prevent them committing further
robberies.
Your most obt. servt,,
Dang Young, Ah Chow, Chang Sing, Lee Seek,
Wong Dang, Chun Bow, Lee Gam Bow,
Yoh Ching.
Thus we have, probably for the first time in pie-
bald Australian history, the wail of the yellow
man against a perpetual close season for niggers.
Questionings.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Life, Death and Spirit—with these problems three
I wrestled long, but failed the truth to see,
Then said I: “To the sages will I go,
And with their light they will enlighten me.”
I asked a» aged Rabb,, wise and keen. . .
“ Life is a shadow, staying not, I ween—
The shadow of a bird that flies away,
And bird and shadow nevermore are seen.”
I sought the Christian of unbounded faith. . .
“ Twin mysteries,” he said, “ are Life and Death.
Spirit alone is real, and survives
All shocks of chance it th’ Eternal’s breath.”
Next to the cold-eyed Scientist I sped ;
“ Pray, tell me what are Life and Death,” I said
“ What is man’s spirit ? Whither does it go
When man himself is numbered with the dead? ”
“ What is our Life A flickering candle-flame
Whereof the wick is our material frame ;
Our food the tallow. Whether snuffed out noon,
Or guttering slow, all candles stink the same !
“ And what is Death ? ’Tis terror to the knave,
Hope to the wretched, freedom to the slave—
A sinking on the breast of Mother Earth
Who gives to all a cradle, crust and grave.
“ What is man’s Spirit ? Whither does it fly
When pulse-beats cease and cold films dull the eye ?
Where is the candle’s flame of yester-night ?
Question the ashes ; wait for their reply ! ”
Blind leaders all! If God or brute I be,
I spend my breath for nought in asking ye ;
I go to Death, the grisly Janitor,
Who holds of every mystery the key !
Christchurch, M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY
M.L. newspaperdom reiterates with fearful
sameness, there being Heralds nine, Stars seven.
Mails seven, Times without number.
Venice as she is :
On either side of the canal of romance staggers a crow( j
of decayed buildings ; from the roof downwards they are
a mass of squalid ruin, broken balconies cling to the
stained and discolored walls, great scabs of plaster have
fallen from their fronts as if a leprosy had eaten into
them ; for a foot above the water the walls are black with
slime, the broken windows are stuffed with rags or paper,
the shattered steps lead up to doors that swing by one
hinge ; the steps themselves are slippery with a greasy
scum ; to the edge of the lower stair there is a fringe of
foul green weed —it swings slowly in the crawling water ;
the iron grilles, once so beautiful, are eaten by bitter salt
rust; the shutters hang at all angles, flapping and creak-
ing in the wind ; in the crazy balconies there is a lumber
of broken flower-pots with dead flowers in them ; and
through all these reeking alleys the greenish grey water
slowly pulses and oozes, covered with straw, egg-shells,
cabbage-stalks, and nameless refuse. Over all this brood
a hundred filthy and obscene smells, each canal contri-
buting a particularly putrid stench of its own. No
longer does Venice sit in state : hour by hour and stone
by stone she is sinking into her dishonored grave.

IRISH MOSS.
“ My Dear Mr. BONNINGTON.
[copy.]
“ Theatre Royal, Sydney, April 20, 1896.
“ Allow me, on behalf of Mrs. Holt, to thank you very sincerely for your
kind thought in sending her, on Saturday, a bottle of your famed Irish Moss. I may say that
it is an old and valued friend of ours, and I am sure our esteem of its virtues will be
strengthened by the acquaintance with it which we now renew, through your good agency.
“ With all sincere wishes for your success and well-being,
“ I remain, yours cordially, BLAND HOLT.”
